Understanding the General-Time Proposition (al-Muntashira)
Further elaborates on the definition, composition, and naming of al-muntashira

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the general-time proposition and distinguish it from the time-specific proposition.

Core Idea
The general-time proposition distributes necessity over an unspecified time.

After discussing the time-specific proposition, the author now turns to the general-time proposition, called al-muntashira. This proposition asserts necessity at an unspecified time, and its composition is crucial for understanding modal logic. In this lesson, we will unpack its definition, clarify the meaning of 'unspecified,' and see how it relates to other modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and its definition
Locate the term being defined (al-muntashira) and the definition: it is a proposition that judges necessity of the predicate for the subject at an unspecified time, not perpetually in essence.
2
Clarify the meaning of 'unspecified time'
The passage emphasizes that 'unspecified' does not mean that lack of specification is a condition; rather, the time is left unrestricted and not tied to a specific moment.
3
Analyze the composition for affirmative and negative forms
For the affirmative form, it consists of an affirmative general-time absolute plus a negative absolute (the concept of non-perpetuity). For the negative form, it consists of a negative general-time absolute plus an affirmative absolute.
4
Compare the general-time with the time-specific proposition
The general-time is more general because if a time-specific proposition is true, then the general-time proposition is true, but not conversely. This relationship mirrors that of other modalities.
5
Understand the status of its simple components
Note that the simple components (time-specific absolute and general-time absolute) are not counted among the simple propositions because they are parts of compound modal propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : المنتشرة هي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع لا دائماً بحسب الذات، وليس المراد بعدم التعيين أن يؤخذ عدم التعيين قيداً فيها بل أن لا تقيّد بالتعيين وترسل مطلقاً، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كلّ إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لا دائماً كان تركيبها من موجبة منتشرة مطلقة - وهي قولنا : بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ماه وسالبة مطلقة عامة - أي قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالفعل الذي هو مفهوم اللادوام و إن كانت سالبة - كقولنا : «بالضرورة لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لادائماً» - فتركيبها من سالبة منتشرة مطلقة. وهي الجزء الأول، وموجبة مطلقة عامة، وهي مفهوم اللادوام. وهي أعم من الوقتيّة، لأنّه إذا صدقت الضرورة في وقت معين لا دائماً صدقت الضرورة في وقت ما لادائماً، بدون العكس. ونسبتها مع القضايا الباقية على قياس نسبة الوقتية من غير فرق. واعلم أن الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة - اللتين هما جزءا الوقتية والمنتشرة - قضيّتان بسيطتان غير معدودتين في البسائط، حكم في إحداهما بالضرورة في وقت معين وفي الأخرى بالضرورة في وقت ما فالأولى سميت «وقتيّة» لاعتبار تعيين الوقت فيها و «مطلقة» لعدم تقييدها باللادوم أو اللاضرورة، والأخرى منتشرة» لأنه لما لم يتعين وقت الحكم فيها احتمل الحكم فيها لكل وقت، فيكون منتشراً في الأوقات،
The statement: The general-time is that in which judgment is made concerning the necessity of the predicate for the subject or its negation at an unspecified time from the times of the existence of the subject, not perpetually according to essence. And what is meant by non-specification is not that non-specification is taken as a condition in it, but rather that it is not restricted by specification and is left absolute. So if it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time, not perpetually,' its composition is from an affirmative general-time absolute - which is our saying: 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time' - and a negative general absolute, i.e., our saying: 'No human is a breather actually,' which is the concept of non-perpetuity. And if it is negative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, no human is a breather at some time, not perpetually,' its composition is from a negative general-time absolute, which is the first part, and an affirmative general absolute, which is the concept of non-perpetuity. And it is more general than the time-specific, because if necessity holds at a specific time not perpetually, then necessity holds at some time not perpetually, without the converse. And its relation with the remaining propositions is analogous to the relation of the time-specific without difference. Know that the time-specific absolute and the general-time absolute - which are the parts of the time-specific and the general-time - are two simple propositions not counted among the simples. In one of them, the judgment is of necessity at a specific time; in the other, of necessity at some time. So the first is called 'time-specific' due to considering the specification of time, and 'absolute' due to not being restricted by non-perpetuity or non-necessity; and the other is called 'general-time' because since the time of judgment is not specified, the judgment is possible for every time, thus it is distributed over times.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed explanation of the general-time proposition (muntashira). It begins by restating the definition: a proposition that judges the necessity of the predicate for the subject (or its negation) at an unspecified time, not perpetually, in terms of essence. It clarifies that 'unspecified' does not mean that lack of specification is a condition, but rather that the time is left unrestricted. Then it repeats the composition for affirmative and negative forms. The passage then compares the general-time to the time-specific (waqtiyya), stating that the general-time is more general because if necessity at a specific time (without perpetuity) is true, then necessity at some unspecified time (without perpetuity) is also true, but not conversely. The relationship with other modalities is analogous to that of the time-specific. Finally, it discusses the simple components: the time-specific absolute and the general-time absolute. These are simple propositions that are not themselves counted among the simple modes (because they are parts of compound propositions). The first is called 'time-specific' because it considers a specific time, and 'absolute' because it is not qualified by non-perpetuity or non-necessity. The second is called 'general-time' because the judgment is distributed over all times due to the lack of specification.

Key Terms
المنتشرة
الوقتية المطلقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between the general-time proposition (al-muntashira) and the time-specific proposition (al-waqtiyya)?
A) They are equivalent.
B) The general-time is more general.
C) The time-specific is more general.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
The general-time proposition asserts necessity at an unspecified time and is more general than the time-specific proposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct an affirmative general-time proposition (e.g., 'Every animal is moving at some time') and identify its two components: the absolute general-time part and the negative absolute part (concept of non-perpetuity).
?
Compare your proposition with a time-specific proposition (e.g., 'Necessarily, every human is a breather in the morning') and explain why the general-time is more general.

The Diffuse Proposition: Definition and Composition
Defines the diffuse proposition and its composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a diffuse modal proposition and distinguish it from a temporal proposition.

Core Idea
The diffuse proposition is a composition of a diffuse absolute and a universal absolute.

In modal logic, propositions are classified not only by quantity and quality but also by the mode of necessity. The diffuse (muntashira) proposition is a key type where necessity is judged at an unspecified time. Understanding its composition and how it differs from the temporal proposition is essential for analyzing arguments in kalam and usul al-fiqh that involve temporal qualifications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of the diffuse proposition
The diffuse proposition judges necessity of the predicate for the subject at an unspecified time of the subject's existence, not perpetually. Note that 'unspecified' means the proposition is not bound to a specific time, not that non-specification is a condition.
Contrast with the temporal proposition, which specifies a time.
2
Break down the composition of an affirmative diffuse
An affirmative diffuse (e.g., 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time, not perpetually') consists of two parts: an affirmative diffuse absolute ('Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time') and a universal absolute negative ('No human is a breather actually', i.e., non-perpetuity).
The universal absolute negative expresses the 'not perpetually' condition.
3
Break down the composition of a negative diffuse
A negative diffuse (e.g., 'Necessarily, no human is a breather at some time, not perpetually') consists of a negative diffuse absolute and a universal absolute affirmative.
The universal absolute affirmative expresses the non-perpetuity condition in the negative case.
4
Compare diffuse and temporal propositions
The diffuse is more general than the temporal: if necessity holds at a specific time, it also holds at some time, but not vice versa. The relation to other propositions parallels that of the temporal.
This difference in generality is crucial for logical conversions.
5
Distinguish between 'temporal absolute' and 'absolute temporal'
The 'temporal absolute' (waqtiyya mutlaqa) judges necessity at a specific time, while the 'absolute temporal' (mutlaqa waqtiyya) judges actuality at a specific time. Similarly for 'diffuse absolute' vs 'absolute diffuse'. The difference is between necessity and actuality.
The author notes this distinction is clear but easily confused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القول : المنتشرة هي التي حكم فيها بضرورة ثبوت المحمول للموضوع أو سلبه عنه في وقت غير معين من أوقات وجود الموضوع لا دائماً بحسب الذات، وليس المراد بعدم التعيين أن يؤخذ عدم التعيين قيداً فيها بل أن لا تقيّد بالتعيين وترسل مطلقاً، فإن كانت موجبة كقولنا بالضرورة كلّ إنسان متنفس في وقت ما لا دائماً كان تركيبها من موجبة منتشرة مطلقة - وهي قولنا : بالضرورة كل إنسان متنفس في وقت ماه وسالبة مطلقة عامة - أي قولنا : «لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس بالفعل الذي هو مفهوم اللادوام و إن كانت سالبة - كقولنا : «بالضرورة لاشيء من الإنسان بمتنفس في وقت ما لادائماً» - فتركيبها من سالبة منتشرة مطلقة. وهي الجزء الأول، وموجبة مطلقة عامة، وهي مفهوم اللادوام. وهي أعم من الوقتيّة، لأنّه إذا صدقت الضرورة في وقت معين لا دائماً صدقت الضرورة في وقت ما لادائماً، بدون العكس. ونسبتها مع القضايا الباقية على قياس نسبة الوقتية من غير فرق. واعلم أن الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة - اللتين هما جزءا الوقتية والمنتشرة - قضيّتان بسيطتان غير معدودتين في البسائط، حكم في إحداهما بالضرورة في وقت معين وفي الأخرى بالضرورة في وقت ما فالأولى سميت «وقتيّة» لاعتبار تعيين الوقت فيها و «مطلقة» لعدم تقييدها باللادوم أو اللاضرورة، والأخرى منتشرة» لأنه لما لم يتعين وقت الحكم فيها احتمل الحكم فيها لكل وقت، فيكون منتشراً في الأوقات، ومطلقة لأنها غير مقيّدة باللادوام أو اللاضرورة؛ ولهذا إذا قيدنا بأحدهما حذف الإطلاق من اسميهما، فكانتا وقتية ومنتشرة لا مطلقتين. افتراق المطلقة الوقتية عن الوقتية المطلقة والمطلقة المنتشرة عن المنتشرة المطلقة] وربما تسمع فيما بعد «مطلقة وقتية» و «مطلقة منتشرة» وهما غير الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، فإن المطلقة الوقتية التي حكم فيها بالنسبة بالفعل في وقت معين والمطلقة المنتشرة هي التي حكم فيها بالنسبة بالفعل في وقت غير معيّن و يفرق بينهما بالعموم والخصوص - وهو واضح لاسترة فيه.
Statement: The diffuse proposition is that in which judgment is made of the necessity of the predicate's affirmation for the subject or its negation from it at an unspecified time from the times of the subject's existence, not perpetually according to essence. And what is meant by non-specification is not that non-specification is taken as a condition in it, but rather that it is not restricted by specification and is sent absolutely. If it is affirmative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time, not perpetually,' its composition is from an affirmative diffuse absolute - which is our saying: 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time' - and a universal absolute negative - i.e., our saying: 'No human is a breather actually,' which is the concept of non-perpetuity. If it is negative, like our saying: 'Necessarily, no human is a breather at some time, not perpetually,' its composition is from a negative diffuse absolute, which is the first part, and a universal absolute affirmative, which is the concept of non-perpetuity. It is more general than the temporal, because if the necessity at a specific time not perpetually is true, then the necessity at some time not perpetually is true, without the converse. Its relation to the remaining propositions is analogous to that of the temporal, without difference. Know that the temporal absolute and the diffuse absolute - which are the two parts of the temporal and the diffuse - are two simple propositions not counted among the simples. One of them judges necessity at a specific time, and the other judges necessity at some time. The first is called 'temporal' because of considering the specification of time in it, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by non-perpetuity or non-necessity. The other is called 'diffuse' because when the time of judgment is not specified, the judgment is possible for every time, so it is diffused over times, and 'absolute' because it is not restricted by non-perpetuity or non-necessity. Hence if we restrict either with one of them, the name 'absolute' is removed from them, and they become temporal and diffuse, not absolute. The distinction between the absolute temporal and the temporal absolute, and the absolute diffuse and the diffuse absolute: Perhaps you will later hear 'absolute temporal' and 'absolute diffuse,' which are different from the temporal absolute and the diffuse absolute. For the absolute temporal is that in which judgment is made of the relation actually at a specific time, and the absolute diffuse is that in which judgment is made of the relation actually at an unspecified time. They are distinguished by generality and specificity - and this is clear with no obscurity.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the diffuse (muntashira) modal proposition. The author begins by stating that a diffuse proposition judges the necessity of a predicate for a subject (or its negation) at an unspecified time of the subject's existence, but not perpetually according to essence. Importantly, 'unspecified' does not mean that non-specification is a condition; rather, the proposition is simply not bound to a specific time. The author then explains the composition of an affirmative diffuse: it consists of an affirmative diffuse absolute (e.g., 'Necessarily, every human is a breather at some time') and a universal absolute negative ('No human is a breather actually'), which captures the idea of non-perpetuity. Similarly, a negative diffuse is composed of a negative diffuse absolute and a universal absolute affirmative. The diffuse is said to be more general than the temporal (waqtiyya), because if a necessity holds at a specific time, it also holds at some time, but not vice versa. The relation to other propositions parallels that of the temporal. The author then clarifies that the two simple components—temporal absolute and diffuse absolute—are not counted among the 'simple' propositions because they are themselves composite in meaning. The temporal absolute judges necessity at a specific time, while the diffuse absolute judges necessity at some time; they are called 'absolute' because they are not qualified by non-perpetuity or non-necessity. If such a qualification is added, they are no longer called 'absolute.' Finally, the passage distinguishes between 'absolute temporal' (mutlaqa waqtiyya) and 'temporal absolute' (waqtiyya mutlaqa), as well as 'absolute diffuse' and 'diffuse absolute.' The former pair judge the relation actually (bil-fi'l) at a specific or unspecified time, respectively, while the latter pair judge necessity. The difference is one of generality and specificity, and the author states it is clear without obscurity.

Key Terms
منتشرة
وقتية مطلقة
مطلقة عامة

Key Takeaway
The diffuse proposition is defined by necessity at an unspecified time and is composed of a diffuse absolute and a universal absolute expressing non-perpetuity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition of the Special Possible (Mumkina Khassa) Proposition
Author defines the special possible proposition and explains its composition from two general possible propositions of opposite quality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the special possible proposition and distinguish it from the general possible by its composition and the sides of necessity negated.

Core Idea
The special possible negates necessity from both sides, making it a conjunction of two general possibles of opposite quality.

In Islamic logic, modal propositions are essential for precise reasoning in theology and jurisprudence. The special possible (mumkina khassa) is a key modal type that expresses genuine two-sided possibility. This lesson unpacks its definition and internal structure, showing how it relates to the simpler general possible (mumkina ‘amma). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between one-sided and two-sided possibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The term being defined is ‘special possible’ (الممكنة الخاصة). Note its qualifier ‘special’ (خاصة) which distinguishes it from the general possible.
The author begins by naming the seventh type of modal proposition.
2
Extract the differentia
The special possible is defined as a judgment removing absolute necessity from both sides (existence and non-existence). ‘Both sides’ means both the affirmative and negative forms of the proposition are possible, not necessary.
Contrast with the general possible, which only negates necessity from one side.
3
Understand the composition
The text states it is composed of two general possible propositions (ممكنتان عامتان) of opposite quality: one affirmative and one negative. This means the special possible is equivalent to the conjunction of ‘it is not necessary that S is P’ and ‘it is not necessary that S is not P’.
The two general possibles are the immediate components.
4
Apply the rule of correspondence
The author gives a rule: ‘non-perpetuity’ (لا دوام) refers to a universal absolute proposition, and ‘non-necessity’ (لا ضرورة) refers to a general possible. These two, when combined opposite in quality but same in quantity, yield the special possible.
This rule connects the special possible to other modal operators.
5
Test with an example
Take the proposition ‘every human is a writer with special possibility’. This is equivalent to: ‘not necessarily every human is a writer’ (general possible affirmative) and ‘not necessarily no human is a writer’ (general possible negative).
The example shows how the special possible decomposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : السابعة الممكنة الخاصة وهي التي يحكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة عن جانبي الوجود والعدم جميعاً، وهي - سواء كانت موجبة كقولنا: «بالإمكان الخاص كلّ إنسان كاتب أو سالبة: «كقولنا بالإمكان الخاص لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب فتركيبها من ممكنتين عامتين إحداهما موجبـة والأخرى سالبة. والضابط فيها أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة، واللاضرورة إشارة إلى ممكنة عامة مخالفتي الكيفية موافقتي الكمية للقضية المقيدة بهما. القول : الممكنة الخاصة هي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المطلقة عن جانبي الإيجاب والسلب، فإذا قلنا : «كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص» أو «لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان الخاص» كان معناه
He said: The seventh is the special possible, which is that in which judgment is made of the removal of absolute necessity from both sides of existence and non-existence together. Whether it is affirmative, like our saying: 'With special possibility, every human is a writer,' or negative, like our saying: 'With special possibility, no human is a writer,' its composition is from two general possible propositions, one affirmative and the other negative. The rule concerning it is that non-perpetuity refers to a universal absolute, and non-necessity refers to a general possible, opposite in quality but agreeing in quantity with the proposition qualified by them. Statement: The special possible is that in which judgment is made of the negation of absolute necessity from both sides of affirmation and negation. So if we say: 'Every human is a writer with special possibility,' or 'No human is a writer with special possibility,' its meaning is...
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the 'special possible' (mumkina khassa) proposition, which is the seventh type of modal proposition discussed. The author explains that it judges the removal of absolute necessity from both sides—i.e., from both existence and non-existence of the predicate for the subject. Equivalently, it negates absolute necessity from both affirmation and negation. The proposition can be affirmative or negative, with the particle 'bi-al-imkan al-khass' indicating special possibility. Its composition is from two general possible propositions (mumkinatan 'ammatan) that are of opposite quality: one affirmative and one negative. The author then gives a rule: 'non-perpetuity' (la-dawam) refers to a universal absolute proposition, and 'non-necessity' (la-darura) refers to a general possible proposition. These two components are opposite in quality but agree in quantity with the proposition they qualify. The passage ends with a reiteration of the definition and examples, but the sentence is incomplete in the provided text, likely continued in subsequent pages.

Key Terms
ممكنة خاصة (mumkina khassa): A modal proposition that negates absolute necessity from both sides (affirmation and negation). It is composed of two general possible propositions of opposite quality. [Note: This term is specific to the Qutbi/Shamsiyya tradition; in other works, 'mumkina' may refer to a simple possible without the 'khassa' qualifier.]
ممكنة عامة (mumkina 'amma): A general possible proposition, which is a component of the special possible. It indicates non-necessity (la-darura). [Note: No significant shift; it is a standard term in modal logic.]
لاضرورة (la-darura): Non-necessity, referring to a general possible proposition that qualifies the proposition by denying necessity. [Note: In other contexts, la-darura might mean 'no harm'; here it is a technical modal term.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the composition of a special possible (mumkina khassa) proposition?
A) Two general possible propositions of the same quality.
B) Two general possible propositions of opposite quality.
C) A universal absolute and a general possible of the same quality.
D) A diffuse absolute and a temporal absolute.

Key Takeaway
The special possible proposition negates absolute necessity from both affirmation and negation, and it is composed of two general possible propositions of opposite quality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every human is a writer with special possibility', write the two general possible propositions that compose it, specifying their quality.
?
Determine whether the proposition 'No human is a writer with special possibility' is an affirmative or negative special possible, and explain its composition.

Parsing Absolute Temporal vs Temporal Absolute
Author distinguishes between two pairs of absolute modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between muthlaqa waqtiyya and waqtiyya muthlaqa (and similarly for the widespread pair) by analyzing the position of 'muthlaqa' in the compound term.

Core Idea
Word order changes logical scope: 'muthlaqa waqtiyya' restricts time, 'waqtiyya muthlaqa' does not.

In modal logic, the order of qualifiers matters. The author alerts us to a subtle but crucial difference between terms like 'muthlaqa waqtiyya' (absolute temporal) and 'waqtiyya muthlaqa' (temporal absolute). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when reading later propositions. This lesson will parse the logical import of each term.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs
Locate in the passage the terms: muthlaqa waqtiyya vs waqtiyya muthlaqa; and muthlaqa muntashira vs muntashira muthlaqa. Note the reversal of word order.
2
Parse the first pair grammatically
In 'muthlaqa waqtiyya', 'muthlaqa' is the noun (absolute proposition) and 'waqtiyya' is its adjective (temporal). So it is an absolute proposition that is qualified by a specific time. In 'waqtiyya muthlaqa', 'waqtiyya' is the noun (temporal proposition) and 'muthlaqa' is its adjective (absolute). So it is a temporal proposition that is unrestricted in its time range.
The same logic applies to the second pair with 'muntashira' (widespread).
3
Determine the scope of each
For muthlaqa waqtiyya, the modality (absolute) is primary and the time is a restriction: the judgment applies at a specific moment. For waqtiyya muthlaqa, the time qualifier is primary and the absoluteness means the judgment holds at any time, without specifying which.
Thus 'muthlaqa waqtiyya' is more specific (particular), while 'waqtiyya muthlaqa' is more general.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'The fasting of Ramadan is obligatory.' If we say 'muthlaqa waqtiyya', it means the obligation is absolute but specifically for the month of Ramadan. If we say 'waqtiyya muthlaqa', it means the obligation is temporal (i.e., applies during Ramadan) but without any further restriction – it could apply at any time during Ramadan. The first restricts the time to the entire month, the second might apply to any part of it.
This distinction helps in analyzing legal rulings that depend on time.”

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افتراق المطلقة الوقتية عن الوقتية المطلقة والمطلقة المنتشرة عن المنتشرة المطلقة]
وربما تسمع فيما بعد «مطلقة وقتية» و «مطلقة منتشرة» وهما غير الوقتية المطلقة والمنتشرة المطلقة، فإن المطلقة الوقتية التي حكم فيها بالنسبة بالفعل في وقت معين والمطلقة المنتشرة هي التي حكم فيها بالنسبة بالفعل في وقت غير معيّن ويفرق بينهما بالعموم والخصوص - وهو واضح لاسترة فيه.
The distinction between the absolute temporal and the temporal absolute, and the absolute widespread and the widespread absolute. And perhaps you will hear later 'absolute temporal' and 'absolute widespread', and they are not the temporal absolute and the widespread absolute. For the absolute temporal is that in which judgment is made concerning the relation in act at a specific time, and the absolute widespread is that in which judgment is made concerning the relation in act at an unspecified time. They are distinguished by generality and particularity - and it is clear due to [?].
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage clarifies a terminological nuance in modal propositions. The author explains that terms like 'muthlaqa waqtiyya' (absolute temporal) and 'muthlaqa muntashira' (absolute widespread) are distinct from 'waqtiyya muthlaqa' (temporal absolute) and 'muntashira muthlaqa' (widespread absolute). In the first pair, 'muthlaqa' modifies the time-bound proposition, indicating the judgment is actually made at a specific time (for temporal) or an unspecified time (for widespread). In the second pair, 'muthlaqa' is the main noun, and the temporal qualification is secondary. The key difference lies in generality and particularity: the 'muthlaqa waqtiyya' is more specific because it restricts the time to a definite moment, while 'waqtiyya muthlaqa' is unrestricted. Similarly for the widespread versions. The final phrase is obscure but likely means the distinction is clear upon reflection.

Defining the Special Possible Proposition
Definition and composition of the special possible proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the special possible proposition, identify its composition, and distinguish it from other compound modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
The special possible proposition negates necessity on both sides, making it the most general compound modal.

In this lesson, we explore the seventh compound modal proposition: the special possible (al-mumkina al-khassa). The passage defines it as a judgment that removes absolute necessity from both existence and non-existence. Understanding its composition from two general possible propositions—one affirmative, one negative—is key to grasping its unique place in the hierarchy of modal propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
The author defines the 'special possible' (al-mumkina al-khassa). Locate the Arabic term and its description as a compound proposition that negates absolute necessity from both sides.
The term is the seventh in a series of compound propositions.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus is 'compound proposition' (qadiya murakkaba). The differentia is that it negates absolute necessity from both affirmation and negation sides, not just one.
Contrast with other compounds that may involve necessity or permanence.
3
Analyze the composition
The author states it is composed of two general possible propositions (mumkina 'amma): one affirmative and one negative. For example, 'Every human is a writer by special possibility' means both 'Affirmation is not necessary' and 'Negation is not necessary'.
The wording may be affirmative or negative, but the underlying meaning is the same.
4
Compare with other modal propositions
The special possible is more general than most compounds (since every compound has at least one possible side), opposite to absolute necessary, and more specific than the general possible. Study the author's comparisons to understand the hierarchy.
The special possible is the most general compound, while the general possible is the most general simple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦١- من القضايا المركبة: الممكنة الخاصة]
قال : السابعة الممكنة الخاصة وهي التي يحكم فيها بارتفاع الضرورة المطلقة عن جانبي الوجود والعدم جميعاً، وهي - سواء كانت موجبة كقولنا: «بالإمكان الخاص كلّ إنسان كاتب» أو سالبة: «كقولنا بالإمكان الخاص لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب» فتركيبها من ممكنتين عامتين إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة.
والضابط فيها أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة، واللاضرورة إشارة إلى ممكنة عامة مخالفتي الكيفية موافقتي الكمية للقضية المقيدة بهما.
القول : الممكنة الخاصة هي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة المطلقة عن جانبي الإيجاب والسلب، فإذا قلنا : «كل إنسان كاتب بالإمكان الخاص» أو «لاشيء من الإنسان بكاتب بالإمكان الخاص» كان معناه إيجاب الكتابة للإنسان وسلبها عنه ليسا بضروريين، لكن سلب ضرورة الإيجاب إمكان عام سالب و سلب ضرورة السلب إمكان عام موجب. فالممكنة الخاصة - سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة – يكون تركيبها من ممكنتين عامتين، إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ فلا فرق بين موجبتها وسالبتها في المعنى، لأن معنى الممكنة الخاصة رفع الضرورة عن الطرفين سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، بل في اللفظ حتّى إذا عبرت بعبارة إيجابية كانت موجبة وإن عبرت بعبارة سلبية كانت سالبة.
وهي أعم من سائر المركبات لأنّ في كلّ منها إيجاباً أو سلباً، ولا أقل فيهما من أن يكون ممكنتين بالإمكان العام، ولا يلزم من إمكان الإيجاب والسلب أن يكون أحدهما بالفعل بالضرورة أو بالدوام.
ومباينة للضرورية المطلقة وأعمّ من الدائمة والعامتين والمطلقة العامة من وجه لتصادقها في مادة الوجودية اللاضرورية، وصدق الممكنة الخاصة بدونها حيث لاخروج للممكن من القوة إلى الفعل، وبالعكس في مادة الضرورية.
وأخص من الممكنة العامة.
ضابطة في النسبة بين القضايا المركبة]
فقد ظهر مما ذكرنا أن الممكنة العامة أعم القضايا البسيطة والممكنة الخاصة أعم المركبات، والضروريّة أخص البسائط، والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات على وجه.
[61 - From the compound propositions: the special possible] He said: The seventh is the special possible, which is that in which judgment is made by the removal of absolute necessity from both sides of existence and non-existence together. It - whether affirmative like our saying: 'By special possibility, every human is a writer' or negative: 'By special possibility, no human is a writer' - its composition is from two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative. And the rule therein: that non-permanence indicates a general absolute, and non-necessity indicates a general possible, opposite in quality, matching in quantity to the proposition qualified by them. The statement: The special possible is that in which judgment is made by negating absolute necessity from both sides of affirmation and negation. So if we say: 'Every human is a writer by special possibility' or 'No human is a writer by special possibility', its meaning is that affirmation of writing for human and its negation are not necessary. But negation of the necessity of affirmation is a negative general possible, and negation of the necessity of negation is a positive general possible. So the special possible - whether affirmative or negative - its composition is from two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative; so there is no difference between its affirmative and negative in meaning, because the meaning of the special possible is removal of necessity from both sides, whether it is affirmative or negative. Rather, in wording, if it is expressed by an affirmative phrase, it is affirmative, and if by a negative phrase, it is negative. And it is more general than the rest of the compounds because in each of them there is affirmation or negation, and the least of them is that they are two general possible propositions. And it is not necessary from the possibility of affirmation and negation that one of them is actual by necessity or permanence. And it is opposite to the absolute necessary, and more general than the perpetual, the two general [propositions], and the general absolute in one respect, because they coincide in the material of existential non-necessary, and the special possible holds true without them where there is no exit of the possible from potency to act, and vice versa in the material of necessity. And it is more specific than the general possible. [A rule concerning the relation between compound propositions] So it has become clear from what we have mentioned that the general possible is the most general of simple propositions, the special possible is the most general of compounds, the necessary is the most specific of simples, and the special conditional is the most specific of compounds in one respect.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh compound modal proposition, al-mumkina al-khassa (the special possible). The author begins by stating that it is a proposition that judges by removing absolute necessity from both sides of existence and non-existence. That is, it denies that either affirmation or negation is necessary. He gives examples: 'Every human is a writer by special possibility' (affirmative) and 'No human is a writer by special possibility' (negative). The key insight is that this proposition is composed of two general possible propositions: one affirmative and one negative. To clarify: saying that affirmation is not necessary is a negative general possible, and saying that negation is not necessary is an affirmative general possible. Thus, any special possible proposition, regardless of its wording, has the same meaning: absolute necessity is negated for both sides. The wording only determines its superficial classification as affirmative or negative. The author then explains that the special possible is more general than other compounds because every compound proposition must have at least one of affirmation or negation, and each of those must be possible in the general sense. However, from the possibility of both sides, it does not follow that either is actual by necessity or permanence. He then compares it to other modal propositions: it is opposite to the absolute necessary (which asserts necessity for one side), more general than the perpetual, the two general ones (general absolute and general possible?), and the general absolute in one respect because they sometimes coincide in the material of existential non-necessary. However, the special possible can be true even when the possible never becomes actual (remains in potency), while the others cannot. Finally, a concluding rule states that the general possible is the most general of simple propositions (because it is the broadest modal), the special possible is the most general of compound propositions, the necessary is the most specific of simples, and the special conditional is the most specific of compounds. This passage is essential for understanding the hierarchy of modal propositions in Islamic logic.

الضرورة المطلقة
الممكنة العامة

Defining General Possible and Specific Possible
Author defines al-mumkinat al-khassa as composite and compares its scope with other modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the general possible and specific possible modal propositions, explain their composition, and identify their verbal versus meaning differences.

Core Idea
The specific possible is a compound of two general possibilities.

Modal propositions classify statements by necessity, possibility, or impossibility. This lesson clarifies two key types: the general possible (al-mumkinat al-'amma), a simple proposition, and the specific possible (al-mumkinat al-khassa), a compound proposition that removes necessity from both sides. Understanding this distinction is foundational for grasping the hierarchy of modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the origin of general possible
Begin by noting that for a human, both affirmation and negation of writing are not necessary. The negation of necessity of affirmation gives a general possible negative, and the negation of necessity of negation gives a general possible affirmative. Thus the general possible can be either affirmative or negative.
The passage uses the example of 'writing for a human' to illustrate the concept.
2
Examine the composition of specific possible
The specific possible is composed of two general possible propositions: one affirmative and one negative. This makes it a compound proposition, unlike the simple general possible.
3
Note the meaning vs wording distinction
In meaning, both affirmative and negative specific possible remove necessity from both sides. The only difference is verbal: if expressed affirmatively, it is called affirmative; if negatively, negative.
4
Contrast with other propositions
The specific possible is more general than all other compound propositions because they involve at least one affirmation or negation, which are possible in the general sense. Also, it is more specific than the general possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضايا الموجهة
إيجاب الكتابة للإنسان وسلبها عنه ليسا بضروريين، لكن سلب ضرورة الإيجاب إمكان عام سالب و سلب ضرورة السلب إمكان عام موجب. فالممكنة الخاصة - سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة – يكون تركيبها من ممكنتين عامتين، إحداهما موجبة والأخرى سالبة؛ فلا فرق بين موجبتها وسالبتها في المعنى، لأن معنى الممكنة الخاصة رفع الضرورة عن الطرفين سواء كانت موجبة أو سالبة، بل في اللفظ حتّى إذا عبرت بعبارة إيجابية كانت موجبة وإن عبرت بعبارة سلبية كانت سالبة.
وهي أعم من سائر المركبات لأنّ في كلّ منها إيجاباً أو سلباً، ولا أقل فيهما من أن يكون ممكنتين بالإمكان العام، ولا يلزم من إمكان الإيجاب والسلب أن يكون أحدهما بالفعل بالضرورة أو بالدوام.
ومباينة للضرورية المطلقة وأعمّ من الدائمة والعامتين والمطلقة العامة من وجه لتصادقها في مادة الوجودية اللاضرورية، وصدق الممكنة الخاصة بدونها حيث لاخروج للممكن من القوة إلى الفعل، وبالعكس في مادة الضرورية.
وأخص من الممكنة العامة.
ضابطة في النسبة بين القضايا المركبة
فقد ظهر مما ذكرنا أن الممكنة العامة أعم القضايا البسيطة والممكنة الخاصة أعم المركبات، والضروريّة أخص البسائط، والمشروطة الخاصة أخص المركبات على وجه.
Modal Propositions. The affirmation of writing for a human and its negation for him are not necessary. But the negation of necessity of affirmation is a general possible negative, and the negation of necessity of negation is a general possible affirmative. So the specific possible — whether affirmative or negative — its composition is from two general possible propositions, one affirmative and the other negative. So there is no difference between its affirmative and negative in meaning, because the meaning of the specific possible is the removal of necessity from both sides, whether it is affirmative or negative. Rather, only in wording: if it is expressed with an affirmative expression it is affirmative, and if it is expressed with a negative expression it is negative. And it is more general than all other compound propositions, because in each of them there is affirmation or negation, and at least both of them are possible with general possibility. And it does not follow from the possibility of affirmation and negation that one of them is actual necessarily or permanently. And it is mutually exclusive with absolute necessary, and more general than the permanent, the two general, and the general absolute from one aspect, because they agree in the matter of existential non-necessary, and the specific possible is true without them where the possible does not go out from potency to act, and vice versa in necessary matter. And it is more specific than the general possible. A rule regarding the relation between compound propositions: For it has become clear from what we mentioned that the general possible is the most general of simple propositions, and the specific possible is the most general of compound propositions, and the necessary is the most specific of simple propositions, and the specific conditional is the most specific of compound propositions absolutely.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of modal propositions, specifically focusing on the distinction between general possible (al-mumkinat al-'amma) and specific possible (al-mumkinat al-khassa). The author begins by stating that the affirmation of writing for a human and its negation are not necessary, meaning that both are possible. Then he explains that the negation of the necessity of affirmation yields a general possible negative, and the negation of the necessity of negation yields a general possible affirmative. This establishes that the general possible proposition can be either affirmative or negative. The author then defines the specific possible: it is composed of two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative. Importantly, there is no difference in meaning between its affirmative and negative forms; they both express the removal of necessity from both sides. The only distinction is verbal: if expressed affirmatively, it is called affirmative; if negatively, negative. Next, the author makes several comparative claims about the scope of the specific possible. It is more general than all other compound propositions because every compound proposition involves at least one affirmation or negation, and both of these are possible in the general sense. However, the possibility of affirmation and negation does not entail that one is actual necessarily or permanently. The specific possible is also mutually exclusive with the absolute necessary proposition. It is more general than the permanent proposition (da'ima), the two general propositions (al-'ammatayn), and the general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) from one aspect: they coincide in existential non-necessary matter, while the specific possible can be true without them when the possible does not actualize; conversely, they can be true without the specific possible in necessary matter. Finally, the specific possible is more specific than the general possible. The author then provides a summarizing rule: the general possible is the most general among simple propositions, the specific possible is the most general among compound propositions, the necessary is the most specific among simple, and the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is the most specific among compound propositions. This rule is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of modal propositions.

الممكنة الخاصة
الضرورية المطلقة

Key Takeaway
The specific possible is a compound proposition that removes necessity from both sides, making it the most general compound modality.

Hierarchy of Modal Propositions: Generality and Specificity
Author defines al-mumkinat al-khassa as composite and compares its scope with other modal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the main modal propositions from most general to most specific using the author's rule.

Core Idea
The general possible is the broadest simple; the specific possible is the broadest compound.

Modal propositions are not all equal. The passage concludes with a rule that orders them from most general to most specific. This hierarchy is essential for determining which modality applies and how they relate. This lesson unpacks that rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four key propositions
The rule mentions: general possible (simple), specific possible (compound), absolute necessary (simple), specific conditional (compound).
2
Understand the extremes
General possible is the most general simple; absolute necessary is the most specific simple. Specific possible is the most general compound; specific conditional is the most specific compound.
3
Analyze the relation between specific possible and others
The earlier part of the passage details that specific possible is mutually exclusive with absolute necessary, more general than permanent and general absolute from one aspect, etc.
4
Formulate the hierarchy
Create an ordering: Most general overall: General possible (simple). Then other simple modals. Then specific possible (most general compound). Then other compound modals. Then specific conditional (narrowest compound). Absolute necessary is the narrowest simple.
The passage states that specific possible is more specific than general possible.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of modal propositions, specifically focusing on the distinction between general possible (al-mumkinat al-'amma) and specific possible (al-mumkinat al-khassa). This establishes that the general possible proposition can be either affirmative or negative. The author then defines the specific possible: it is composed of two general possible propositions, one affirmative and one negative. Importantly, there is no difference in meaning between its affirmative and negative forms; they both express the removal of necessity from both sides. The only distinction is verbal: if expressed affirmatively, it is called affirmative; if negatively, negative. Next, the author makes several comparative claims about the scope of the specific possible. It is more general than all other compound propositions because every compound proposition involves at least one affirmation or negation, and both of these are possible in the general sense. However, the possibility of affirmation and negation does not entail that one is actual necessarily or permanently. The specific possible is also mutually exclusive with the absolute necessary proposition. It is more general than the permanent proposition (da'ima), the two general propositions (al-'ammatayn), and the general absolute proposition (al-mutlaqa al-'amma) from one aspect: they coincide in existential non-necessary matter, while the specific possible can be true without them when the possible does not actualize; conversely, they can be true without the specific possible in necessary matter. Finally, the specific possible is more specific than the general possible. The author then provides a summarizing rule: the general possible is the most general among simple propositions, the specific possible is the most general among compound propositions, the necessary is the most specific among simple, and the specific conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa) is the most specific among compound propositions. This rule is crucial for understanding the hierarchy of modal propositions.

Key Terms
الممكنة العامة
الممكنة الخاصة
الضرورية المطلقة
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which proposition is the most specific among compound propositions?
A) الممكنة الخاصة
B) الممكنة العامة
C) المشروطة الخاصة
D) الضرورية المطلقة

Key Takeaway
The rule of modal proposition hierarchy: General possible is the broadest simple; specific possible is the broadest compound; absolute necessary is the narrowest simple; specific conditional is the narrowest compound.

Why 'La Dawam' and 'La Darura' Are Indications, Not Direct Meanings
Author explains why 'la dawam' and 'la darura' indicate general absolute and general possible propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a term's relationship to a proposition is literal (mutabaqi) or implicative (iltizami) using the examples of 'la dawam' and 'la darura'.

Core Idea
One term indicates directly, the other implies; both are not literal synonyms.

In the study of compound modal propositions, precision is critical. The author examines two key terms — 'la dawam' (non-permanent) and 'la darura' (non-necessary) — and clarifies that they are not both direct synonyms for the propositions they indicate. Understanding the difference between literal and implicative meaning is essential for correctly interpreting and constructing such propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms and their indicated propositions
Note that 'la dawam' indicates the general absolute proposition, and 'la darura' indicates the general possible proposition. Observe that they differ in quality and agree in quantity with the original proposition.
This is the rule for compound propositions.
2
Compare literal meaning with the proposition
For 'la dawam', the literal meaning is 'removal of permanence' (e.g., of affirmation). The general absolute proposition is not that removal, but a necessary consequence. For 'la darura', the literal meaning 'negation of necessity' is exactly the same as the general possible proposition.
This contrast shows why the author uses 'indication' (ishara) for both.
3
Understand the author's choice of 'indication'
Because 'la dawam' relates implicatively (iltizami) and 'la darura' relates literally (mutabaqi), the term 'indication' covers both cases without implying that both are direct meanings.
This precision avoids confusion.
4
Apply the distinction to other terms
Use the concepts of mafhum mutabaqi and mafhum iltizami to analyze other logical terms, like 'wajib' (necessary) or 'mumtani' (impossible).
This skill is central to interpreting modal propositions correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وظهر أيضاً أن اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة، واللاضرورة إلى ممكنة عامة مخالفتين في الكيف للقضية المقيدة بهما - حتّى إن كانت موجبة كانتا سالبتين، وإن كانت سالبة كانتا موجبتين – وموافقتين لها في الكم، فإن كانت كلية كانتا كلّيتين، وإن كانت جزئيّة كانتا جزئيتين، هذا هو الضابط في معرفة تركيب القضايا المركبة.
وإنما قال : «اللادوام إشارة إلى مطلقة عامة» ولم يقل «اللادوام معناه المطلقة العامة» لأن المعنى إذا أطلق يراد به المفهوم المطابقي ، وليس مفهوم اللادوام المطابقي المطلقة العامة، فإنّ لادوام الإيجاب مثلاً مفهومه الصريح رفع دوام الإيجاب، وإطلاق السلب ليس هو نفس رفع دوام الإيجاب بل لازمه، فهو معناه الالتزامي.
و أما اللاضرورة فمعناه الصريح الإمكان العام ، لأنّ «لاضرورة الإيجاب» مثلا هو سلب ضرورة الإيجاب، وهو عين إمكان السلب، فلما كان إحدى القضيّتين عين معنى إحدى العبارتين، والأخرى ليست بمعنى الأخرى، بل من لوازمها ، استعمل عبارة الإشارة لتكون مشتركة بينهما.
(۱) هذا كلام صحيح، وجواز تقسيم معنى اللفظ إلى المطابقي والتضمني والالتزامي لاينافي ماذكره، فإن الوجود إذا أطلق يتبادر منه الوجود الخارجي مع أنه يصح تقسيمه إلى الخارجي والذهني (شريف).
And it also became clear that 'non-permanent' (la dawam) is an indication of the general absolute, and 'non-necessary' (la darura) is an indication of the general possible, differing in quality from the proposition qualified by them — so that if it is affirmative, they are both negative; and if it is negative, they are both affirmative — and agreeing with it in quantity. For if it is universal, they are both universal; and if it is particular, they are both particular. This is the rule for knowing the composition of compound propositions. And he only said: 'la dawam is an indication of the general absolute' and did not say 'la dawam means the general absolute', because meaning, when taken absolutely, is understood as the literal (mutabaqi) concept, and the literal concept of la dawam is not the general absolute. For example, la dawam of affirmation, its explicit concept is the removal of permanence of affirmation, and the absolute proposition is not the same as the removal of permanence of affirmation but is its necessary consequence, so it is its implicative (iltizami) meaning. As for la darura, its explicit meaning is general possibility, because 'la darura of affirmation' for example is the negation of necessity of affirmation, which is exactly the possibility of negation. Since one of the two propositions is exactly the meaning of one of the two expressions, and the other is not the meaning of the other but one of its necessary consequences, the expression 'indication' is used to be common between them. (1) This statement is correct, and the permissibility of dividing the meaning of a word into literal (mutabaqi), contextual (tadammuni), and implicative (iltizami) does not contradict what he mentioned, for existence, when taken absolutely, is initially understood as external existence, while it is valid to divide it into external and mental (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage explains the precise meaning and usage of the terms 'la dawam' (non-permanent) and 'la darura' (non-necessary) in relation to the general absolute and general possible propositions. The author begins by affirming that it is established from earlier discussion that 'la dawam' is an indication (ishara) of the general absolute, and 'la darura' is an indication of the general possible. They differ in quality from the proposition they qualify: if the original proposition is affirmative, both 'la dawam' and 'la darura' are negative; if the original is negative, they are affirmative. They agree in quantity: if the original is universal, they are universal; if particular, they are particular. This is the rule for constructing compound propositions. Then the author provides a rationale for why he used the word 'indication' (ishara) rather than 'meaning' (ma'na) for 'la dawam'. The reason is that the term 'meaning', when unqualified, refers to the literal (mutabaqi) concept. But the literal concept of 'la dawam' (e.g., 'la dawam of affirmation') is the removal of permanence of affirmation. The general absolute proposition is not identical to this removal; rather, it is a necessary consequence (lazim) of it. Therefore, the relationship is implicative (iltizami), not literal. In contrast, 'la darura' has a different relationship. The explicit meaning of 'la darura of affirmation' is the negation of necessity of affirmation, which is exactly the same as the possibility of negation—in other words, the general possible proposition. So here the expression is exactly the meaning. Because one of the two terms ('la darura') coincides with its corresponding proposition, while the other ('la dawam') does not, the author opted for the common expression 'indication' to cover both cases. The footnote by al-Sharif confirms the correctness of this analysis and notes that the division of a word's meaning into literal (mutabaqi), contextual (tadammuni), and implicative (iltizami) does not contradict this point. For instance, the word 'existence' immediately brings to mind external existence, yet it can be divided into external and mental existence. This illustrates that a word's initial understanding does not preclude a more nuanced analysis.

Parts of a Conditional and Main Division
Author defines conditional and its main division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the antecedent and consequent in any conditional proposition and classify it as conjunctive or disjunctive.

Core Idea
Every conditional has two parts; the connective determines whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive.

In this passage, the author begins by naming the two parts of a conditional proposition: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali). Then he draws the fundamental distinction between conjunctive (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) conditionals. Understanding this division is the first step in analyzing compound propositions used in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of a conditional
Look for the connective. In Arabic, for conjunctive conditionals, the phrase 'إذا ... فـ' often appears; for disjunctive, 'إما ... أو'. The first part is the antecedent (muqaddam), the second is the consequent (tali).
The author explicitly states: 'The first part is called muqaddam, the second tali.'
2
Classify the type of conditional
If the two parts are connected by an 'if-then' relationship, it is conjunctive (muttasila). If they are connected by 'either-or', it is disjunctive (munfasila).
Memorize the Arabic terms: متصلة for conjunctive, منفصلة for disjunctive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني في أقسام الشرطية . الجزء الأول منها يسمّى «مقدماً» والثاني «تالياً». وهي إما متصلة أو منفصلة: أما المتصلة: فإما «لزوميّة»، وهي التي يكون فيها صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك - كالعلية والتضايف. وإما «اتفاقية»، وهي التي يكون فيها ذلك بمجرد توافق الجزأين على الصدق، كقولنا: إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق». وأما المنفصلة: فإما «حقيقية»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزأيها في الصدق والكذب معاً، كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً». وإما «مانعة الجمع»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين الجزأين في الصدق فقط، كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حجراً أو شجراً». وإما «مانعة الخلو»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين الجزأين في الكذب فقط، كقولنا : «إمّا أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق». أقول : لما وقع الفراغ من الحمليات وأقسامها شرع في أقسام الشرطيات؛ وقد سمعت أن الشرطية ما تتركب من قضيتين.
He said: The second chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. The first part of it is called 'antecedent' (muqaddam) and the second 'consequent' (tali). And it is either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). As for the conjunctive: it is either 'luzumiyya' (implicative), which is that wherein the truth of the consequent follows from the truth of the antecedent due to a relation between them that necessitates that – like causality and correlation; or 'ittifaqiyya' (coincidental), which is that wherein that [truth] occurs merely from the agreement of the two parts in truth, like our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' As for the disjunctive: it is either 'haqiqiyya' (true disjunction), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between its two parts in truth and falsehood together, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd'; or 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventer of conjunction), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in truth only, like our saying: 'Either this thing is a stone or a tree'; or 'mani'at al-khuluww' (preventer of vacuity), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in falsehood only, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown.' I say: When the categorical propositions and their divisions were finished, he began on the divisions of conditionals; and you have heard that the conditional is composed of two propositions.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional propositions. The author begins by naming the two parts of a conditional: the 'muqaddam' (antecedent) and the 'tali' (consequent). Then he divides conditionals into two main categories: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). Conjunctive conditionals are those where the truth of the consequent is dependent on the truth of the antecedent (e.g., 'If..., then...'). These are further divided into 'luzumiyya' (implicative) where the connection is based on a real relation such as causality or correlation, and 'ittifaqiyya' (coincidental) where the connection is merely coincidental, like the example given: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying' – there is no real relation, just both happen to be true. Disjunctive conditionals are those where the two parts mutually exclude each other in some way. These are of three types: 'haqiqiyya' (true disjunction) where they cannot be both true nor both false, like a number being either even or odd; 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventer of conjunction) where they cannot both be true but can both be false, like a thing being either a stone or a tree (it could be something else); and 'mani'at al-khuluww' (preventer of vacuity) where they cannot both be false but can both be true, like Zayd being in the sea or not drowning (he could be both in the sea and drown? Actually the example is problematic, but it means that at least one must be true; if he is not in the sea, he does not drown, so they cannot both be false). The author then remarks that after finishing categoricals, he proceeds to conditionals, noting that a conditional is composed of two propositions.

مقدم
تالي

منفصلة

Key Takeaway

Conjunctive Conditionals: Implicative vs. Coincidental
Author subdivides conjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a luzumiyya (implicative) and ittifaqiyya (coincidental) conjunctive conditional by checking the nature of the connection between antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conjunctive conditional is only useful for inference when the connection is necessary (luzumiyya), not coincidental (ittifaqiyya).

The author divides conjunctive conditionals into two types based on the nature of the implication. In the first, the truth of the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent due to a real relation like causality. In the second, the connection is merely accidental. This distinction is crucial for evaluating the strength of conditional arguments used in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify that the conditional is conjunctive
Check if it has the form 'If A then B' (e.g., إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق). If so, it is muttasila.
2
Determine the nature of the connection
Ask: Is there a real relation between A and B that necessitates B when A is true? For example, causality, correlation, or definitional connection. If yes, it is luzumiyya. If the truth of both parts is merely coincidental, it is ittifaqiyya.
The passage gives causality and correlation as examples of real relations.
3
Test with the given examples
The author's example of ittifaqiyya is 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying'. There is no necessary connection; they just happen to be true. A luzumiyya example would be 'If fire touches cotton, it burns' (causality).
Note that the luzumiyya example is not in the passage but is a standard example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Disjunctive Conditionals: Three Types of Exclusion
Author subdivides disjunctive conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals: haqiqiyya (true disjunction), mani'at al-jam' (preventer of conjunction), and mani'at al-khuluww (preventer of vacuity).

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals differ in what they exclude: only haqiqiyya excludes both truth and falsehood together.

The author presents three kinds of disjunctive conditionals. The first, haqiqiyya, is the strict exclusive 'or' that we commonly understand. The other two are weaker: one only prevents both being true, the other only prevents both being false. Recognizing these types is essential for correctly evaluating disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify that the conditional is disjunctive
Look for the form 'إما ... أو' (either ... or).
2
Analyze the truth possibilities
Consider two aspects: Can both parts be true? Can both parts be false? If neither can happen, it is haqiqiyya. If only cannot both be true (but can both be false), it is mani'at al-jam'. If only cannot both be false (but can both be true), it is mani'at al-khuluww.
Use the examples from the passage to test.
3
Classify the examples
The authors examples: 'إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً' – a number cannot be both even and odd (haqiqiyya). 'إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حجراً أو شجراً' – a thing cannot be both stone and tree, but could be something else (mani'at al-jam'). 'إما أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق' – both cannot be false, but can both be true (mani'at al-khuluww).
Memorize the Arabic terms for each type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الفصل الثاني في أقسام الشرطية . الجزء الأول منها يسمّى «مقدماً» والثاني «تالياً». وهي إما متصلة أو منفصلة: أما المتصلة: فإما «لزوميّة»، وهي التي يكون فيها صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك - كالعلية والتضايف. وإما «اتفاقية»، وهي التي يكون فيها ذلك بمجرد توافق الجزأين على الصدق، كقولنا: إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق». وأما المنفصلة: فإما «حقيقية»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزأيها في الصدق والكذب معاً، كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً». وإما «مانعة الجمع»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين الجزأين في الصدق فقط، كقولنا: إما أن يكون هذا الشيء حجراً أو شجراً». وإما «مانعة الخلو»، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين الجزأين في الكذب فقط، كقولنا : «إمّا أن يكون زيد في البحر أو لا يغرق». أقول : لما وقع الفراغ من الحمليات وأقسامها شرع في أقسام الشرطيات؛ وقد سمعت أن الشرطية ما تتركب من قضيتين.
He said: The second chapter: On the divisions of the conditional. The first part of it is called 'antecedent' (muqaddam) and the second 'consequent' (tali). And it is either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). As for the conjunctive: it is either 'luzumiyya' (implicative), which is that wherein the truth of the consequent follows from the truth of the antecedent due to a relation between them that necessitates that – like causality and correlation; or 'ittifaqiyya' (coincidental), which is that wherein that [truth] occurs merely from the agreement of the two parts in truth, like our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' As for the disjunctive: it is either 'haqiqiyya' (true disjunction), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between its two parts in truth and falsehood together, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd'; or 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventer of conjunction), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in truth only, like our saying: 'Either this thing is a stone or a tree'; or 'mani'at al-khuluww' (preventer of vacuity), which is that wherein mutual exclusion is judged between the two parts in falsehood only, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown.' I say: When the categorical propositions and their divisions were finished, he began on the divisions of conditionals; and you have heard that the conditional is composed of two propositions.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage classifies conditional propositions. The author begins by naming the two parts of a conditional: the 'muqaddam' (antecedent) and the 'tali' (consequent). Then he divides conditionals into two main categories: conjunctive (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila). Conjunctive conditionals are those where the truth of the consequent is dependent on the truth of the antecedent (e.g., 'If..., then...'). These are further divided into 'luzumiyya' (implicative) where the connection is based on a real relation such as causality or correlation, and 'ittifaqiyya' (coincidental) where the connection is merely coincidental, like the example given: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying' – there is no real relation, just both happen to be true. Disjunctive conditionals are those where the two parts mutually exclude each other in some way. These are of three types: 'haqiqiyya' (true disjunction) where they cannot be both true nor both false, like a number being either even or odd; 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventer of conjunction) where they cannot both be true but can both be false, like a thing being either a stone or a tree (it could be something else); and 'mani'at al-khuluww' (preventer of vacuity) where they cannot both be false but can both be true, like Zayd being in the sea or not drowning (he could be both in the sea and drown? Actually the example is problematic, but it means that at least one must be true; if he is not in the sea, he does not drown, so they cannot both be false). The author then remarks that after finishing categoricals, he proceeds to conditionals, noting that a conditional is composed of two propositions.

Key Terms
منفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive conditionals come in three types: haqiqiyya (excludes both truth and falsehood), mani'at al-jam' (excludes truth only), and mani'at al-khuluww (excludes falsehood only).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each disjunctive as haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluww: (a) 'Either this animal is a cow or a horse' – mani'at al-jam' (cannot be both, but can be neither). (b) 'Either the world is created or it is pre-eternal' – in kalam, this is often considered haqiqiyya (cannot be both, cannot be neither). (c) 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' – mani'at al-khuluww (cannot both be false).
