Classification and Refinement of Conditional Proposition Definitions
Author defines conditional propositions and their subtypes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conditional proposition as connected or disconnected, and within connected, as implicative or accidental, and evaluate whether the definition covers false conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions gain logical force from a real relation; without it, they are merely accidental.

In this lesson, we delve into the classification of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya) as laid out in the commentary tradition. The author first divides them into connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila), then subdivides connected into implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). He also refines the definitions to include false cases, ensuring the definitions are not too narrow. This rigorous approach is typical of Islamic logic and is essential for precise reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish connected from disconnected
Identify whether the conditional proposition affirms or denies occurrence of one part with the other (connected) or separation between the parts (disconnected). For example, 'If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son' is connected; 'Either the world is eternal or it is created' is disconnected.
The Arabic terms are 'muttasila' (متصل) and 'munfasila' (منفصل).
2
Subdivide connected into implicative and accidental
Check whether the truth of the consequent on the supposition of the antecedent is due to a relation (alāqa) or merely coincidental. If there is a real relation (causality or correlation), it is implicative; otherwise, it is accidental.
The relation can be causality (cause-effect, effect-cause, or common cause) or correlation (e.g., father-son).
3
Test the definition against false conditionals
Observe how the author critiques the initial definitions for not covering false implicative or accidental conditionals. A proper definition must include cases where the judgment of relation is made even if it does not correspond to reality.
For implicative, the refined definition uses 'judged' (hukima) to allow for false claims of relation.
4
Apply the general vs. specific accidental distinction
Note that the accidental conditional can be understood in a general sense (mere truth of consequent, antecedent may be false) or a specific sense (both parts actually true). The general sense covers false accidentals.
The text says 'by the first sense a specific accidental' and 'by this sense a general accidental'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي إما متصلة إن أوجبت أو سلبت حصول إحداهما عند الأخرى. أو منفصلة إن أوجبت أو سلبت انفصال إحداهما عن الأخرى. والقضية الأولى من جزأي الشرطية سواء كانت متصلة أو منفصلة- تسمّى «مقدماً» لتقدمها في الذكر والقضية الثانية تسمى «تالياً» لتلوها إياها. ثم إن المتصلة إما لزومية وإما اتفاقية. أما اللزومية فهي التي يُحكم بصدق التالي فيها على تقدير صدق المقدم لعلاقة بينهما توجب ذلك ، والمراد بالعلاقة شيء بسببه يستصحب الأول الثاني، كالعلية والتضايف. أما العلية : فبأن يكون المقدّم علّة للتالي، كقولنا : «إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود أو معلولاً له، كقولنا : «إن كان النهار موجوداً طالعة ؛ أو يكونا معلولي علة واحدة، كقولنا : «إن كان النهار موجوداً فالعالم مضيء» فإن وجود النهار وإضاءة العالم معلولان لطلوع الشمس. وأما التضايف فبأن يكونا متضايفين كقولنا : «إن كان زيد أبا عمر كان عمرو ابنه». وهذا التعريف لا يتناول اللزومية الكاذبة، لعدم اعتبار صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدّم لعلاقة فيها ؛ فالأولى أن يقال : «اللزومية ماحكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير قضيّة أخرى لعلاقة بينهما موجبة لذلك» وهو متناول للزومية الكاذبة، لأن الحكم للعلاقة إن طابق الواقع كان الحكم متحققاً والعلاقة أيضاً متحققة، وإن لم يطابق الواقع : فإما لعدم الحكم في الواقع أو لثبوته من غير علاقة. وأما الاتفاقية فهي التي يكون ذلك - أي صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدّم فيها - لا لعلاقة موجبة لذلك، بل بمجرد توافق صدق الجزأين، كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق فإنه لا علاقة بين ناهقية الحمار وناطقيّة الإنسان حتى لا يجوز العقل تحقق كل واحد منهما بدون الآخر ، وليس فيها إلا توافق الطرفين على الصدق. ولو قال : هي التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لالعلاقة ، بل بمجرد صدقهما لكان أولى ليتناول الاتفاقية الكاذبة، فإن الحكم فيها بصدق التالي لا لعلاقة ربما يطابق الواقع، بأن يصدق التالي ولا توجد العلاقة، وربما لم يطابق الواقع بأن لا يصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم أو يصدق وتوجد العلاقة؛ وقد يكتفى في الاتفاقية بصدق التالي، حتى يقال: «إنّها التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير المقدم لالعلاقة ، بل بمجرد صدق التالي »؛ ويجوز أن يكون المقدّم فيها صادقاً أو كاذباً، وتسمّى بهذا المعنى اتفاقية عامة، وبالمعنى الأول اتفاقية خاصة،
And it (the conditional proposition) is either connected, if it affirms or denies the occurrence of one of them (the two parts) with the other, or disconnected, if it affirms or denies the separation of one of them from the other. The first proposition of the two parts of the conditional, whether connected or disconnected, is called the 'antecedent' due to its precedence in mention, and the second proposition is called the 'consequent' due to its following it. Then, the connected conditional is either implicative or accidental. As for the implicative, it is that in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation between them that necessitates that; and by 'relation' is meant something by reason of which the first (antecedent) entails the second (consequent), such as causality and correlation. As for causality: either the antecedent is a cause of the consequent, like our saying 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists'; or it is an effect of it, like our saying 'If daytime exists, then the sun is shining'; or both are effects of one cause, like our saying 'If daytime exists, then the world is illuminated', for the existence of daytime and the illumination of the world are both effects of the sun's rising. As for correlation: that they be correlatives, like our saying 'If Zayd is the father of 'Amr, then 'Amr is his son'. And this definition does not include false implicative conditionals, because it does not consider the truth of the consequent on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation in them; so it is better to say: 'The implicative is that in which the truth of a proposition is judged on the supposition of another proposition due to a relation between them that necessitates that' – and this includes false implicative conditionals, because the judgment concerning the relation – if it corresponds to reality, then the judgment is realized and the relation is also realized; and if it does not correspond to reality, then either because of the non-existence of the judgment in reality or because of its establishment without a relation. As for the accidental, it is that in which that – i.e., the truth of the consequent on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent – is not due to a necessitating relation, but merely due to the coincidence of the truth of the two parts, like our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying' – for there is no relation between the donkey's braying and man's rationality such that the intellect would not allow each one to exist without the other; and there is only the coincidence of the two sides in truth. And if he said: 'It is that in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to their being true' – that would be better, so that it includes false accidental conditionals. For the judgment therein concerning the truth of the consequent without a relation may correspond to reality, in that the consequent is true and the relation is absent; or it may not correspond to reality, in that the consequent is not true on the supposition of the truth of the antecedent, or it is true but the relation exists. And it is sufficient in the accidental that the consequent be true, such that it is said: 'It is that in which the truth of the consequent is judged on the supposition of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to the truth of the consequent'; and it is permissible that the antecedent therein be true or false; and it is called in this sense a general accidental, and by the first sense a specific accidental.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and classificatory exposition from a commentary (sharh) on a matn of logic, likely al-Risala al-Shamsiyya. The author begins by dividing conditional propositions (shartiyya) into two primary types: connected (muttasila) and disconnected (munfasila). A connected conditional is one that affirms or denies the occurrence of one part together with the other (e.g., 'If A then B' or 'If A then not B'), while a disconnected conditional affirms or denies separation between the parts (e.g., 'Either A or B'). Within the connected conditional, the first part is called the antecedent (muqaddam) and the second the consequent (tali).

Next, the author subdivides the connected conditional into implicative (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya). An implicative conditional is defined as one where the truth of the consequent is judged to follow from the truth of the antecedent due to a relation (alāqa) between them that necessitates that entailment. This relation is further specified as either causality (al-‘illiyya) or correlation (al-taḍā’uf). Causality can take three forms: the antecedent is a cause of the consequent (e.g., sunrise causes daytime), the antecedent is an effect of the consequent (e.g., daytime implies sunrise), or both are effects of a common cause (e.g., daytime and world illumination both result from sunrise). Correlation means the antecedent and consequent are correlatives, like fatherhood and sonship.

The author then critiques the initial definition of the implicative conditional for being too narrow: it does not cover false implicatives (al-luzūmiyya al-kādhiba) because it requires actual truth of the consequent. A better definition is proposed: 'The implicative is that in which the truth of a proposition is judged on the supposition of another proposition due to a relation between them that necessitates that' – this includes cases where the relation is asserted but may not correspond to reality, thus including false implicatives. The judgment about the relation may be correct or mistaken.

Turning to accidental conditionals, they are defined as those where the truth of the consequent on the supposition of the antecedent is not due to a necessitating relation but merely to the coincidence of both parts being true. An example given is 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying' – there is no real connection; both parts happen to be true. The author again notes that the definition should also cover false accidental conditionals (where the consequent may be false or the relation actually exists). A refined definition states that in an accidental conditional, the truth of the consequent is judged on the supposition of the antecedent without a relation, merely due to the truth of the consequent. This broader sense is called the general accidental, while the earlier sense (requiring actual truth of both parts) is the specific accidental.

The passage exemplifies the careful analytical method of Islamic logic: definitions are not left as static; they are tested against counterexamples and refined to ensure proper coverage of all intended cases, including false conditionals. This attention to logical rigor is typical of the commentary tradition.

Distinguishing Luzumiyya and Ittifaqiyya in Conditional Propositions
Author defines two types of connected conditionals and refines definitions for false cases

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the implicative (luzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya) conditional propositions and identify the correct definitions that cover both true and false cases.

Core Idea
A conditional may be true without a logical relation; mere coincidence suffices.

In Islamic logic, conditional propositions are used extensively in kalam and usul al-fiqh to construct arguments. Understanding whether a conditional expresses a genuine implication or a mere coincidence is crucial for evaluating the strength of evidence. This passage refines the standard definitions to ensure they also cover false conditionals, which are common in hypothetical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial definition of luzumiyya
The common definition says the consequent is true upon estimation of the antecedent due to a relation. Identify what 'relation' means here and why it only covers true implicatives.
The relation (luzum) is a real connection, like causality or logical necessity.
2
See the refined definition of luzumiyya
The author proposes: 'the implicative is that in which it is judged that a proposition is true upon estimation of another due to a relation necessitating that.' This includes false implicatives because the judgment may not correspond to reality.
3
Understand the coincidental (ittifaqiyya) definition
The coincidental has no relation; the consequent is true upon the antecedent merely because both happen to be true. Example: 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying.'
There is no connection between rationality and braying.
4
Note the refinement for ittifaqiyya to include false cases
The author adds that the definition should say 'judged that the consequent is true upon estimation of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to their truth' to cover false coincidentals. Also distinguishes general (consequent always true) and specific (both parts true) coincidentals.
In the general coincidental, the consequent is true in itself, so it is true with any antecedent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية على تقدير صدق المقدّم لعلاقة فيها ؛ فالأولى أن يقال : «اللزومية ماحكم فيها بصدق قضيّة على تقدير قضيّة أخرى لعلاقة بينهما موجبة لذلك» وهو متناول للزومية الكاذبة، لأن الحكم للعلاقة إن طابق الواقع كان الحكم متحققاً والعلاقة أيضاً متحققة، وإن لم يطابق الواقع : فإما لعدم الحكم في الواقع أو لثبوته من غير علاقة.
وأما الاتفاقية فهي التي يكون ذلك - أي صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدّم فيها - لا لعلاقة موجبة لذلك، بل بمجرد توافق صدق الجزأين، كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق فإنه لا علاقة بين ناهقية الحمار وناطقيّة الإنسان حتى لا يجوز العقل تحقق كل واحد منهما بدون الآخر ، وليس فيها إلا توافق الطرفين على الصدق.
ولو قال : هي التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم لالعلاقة ، بل بمجرد صدقهما لكان أولى ليتناول الاتفاقية الكاذبة، فإن الحكم فيها بصدق التالي لا لعلاقة ربما يطابق الواقع، بأن يصدق التالي ولا توجد العلاقة، وربما لم يطابق الواقع بأن لا يصدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم أو يصدق وتوجد العلاقة؛ وقد يكتفى في الاتفاقية بصدق التالي، حتى يقال: «إنّها التي حكم فيها بصدق التالي على تقدير المقدم لالعلاقة ، بل بمجرد صدق التالي »؛ ويجوز أن يكون المقدّم فيها صادقاً أو كاذباً، وتسمّى بهذا المعنى اتفاقية عامة، وبالمعنى الأول اتفاقية خاصة،
۱) یعنی أن التالي إذا كان صادقاً في نفس الأمر فهو صادق مع جميع الأمور الصادقة في نفس الأمر، ومع جميع ما يقدر صدقه في نفس الأمر، كقولك «إن كان زيد فرساً فالحمار ناهق» (شریف).
Sections of conditional propositions: (The connected conditional) based on the estimation of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation in it. So it is better to say: 'The implicative is that in which it is judged that a proposition is true upon the estimation of another proposition due to a relation between them necessitating that.' And this includes the false implicative, because the judgment of the relation: if it corresponds to reality, then the judgment is realized and the relation is also realized; and if it does not correspond to reality, it is either because there is no judgment in reality or because it is established without a relation.
As for the coincidental, it is that in which that—i.e., the truth of the consequent upon the estimation of the truth of the antecedent—occurs not due to a relation necessitating that, but merely due to the agreement of the truth of the two parts, like our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' For there is no relation between the braying of the donkey and the rationality of man such that the mind does not allow each to be realized without the other; there is only the agreement of the two sides in truth.
And if he had said: 'It is that in which it is judged that the consequent is true upon the estimation of the truth of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to their truth,' it would be more appropriate so as to include the false coincidental. For the judgment that the consequent is true without a relation may correspond to reality, such that the consequent is true and the relation does not exist, and may not correspond to reality, such that the consequent is not true upon the estimation of the truth of the antecedent, or it is true and the relation exists. It may suffice in the coincidental with the truth of the consequent, so that it is said: 'It is that in which it is judged that the consequent is true upon the estimation of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to the truth of the consequent.' It is permissible that the antecedent in it be true or false. It is called by this meaning 'general coincidental' and by the first meaning 'specific coincidental.' (1) Meaning that if the consequent is true in itself, it is true with all true matters in themselves, and with everything whose truth is estimated in itself, like your saying: 'If Zayd were a horse, the donkey is braying.' (Sharif).
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage defines the two types of connected conditional (al-shartiyya al-muttasila). The author begins by critiquing a common definition that 'the consequent is true upon the estimation of the truth of the antecedent due to a relation' because this only covers true implicatives. He proposes a better definition: 'the implicative is that in which it is judged that a proposition is true upon the estimation of another proposition due to a relation between them necessitating that.' This includes false implicatives because the judgment of relation may or may not correspond to reality. Then he defines the coincidental (ittifaqiyya) as one where the truth of the consequent upon the estimation of the antecedent occurs not due to a relation but merely because both parts happen to be true, giving the example 'If man is rational, the donkey is braying.' There is no logical relation here. The author further refines the definition of coincidental: it should be 'judged that the consequent is true upon the estimation of the antecedent without a relation, but merely due to their truth' to cover false coincidentals. He distinguishes a general coincidental (where the consequent is true regardless of antecedent) and a specific coincidental (where both parts are true). The footnote by Sharif explains that in the general coincidental, if the consequent is true in itself, it is true with all antecedents that are true in themselves.

Key Terms

الاتفاقية
الشرطية المتصلة
المقدم
التالي
العلاقة

Checkpoint

A) It is a conditional where the consequent is true upon the estimation of the antecedent due to a relation.
B) It is a conditional where the consequent is true upon the estimation of the antecedent not due to a relation, but merely due to the truth of the consequent.
C) It is a conditional where both parts are true in reality.
D) It is a conditional where the antecedent is always false.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between implicative and coincidental conditionals hinges on the presence or absence of a relation between antecedent and consequent; definitions must cover false cases to be complete.

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions: Haqiqiyya, Mani'at al-Jam', Mani'at al-Khuluww
Author enumerates and defines three types of disjunctive conditionals: haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', and mani'at al-khuluww, with a critical note on the meaning of 'tanafi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive propositions based on their truth conditions and identify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Disjunctions differ in whether they exclude both truth and falsity, or only one of them.

This passage from Quṭb al-Dīn al-Rāzī's logic text systematically defines the three types of disjunctive conditionals. Each type has distinct truth conditions that determine how the disjunction behaves in reasoning. Understanding these types is essential for analyzing arguments that present alternatives, such as in juristic reasoning or theological proofs.

Guided Path
1
List the three types
Look at the passage and list the three types of disjunctive propositions: haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluww.
2
Examine truth conditions for each
For each type, note which combination(s) of truth values are excluded. Haqiqiyya excludes both truth together and falsity together; mani'at al-jam' excludes only truth together; mani'at al-khuluww excludes only falsity together.
3
Analyze the provided examples
The author provides examples: number even/odd (haqiqiyya), tree/stone (mani'at al-jam'), Zayd in sea/not drown (mani'at al-khuluww). Check that each example satisfies its truth conditions.
The example for mani'at al-khuluww is problematic; focus on the logical structure.
4
Understand the naming rationale
The author explains why each type is named as such: haqiqiyya (true disjunction) because its exclusion is strongest; mani'at al-jam' (preventer of conjunction) because it prevents both being true; mani'at al-khuluww (preventer of emptiness) because it prevents both being false (i.e., reality is not empty of one).
5
Note the subtle point on 'tanafi'
The author mentions that 'mutual exclusion' (tanafi) in conjunction means the two parts are not both true of a single essence, not that they do not coexist in existence. This distinguishes logical exclusion from mere existential coexistence.
This is a deeper logical point about intensional vs. extensional reading.

كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً».
ومانعة الخلو، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزايها كذباً فقط، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق».
وإنما سميت الأولى حقيقيّة، لأنّ التنافي بين جزأيها أشد من التنافي بين جزاي الآخرين، لأنه في الصدق والكذب معاً، فهي أحق باسم «المنفصلة » بل هي حقيقية الانفصال.

As for the disjunctive, it has been known that it is of three types:
True (haqiqiyya): that in which it is judged that there is mutual exclusion (tanafi) between its two parts in truth and falsity, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd.'
And Mani'at al-Jam' (exclusive of conjunction): that in which it is judged that there is mutual exclusion between its two parts in truth only, like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone.'
And Mani'at al-Khuluww (exclusive of emptiness): that in which it is judged that there is mutual exclusion between its two parts in falsity only, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown.'
And the first is called 'haqiqiyya' (true) because the mutual exclusion between its two parts is stronger than the mutual exclusion between the parts of the other two, for it is in truth and falsity together, so it is more deserving of the name 'disjunctive'; indeed it is the true disjunction.
The second is called 'mani'at al-jam'' because it includes the prevention of conjunction between its two parts.
The third is called 'mani'at al-khuluww' because reality is not empty of one of its two parts.
And sometimes 'mani'at al-jam'' and 'mani'at al-khuluww' are said for that in which it is judged that there is mutual exclusion in truth or in falsity absolutely, and in this sense they are more general.
And some eminent scholars have a noble investigation here: that the intended meaning of 'mutual exclusion' in conjunction is that they are not both true of a single essence, not that they do not coexist in existence, for if...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of disjunctive conditionals (al-munfasila). The author first states that there are three kinds. The first is the 'true disjunction' (haqiqiyya) which excludes both truth together and falsity together; the example is 'Either this number is even or odd' — they cannot both be true nor both false. The second is 'mani'at al-jam'' (exclusive of conjunction) which excludes only their being together in truth; the example is 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone' — it cannot be both a tree and a stone, but it could be something else (so both could be false). The third is 'mani'at al-khuluww' (exclusive of emptiness) which excludes only their being together in falsity; the example is 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown' — it cannot be that both are false (because if he is not in the sea, he does not drown? Actually the example is problematic, but the author uses it). The author then explains the naming: the first is called 'haqiqiyya' because its mutual exclusion is stronger (in both truth and falsity). The second and third are named based on what they prevent: one prevents conjunction, the other prevents emptiness (i.e., at least one must be true). The author notes that sometimes these terms are used more broadly to mean any disjunction that prevents truth or prevents falsity absolutely, making them more general. Finally, a noble research is mentioned: the meaning of 'mutual exclusion' in conjunction is that the two parts are not both true of a single essence, not that they do not coexist in existence. This is a subtle logical point distinguishing between intensional and extensional interpretation.

D) Either the act is permissible or forbidden.

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunctive propositions are defined by which truth combinations they exclude: haqiqiyya excludes both, mani'at al-jam' excludes truth together, and mani'at al-khuluww excludes falsity together.

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Author enumerates and defines the three types of disjunctive conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any disjunctive proposition into one of the three types: haqiqiyyah, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluw.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions have three degrees of exclusion; only the true disjunctive excludes both truth and falsehood.

In this passage, the author defines the three types of disjunctive conditional propositions. These types are distinguished by whether mutual exclusion applies to truth, falsehood, or both. Understanding this classification is essential for constructing valid disjunctive syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
Find the statement where the author says the disjunctive is of three divisions. This sets up the classification.
The phrase 'وهي على ثلاثة أقسام' introduces the three types.
2
Distinguish each type by its exclusion criteria
For each type, note whether it excludes truth (صدق), falsehood (كذب), or both. Haqiqiyyah excludes both; mani'at al-jam' excludes only truth; mani'at al-khuluw excludes only falsehood.
You can create a table to keep track.
3
Match each type with its example
Connect the Arabic examples to the type. Confirm that the example exhibits the correct exclusion pattern.
The example for haqiqiyyah is 'Either this number is even or odd' – a clear binary choice.
4
Examine the naming rationale
The author explains why each type is named as such. Haqiqiyyah (true) because the exclusion is stronger; mani'at al-jam' because it prevents conjunction (both true); mani'at al-khuluw because reality is never vacant of one part.
Understand the etymology to remember the function.
5
Recognize the broader usage
The author notes that sometimes 'mani'at al-jam'' and 'mani'at al-khuluw' are used in a broader sense to refer to any proposition where exclusion is in truth or falsehood absolutely.
This broader usage is more general than the strict definitions.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المنفصلة فقد عرفت أنها على ثلاثة أقسام : حقيقية وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزايهـا صدقاً وكذباً، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو فرداً». ومانعة الجمع، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزايها صدقاً فقط، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء شجراً أو حجراً». ومانعة الخلق، وهي التي يحكم فيها بالتنافي بين جزايها كذباً فقط، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد في البحر وإما أن لا يغرق». وإنما سميت الأولى حقيقيّة، لأنّ التنافي بين جزأيها أشد من التنافي بين جزاي الآخرين، لأنه في الصدق والكذب معاً، فهي أحق باسم «المنفصلة » بل هي حقيقية الانفصال. والثانية مانعة الجمع، لاشتمالها على منع الجمع بين جزأيها. والثالثة مانعة الخلو، لأن الواقع ليس يخلو عن أحد جزأيها. وربما يقال مانعة الجمع ومانعة الخلوّ على التي حكم فيها بالتنافي في الصدق أو في الكذب مطلقاً، وبهذا المعنى يكونان أعم.
As for the disjunctive, it has been known that it is of three divisions: (1) True (haqiqiyyah), which is that in which mutual exclusion between its two parts is judged in truth and falsehood, like our saying: 'Either this number is even or odd.' (2) Preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), which is that in which mutual exclusion between its two parts is judged only in truth, like our saying: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone.' (3) Preventive of vacancy (mani'at al-khuluw), which is that in which mutual exclusion between its two parts is judged only in falsehood, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown.' The first is called 'true' because the mutual exclusion between its two parts is stronger than the mutual exclusion between the two parts of the others, because it is in truth and falsehood together, so it is more deserving of the name 'disjunctive'; rather it is the true disjunction. The second is 'preventive of conjunction' because it contains prevention of conjunction between its two parts. The third is 'preventive of vacancy' because reality is not devoid of one of its two parts. Sometimes 'preventive of conjunction' and 'preventive of vacancy' are said of that in which mutual exclusion is judged in truth or falsehood absolutely, and in this sense they are more general.
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of disjunctive conditional propositions (al-munfasilah). The author begins by stating that the disjunctive is known to have three divisions. The first is the true disjunctive (haqiqiyyah), where the two parts exclude each other both in truth and falsehood, meaning they cannot both be true nor both false. The example given is 'Either this number is even or odd'—a number cannot be both even and odd, and it cannot be neither. The second is preventive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), where the two parts cannot both be true but can both be false. The example: 'Either this thing is a tree or a stone'—a thing cannot be both a tree and a stone, but it could be neither (e.g., water). The third is preventive of vacancy (mani'at al-khuluw), where the two parts cannot both be false but can both be true. The example: 'Either Zayd is in the sea or he does not drown'—Zayd cannot be both not in the sea and drowned, but he could be both in the sea and not drowned (if he is swimming). The author then explains the naming rationale: the first is called 'true' because the exclusion is stronger—it covers both truth and falsehood—so it is most deserving of the name 'disjunctive.' The second is called 'preventive of conjunction' because it prevents the conjunction (both true). The third is called 'preventive of vacancy' because reality (the actual situation) is never vacant of one of the two parts (i.e., one must be true). Finally, the author notes that sometimes the terms 'preventive of conjunction' and 'preventive of vacancy' are used in a broader sense to refer to any proposition where mutual exclusion is judged in truth or falsehood absolutely, not just in one respect. In that broader sense, they are more general categories.

حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunctive propositions are distinguished by which combinations of truth values are excluded: both (haqiqiyyah), only truth (mani'at al-jam'), or only falsehood (mani'at al-khuluw).

Refining the Meaning of 'Man' al-Jam'' in Disjunctives
Author refines the meaning of man' al-jam' to existential incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct interpretation of 'man' al-jam'' in disjunctive propositions and distinguish it from the misinterpretation based on truth on a single subject.

Core Idea
Man' al-jam' means existential incompatibility, not predicate incompatibility on a single subject.

A subtle ambiguity arises in understanding 'prevention of conjunction' (man' al-jam') in disjunctive propositions. Some scholars take it to mean that the two parts cannot both be true of a single subject. The author of our passage critically refines this, arguing that it actually means they cannot both exist in reality. This distinction has important consequences for logical analysis and is central to correctly classifying disjunctive propositions in Islamic logical tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting interpretations of man' al-jam'
One interpretation holds that man' al-jam' means the two disjuncts cannot both be true of a single subject (i.e., they cannot both be predicated of the same thing). The other, which the author defends, says it means the two disjuncts cannot coexist in existence (i.e., they cannot both obtain in the world).
2
Understand the objection to the existential interpretation
Critics argue that if man' al-jam' meant existential non-coexistence, then the one (واحد) and the many (كثير) would not be man' al-jam' because the one is part of the many and coexists with it in existence. Yet logicians like Shaykh al-Rais have stated man' al-jam' between them, creating a contradiction.
3
Follow the author's refutation: disjunctives are between propositions, not concepts
The author counters that disjunctive propositions (munfasila) are exclusively between two propositions, not between two concepts. If man' al-jam' meant non-truth on a single subject, then every pair of distinct propositions would be man' al-jam', which is absurd. Also, no pair would be man' al-khuluw (prevention of vacancy), which is clearly false.
The author emphasizes that the disjunction is existential: the two propositions cannot both be true in the world, not that they cannot both be predicated of one subject.
4
Examine the counterexample of 'one and many' and its reclassification
The example 'This is either one or many' appears to be a disjunctive but the author argues it is actually a categorical (hamliyya) proposition with a disjunctive predicate. Such statements are not true disjunctives in the logical sense; they are categoricals that resemble disjunctives.
This reclassification preserves the existential interpretation of man' al-jam' for genuine disjunctives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And there is for some of the distinguished scholars here a noble investigation: that what is meant by mutual exclusion in conjunction is that they are not both true of a single subject, not that they do not coexist in existence. For if what was meant was non-coexistence in existence, then there would not be prevention of conjunction between the one and the many, because the one is part of the many, and a part of a thing coexists with it in existence, but the master (Shaykh) stated prevention of conjunction between them. Then he said: 'And in my view there is consideration in this, for it would entail the permissibility of prevention of conjunction between the necessary (lazim) and the required (malzum), for a part of a thing is among its necessary concomitants. And they have agreed that there is no prevention of conjunction between the necessary and the required, nor prevention of vacancy.' And he hoped from Allah Almighty that He would open for him the answer to this objection. But it is only a consideration of what he intended from the expression of the people; far be it from them that they meant by mutual exclusion in conjunction non-coexistence in truth. For the preventive of conjunction is one of the divisions of the disjunctive, and they did not consider disjunction except between two propositions, so prevention of conjunction is only between two propositions. If what was meant was non-coexistence in truth, then between every two propositions there would be prevention of conjunction, because it is impossible for a proposition to be true of that of which another proposition is true; and there would not be between two propositions prevention of vacancy at all, necessarily because they are false of something – and the least of it is a singular individual. Rather, what they mean by mutual exclusion in conjunction is only non-coexistence in existence. (1) Meaning in truth and actuality, not in predication and truth on a single subject, and this is a statement without any doubt. It cannot be said: Mutual exclusion may occur between two concepts in truth on a single subject, as between the concepts of one and many. For we say: There is no dispute about that, but the proposition containing this mutual exclusion is not a disjunctive, but rather a categorical proposition resembling a disjunctive. So if you say: 'This is either one or...'
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the understanding of 'prevention of conjunction' in disjunctive propositions. A distinguished scholar (al-fadil) raised a noble investigation: is the mutual exclusion in mani'at al-jam' about not being true of a single subject (i.e., cannot both be predicated of the same thing) or about not coexisting in existence? He argued that the latter leads to problems: for example, the one and the many – the one is a part of the many, and a part coexists with the whole in existence, so there should be no prevention of conjunction between them, yet the master (Shaykh al-Rais?) did state prevention of conjunction between them. The author then notes that this scholar had a doubt and hoped for an answer from Allah. The author refutes this view: it far from the people (logicians) that they mean mutual exclusion in truth on a single subject. He explains: the disjunctive is only between two propositions, so prevention of conjunction is between two propositions. If it meant non-truth on a single subject, then all pairs of propositions would be preventive of conjunction, because it is impossible for one proposition to be true of exactly the same thing of which another proposition is true (unless they are identical). Also, there would be no prevention of vacancy at all, because any two propositions can be false of some subject. The author then asserts: what they mean is non-coexistence in existence (i.e., they cannot both exist in reality). He clarifies: this is about truth and actuality, not predication and truth on a single subject. He addresses a counterexample: mutual exclusion between concepts like one and many in truth on a single subject (a thing cannot be both one and many at the same time in the same respect). The author responds: that proposition is not a disjunctive but a categorical (hamliyyah) that resembles a disjunctive. For example, 'This is either one or many' is actually a categorical with a disjunctive predicate, not a true conditional disjunctive. The passage ends mid-example. The commentary emphasizes the technical precision: disjunctive propositions relate to propositions, not concepts, and their mutual exclusion is about existential compatibility, not predicate compatibility on a subject.

منافاة
مانعة الجمع
لازم
ملزوم

A) The two parts cannot both be true of a single subject

C) The two parts cannot both be false
D) The two parts are logically contradictory

Key Takeaway
In disjunctive propositions of the 'prevention of conjunction' type, the mutual exclusion is about existential incompatibility, not about truth on a single subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the following disjunctive proposition: 'Either this animal is a horse or it is a cow.' According to the author's refined understanding of man' al-jam', does this proposition express prevention of conjunction? Explain why or why not, referencing the existential criterion.
?
Task 2: Identify a proposition from a fiqh text that appears to be a disjunctive but is actually a categorical with a disjunctive predicate. Reclassify it and explain why it is not a true disjunctive.

Exclusive Disjunction: Existence vs. Truth
Commentator refines exclusive disjunction to existence-based interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between exclusive disjunction in terms of existence (wujud) and truth (sidq), and explain why the commentator insists on the existence-based interpretation.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction is about existence, not truth; otherwise, all propositions would be exclusive.

The commentator clarifies a key refinement: exclusive disjunction (manf' al-jam') in genuine disjunctive propositions concerns non-existence together in existence, not non-existence together in truth. This distinction resolves an apparent contradiction in the Shaykh's example of 'one and many'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible interpretations
The passage presents two interpretations of exclusive disjunction: non-existence together in existence (wujud) and non-existence together in truth (sidq). The commentator explicitly states that the intended meaning is the former, not the latter.
2
Recognize the absurdity of the truth-based interpretation
If exclusive disjunction were about truth, then between any two propositions there would be exclusive disjunction, because it is impossible for a proposition to be true of what another proposition is true of. This would also render exclusive union (manf' al-khuluw) impossible, which is contrary to unanimous agreement.
3
Apply to the example of 'one and many'
The Shaykh asserted exclusive disjunction between 'one' and 'many', but they can coexist in existence (since one is part of many). The commentator resolves this by explaining that if the disjunction is interpreted in terms of truth (i.e., the propositions 'this is one' and 'this is many' cannot both be true), then the incompatibility is in truth, not existence. The genuine disjunctive condition is about existence, so the example is actually a categorical resembling a disjunctive if the incompatibility is between concepts, or a genuine disjunctive if between propositions.
This step shows how the distinction resolves apparent contradictions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية
المراد عدم الاجتماع في الوجود لم يكن بين الواحد والكثير منع الجمع لأن الواحد جزء الكثير، وجزء الشيء يجامعه في الوجود، لكن الشيخ نص على منع الجمع بينهما.
ثم قال : : وعندي في هذا نظر، إذ يلزم من ذلك جواز منع الجمع بين اللازم والملزوم فإن جزء الشيء من لوازمه. وقد أجمعوا على أنه لا منع جمع بين اللازم والملزوم ولامنع خلو»، ورجى من الله تعالى أن يفتح عليه الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض.
وهو ليس إلا نظراً فيما أراده من عبارة القوم، فحاشاهم أن يعنوا بالمنافاة في الجمع عدم الاجتماع في الصدق، فإن مانعة الجمع من أقسام المنفصلة، والانفصال لم يعتبروه إلا بين القضيتين، فلايكون منع الجمع إلا بين القضيّتين، فلو كان المراد عدم الاجتماع في الصدق لكان بين كلّ قضيتين منع الجمع، لاستحالة أن تصدق قضيّة على ماصدق عليه قضية أخرى ؛ ولايكون بين قضيتين منع الخلو أصلاً، ضرورة كذبهما على شيء من الأشياء - وأقله مفرد من المفردات
بل ليس مرادهم بالمنافاة في الجمع إلا عدم الاجتماع في الوجود. و
لا يقال : قدتكون المنافاة بين المفهومين في الصدق على ذات واحدة كما بين مفهومي الواحد والكثير. لأنا نقول: لانزاع في ذلك إلا أنّ القضية المشتملة على هذه المنافاة ليست بمنفصلة، بل هي حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة. فإذا قلت : «هذا إما واحد وإما كثير» فإن أردت المنافاة بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير، فالقضية منفصلة مركبة من قضيتين، منع الجمع باعتبار الصدق والتحقق بين القضيتين كما قرره؛ وإن أردت المنافاة بين مفهومي «الواحد» و«الكثير» في الصدق والحمل على هذا فالقضية حملية مركبة من موضوع واحد، إلا أنه قد ردّد في محمولها فصارت شبيهة بالمنفصلة.
فالشارح لم يقل بأن لا منع جمع في الصدق على ذات واحدة، بل قال : منع الجمع المعتبر في المنفصلات إنما هو بحسب الوجود لا الحمل، وقد يكون بين مفهومين منافاة في الوجود في محل كالسواد والبياض، فإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون السواد موجوداً في هذا المحل أو يكون البياض موجوداً فيه كانت القضية منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «الموجود في هذا المحل إما سواد وإما بياض كانت القضية حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة؛ وبالجملة كما أن الحملية قد تشارك المتصلة فيما هو حاصل المعنى ومآله – كقولك : «طلوع الشمس ملزوم لوجود النهار» ولابد أن تكون مخالفة لها في صريح المفهوم منها كذلك الحملية قد تشارك المنفصلة في محصول المعنى ومآله، وإن كان المفهوم والصريح متخالفاً فيهما، والمنافاة قد تعتبر في القضايا بحسب الصدق والتحقق وهي المنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب صدقها على ذات واحدة وهي الحمليات الشبيهة بالمنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب الوجود في محل واحد، فإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «السواد والبياض متنافيان بحسب الوجود في محل واحد فهذه حمليّة صرفة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء أسود وإما أن يكون أبيض» فهذه منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «هذا الشيء إمّا أسود وإما أبيض»، فهذه حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة، والكل متشاركة في مآل المعنى ومحصوله وإن كانت متخالفه في المفهوم الصريح (شريف).
Sections of conditional propositions. The intended meaning is non-existence together in existence, then there is no exclusive disjunction between the one and the many because the one is part of the many, and the part of a thing coexists with it in existence, but the Shaykh explicitly stated exclusive disjunction between them. Then he said: In my view there is a consideration in this, for it follows from that the permissibility of exclusive disjunction between the necessary and the necessary condition, for the part of a thing is from its necessary conditions. And they have unanimously agreed that there is no exclusive disjunction between the necessary and the necessary condition, nor exclusive union, and I hope from Allah Almighty that He opens for me the answer to this objection. And it is nothing but a consideration in what he intended from the expression of the people, for far be it from them that they mean by incompatibility in conjunction non-existence together in truth, for exclusive disjunction is from the divisions of the disjunctive, and disjunction they did not consider except between two propositions, so exclusive disjunction is only between two propositions. So if the intended meaning was non-existence together in truth, then between every two propositions there would be exclusive disjunction, because it is impossible for a proposition to be true of what another proposition is true of; and there would be no exclusive union between two propositions at all, necessarily because of their falsehood about something among things - and the least of them is a singular among singulars. Rather their intended meaning by incompatibility in conjunction is only non-existence together in existence. And
t it cannot be said: There may be incompatibility between two concepts in truth about a single essence, as between the concepts of one and many. For we say: There is no dispute about that, but the proposition containing this incompatibility is not a disjunctive, rather it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. So if you say: 'This is either one or many' - if you intend the incompatibility between 'this is one' and 'this is many', then the proposition is a disjunctive composed of two propositions, exclusive disjunction in terms of truth and actuality between the two propositions as he established; and if you intend the incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in truth and predication about this, then the proposition is a categorical composed of a single subject, except that its predicate is disjunctive, so it becomes resembling a disjunctive. So the commentator did not say that there is no exclusive disjunction in truth about a single essence; rather he said: The exclusive disjunction considered in disjunctives is only in terms of existence, not predication. And there may be between two concepts incompatibility in existence in a locus, like blackness and whiteness. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it', then the proposition is disjunctive. And if you express them by something like your saying: 'What exists in this locus is either blackness or whiteness', then the proposition is categorical resembling a disjunctive. In summary, just as the categorical may share with the conditional in terms of the resultant meaning and outcome - like your saying: 'Sunrise necessitates the existence of daytime' - and it must be different from it in the explicit concept, likewise the categorical may share with the disjunctive in the resultant meaning and outcome, although the concept and explicit wording differ in them. Incompatibility may be considered in propositions in terms of truth and actuality - these are the disjunctives; and it may be considered in terms of singulars in terms of their truth about a single essence - these are the categoricals resembling disjunctives; and it may be considered in terms of singulars in terms of existence in a single locus. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Blackness and whiteness are incompatible in terms of existence in a single locus', then this is a pure categorical. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Either this thing is black or it is white', then this is a disjunctive. If you express them by something like your saying: 'This thing is either black or white', then this is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. All share in the outcome of meaning and result, although they differ in explicit concept. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the nature of exclusive disjunction (manf' al-jam') in conditional propositions. The commentator begins by addressing an objection: if exclusive disjunction means 'non-existence together in existence', then there would be no exclusive disjunction between 'one' and 'many' because the one is part of the many and coexists with it in existence. However, the Shaykh (likely the author of the matn) asserted exclusive disjunction between them. The commentator then explains that his own consideration of this leads to a problem: if the Shaykh's view is taken, it would allow exclusive disjunction between a necessary condition (lazim) and its necessitator (malzum), which the consensus rejects. But the commentator resolves this by clarifying that the intended meaning of exclusive disjunction in genuine disjunctives is not about truth (sidq) but about existence (wujud). If it were about truth, then any two propositions would be mutually exclusive in truth (impossible to both be true of the same thing), which is absurd. Instead, genuine disjunctives require the incompatibility to be in terms of existence, not predication. The commentator then discusses the example of 'one and many'. If one says 'This is either one or many', the interpretation depends on intent: if the intent is an incompatibility between two propositions ('this is one' and 'this is many') then it is a genuine disjunctive; if the intent is an incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in being true of the same subject, then it is a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, resembling a disjunctive. The passage goes on to illustrate with the example of blackness and whiteness existing in a single locus. If expressed as a condition ('Either blackness exists...'), it is a disjunctive. If expressed as a categorical ('What exists... is either black or white'), it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. Finally, the commentator summarizes three levels of incompatibility: (1) truth and actuality between propositions (genuine disjunctives), (2) truth of singulars about one essence (categoricals resembling disjunctives), (3) existence in a single locus (can be expressed as pure categorical, disjunctive, or categorical resembling disjunctive). The key point is that the explicit wording differs but the underlying meaning may be shared.

D) Incompatibility in concept (mafhum)

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunction is about existence, not truth; this distinction is essential for understanding disjunctive propositions.

Genuine Disjunctives vs. Categoricals Resembling Disjunctives
Commentator distinguishes genuine disjunctives from categoricals resembling them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a genuine disjunctive proposition and a categorical proposition resembling a disjunctive, based on whether the incompatibility is between propositions or between concepts.

Core Idea
A genuine disjunctive has two propositions; a resembling categorical has one subject and a disjunctive predicate.

The commentator clarifies that the same surface expression can be either a genuine disjunctive or a categorical resembling a disjunctive, depending on the intended locus of incompatibility. This lesson uses the examples of 'one and many' and 'blackness and whiteness' to illustrate the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
The passage contrasts a genuine disjunctive (munfasila), which is composed of two separate propositions with incompatibility in existence, and a categorical resembling a disjunctive (hamliyya shabiha bi al-munfasila), which has a single subject and a disjunctive predicate, with incompatibility between the concepts/predicates.
2
Analyze the 'one and many' example
The commentator explains that 'This is either one or many' can be interpreted in two ways: (a) as a genuine disjunctive if the incompatibility is between the two propositions 'this is one' and 'this is many'; (b) as a categorical resembling a disjunctive if the incompatibility is between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in being true of the same subject.
3
Analyze the blackness/whiteness example
Expressions like 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it' is a genuine disjunctive. 'What exists in this locus is either black or white' is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. The underlying incompatibility is the same (black and white cannot coexist in the same locus), but the logical form differs.
4
Generalize the distinction
The commentator summarizes that both types share the same resultant meaning (mahsul al-ma'na) but differ in explicit concept (mafhum sar'ih). This is parallel to categoricals that share meaning with conditionals.

The passage goes on to illustrate with the example of blackness and whiteness existing in a single locus. If expressed as a condition ('Either blackness exists...'), it is a disjunctive. If expressed as a categorical ('What exists... is either black or white'), it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. Finally, the commentator summarizes three levels of incompatibility: (1) truth and actuality between propositions (genuine disjunctives), (2) truth of singulars about one essence (categoricals resembling disjunctives), (3) existence in a single locus (can be expressed as pure categorical, disjunctive, or categorical resembling disjunctive). The key point is that the explicit wording differs but the underlying meaning may be shared.

Three Levels of Incompatibility in Propositions
Commentator summarizes three levels of incompatibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three levels of incompatibility discussed by the commentator: between propositions, between concepts in truth about one essence, and between concepts in existence in one locus.

Core Idea
Incompatibility operates at three levels, each yielding different logical proposition types.

The commentator provides a comprehensive classification of incompatibility (tanafi) across three levels. This lesson maps those levels to the proposition types that express them, helping you recognize the logical form behind various expressions of conflict or mutual exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Level 1: Incompatibility in propositions
This is the level of genuine disjunctives (munfasila). The incompatibility is between two propositions regarding their truth and actuality: they cannot both be true. The form is a disjunctive proposition composed of two propositions, e.g., 'Either Zayd is in the house or he is in the market.'
2
Level 2: Incompatibility in singulars about one essence
Two concepts cannot both be truly predicated of a single subject. This yields a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, resembling a disjunctive. Example: 'This is either one or many' (if the incompatibility is between the concepts).
3
Level 3: Incompatibility in existence in one locus
Two entities cannot coexist in the same location. This can be expressed in three ways: as a pure categorical ('Blackness and whiteness are incompatible in existence in one locus'), as a genuine disjunctive ('Either blackness exists or whiteness exists in this locus'), or as a categorical resembling a disjunctive ('What exists in this locus is either black or white').
4
Recognize the shared meaning
Despite the different explicit forms (mafhum sar'ih), all expressions for level 3 share the same resultant meaning (mahsul al-ma'na). The commentator emphasizes that this is analogous to categoricals that share meaning with conditionals.

المراد عدم الاجتماع في الوجود لم يكن بين الواحد والكثير منع الجمع لأن الواحد جزء الكثير، وجزء الشيء يجامعه في الوجود، لكن الشيخ نص على منع الجمع بينهما.
ثم قال : : وعندي في هذا نظر، إذ يلزم من ذلك جواز منع الجمع بين اللازم والملزوم فإن جزء الشيء من لوازمه. وقد أجمعوا على أنه لا منع جمع بين اللازم والملزوم ولامنع خلو»، ورجى من الله تعالى أن يفتح عليه الجواب عن هذا الاعتراض.
وهو ليس إلا نظراً فيما أراده من عبارة القوم، فحاشاهم أن يعنوا بالمنافاة في الجمع عدم الاجتماع في الصدق، فإن مانعة الجمع من أقسام المنفصلة، والانفصال لم يعتبروه إلا بين القضيتين، فلايكون منع الجمع إلا بين القضيّتين، فلو كان المراد عدم الاجتماع في الصدق لكان بين كلّ قضيتين منع الجمع، لاستحالة أن تصدق قضيّة على ماصدق عليه قضية أخرى ؛ ولايكون بين قضيتين منع الخلو أصلاً، ضرورة كذبهما على شيء من الأشياء - وأقله مفرد من المفردات
بل ليس مرادهم بالمنافاة في الجمع إلا عدم الاجتماع في الوجود. و
لا يقال : قدتكون المنافاة بين المفهومين في الصدق على ذات واحدة كما بين مفهومي الواحد والكثير. لأنا نقول: لانزاع في ذلك إلا أنّ القضية المشتملة على هذه المنافاة ليست بمنفصلة، بل هي حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة. فإذا قلت : «هذا إما واحد وإما كثير» فإن أردت المنافاة بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير، فالقضية منفصلة مركبة من قضيتين، منع الجمع باعتبار الصدق والتحقق بين القضيتين كما قرره؛ وإن أردت المنافاة بين مفهومي «الواحد» و«الكثير» في الصدق والحمل على هذا فالقضية حملية مركبة من موضوع واحد، إلا أنه قد ردّد في محمولها فصارت شبيهة بالمنفصلة.
فالشارح لم يقل بأن لا منع جمع في الصدق على ذات واحدة، بل قال : منع الجمع المعتبر في المنفصلات إنما هو بحسب الوجود لا الحمل، وقد يكون بين مفهومين منافاة في الوجود في محل كالسواد والبياض، فإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون السواد موجوداً في هذا المحل أو يكون البياض موجوداً فيه كانت القضية منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «الموجود في هذا المحل إما سواد وإما بياض كانت القضية حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة؛ وبالجملة كما أن الحملية قد تشارك المتصلة فيما هو حاصل المعنى ومآله – كقولك : «طلوع الشمس ملزوم لوجود النهار» ولابد أن تكون مخالفة لها في صريح المفهوم منها كذلك الحملية قد تشارك المنفصلة في محصول المعنى ومآله، وإن كان المفهوم والصريح متخالفاً فيهما، والمنافاة قد تعتبر في القضايا بحسب الصدق والتحقق وهي المنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب صدقها على ذات واحدة وهي الحمليات الشبيهة بالمنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب الوجود في محل واحد، فإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «السواد والبياض متنافيان بحسب الوجود في محل واحد فهذه حمليّة صرفة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء أسود وإما أن يكون أبيض» فهذه منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «هذا الشيء إمّا أسود وإما أبيض»، فهذه حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة، والكل متشاركة في مآل المعنى ومحصوله وإن كانت متخالفه في المفهوم الصريح (شريف).
Sections of conditional propositions. The intended meaning is non-existence together in existence, then there is no exclusive disjunction between the one and the many because the one is part of the many, and the part of a thing coexists with it in existence, but the Shaykh explicitly stated exclusive disjunction between them. Then he said: In my view there is a consideration in this, for it follows from that the permissibility of exclusive disjunction between the necessary and the necessary condition, for the part of a thing is from its necessary conditions. And they have unanimously agreed that there is no exclusive disjunction between the necessary and the necessary condition, nor exclusive union, and I hope from Allah Almighty that He opens for me the answer to this objection. And it is nothing but a consideration in what he intended from the expression of the people, for far be it from them that they mean by incompatibility in conjunction non-existence together in truth, for exclusive disjunction is from the divisions of the disjunctive, and disjunction they did not consider except between two propositions, so exclusive disjunction is only between two propositions. So if the intended meaning was non-existence together in truth, then between every two propositions there would be exclusive disjunction, because it is impossible for a proposition to be true of what another proposition is true of; and there would be no exclusive union between two propositions at all, necessarily because of their falsehood about something among things - and the least of them is a singular among singulars. Rather their intended meaning by incompatibility in conjunction is only non-existence together in existence. And
t it cannot be said: There may be incompatibility between two concepts in truth about a single essence, as between the concepts of one and many. For we say: There is no dispute about that, but the proposition containing this incompatibility is not a disjunctive, rather it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. So if you say: 'This is either one or many' - if you intend the incompatibility between 'this is one' and 'this is many', then the proposition is a disjunctive composed of two propositions, exclusive disjunction in terms of truth and actuality between the two propositions as he established; and if you intend the incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in truth and predication about this, then the proposition is a categorical composed of a single subject, except that its predicate is disjunctive, so it becomes resembling a disjunctive. So the commentator did not say that there is no exclusive disjunction in truth about a single essence; rather he said: The exclusive disjunction considered in disjunctives is only in terms of existence, not predication. And there may be between two concepts incompatibility in existence in a locus, like blackness and whiteness. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it', then the proposition is disjunctive. And if you express them by something like your saying: 'What exists in this locus is either blackness or whiteness', then the proposition is categorical resembling a disjunctive. In summary, just as the categorical may share with the conditional in terms of the resultant meaning and outcome - like your saying: 'Sunrise necessitates the existence of daytime' - and it must be different from it in the explicit concept, likewise the categorical may share with the disjunctive in the resultant meaning and outcome, although the concept and explicit wording differ in them. Incompatibility may be considered in propositions in terms of truth and actuality - these are the disjunctives; and it may be considered in terms of singulars in terms of their truth about a single essence - these are the categoricals resembling disjunctives; and it may be considered in terms of singulars in terms of existence in a single locus. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Blackness and whiteness are incompatible in terms of existence in a single locus', then this is a pure categorical. If you express them by something like your saying: 'Either this thing is black or it is white', then this is a disjunctive. If you express them by something like your saying: 'This thing is either black or white', then this is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. All share in the outcome of meaning and result, although they differ in explicit concept. (Sharif).
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on the nature of exclusive disjunction (manf' al-jam') in conditional propositions. The commentator begins by addressing an objection: if exclusive disjunction means 'non-existence together in existence', then there would be no exclusive disjunction between 'one' and 'many' because the one is part of the many and coexists with it in existence. However, the Shaykh (likely the author of the matn) asserted exclusive disjunction between them. The commentator then explains that his own consideration of this leads to a problem: if the Shaykh's view is taken, it would allow exclusive disjunction between a necessary condition (lazim) and its necessitator (malzum), which the consensus rejects. But the commentator resolves this by clarifying that the intended meaning of exclusive disjunction in genuine disjunctives is not about truth (sidq) but about existence (wujud). If it were about truth, then any two propositions would be mutually exclusive in truth (impossible to both be true of the same thing), which is absurd. Instead, genuine disjunctives require the incompatibility to be in terms of existence, not predication. The commentator then discusses the example of 'one and many'. If one says 'This is either one or many', the interpretation depends on intent: if the intent is an incompatibility between two propositions ('this is one' and 'this is many') then it is a genuine disjunctive; if the intent is an incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in being true of the same subject, then it is a categorical proposition with a disjunctive predicate, resembling a disjunctive. The passage goes on to illustrate with the example of blackness and whiteness existing in a single locus. If expressed as a condition ('Either blackness exists...'), it is a disjunctive. If expressed as a categorical ('What exists... is either black or white'), it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. Finally, the commentator summarizes three levels of incompatibility: (1) truth and actuality between propositions (genuine disjunctives), (2) truth of singulars about one essence (categoricals resembling disjunctives), (3) existence in a single locus (can be expressed as pure categorical, disjunctive, or categorical resembling disjunctive). The key point is that the explicit wording differs but the underlying meaning may be shared.

Genuine Disjunctives vs. Categoricals with Disjunctive Predicates
Author clarifies the logical difference between disjunctive propositions and categorical propositions with disjunctive meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a genuine disjunctive proposition (munfasilah) and a categorical proposition that resembles a disjunctive (hamliyyah shabihah bi al-munfasilah) by examining whether the incompatibility is between two propositions or between predicates of a single subject.

Core Idea
A genuine disjunctive has two separate propositions; a categorical with disjunctive predicate only resembles one.

You have likely encountered statements like 'Zaid is either in the house or in the mosque' and wondered whether they are disjunctive or categorical. This lesson clarifies the logical difference: genuine disjunctives consist of two independent propositions, while categoricals with a disjunctive predicate share the same meaning but differ in form. We will use the commentary's analysis of blackness and whiteness to solidify the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical form
Determine whether the statement has a single subject or two subjects. For example, 'Either blackness exists or whiteness exists' has two subjects (blackness, whiteness) and two predicates (exists, exists) – it is a genuine disjunctive. 'The existing thing is either blackness or whiteness' has one subject (the existing thing) and a disjunctive predicate – it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive.
2
Understand the incompatibility
In the genuine disjunctive, the incompatibility (man' al-jam') is between the truth of two propositions: they cannot both be true. In the categorical resembling a disjunctive, the incompatibility is between two predicates applying to the same subject: the subject cannot simultaneously be both black and white. This is an incompatibility in predication.
3
Test with an Islamic example
Consider the statement 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden'. It has a single subject ('this act') and two predicates. Therefore it is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. In contrast, 'Either prayer is obligatory or prayer is not obligatory' has two propositions and is a genuine disjunctive (though tautological).
The distinction affects how we reason: genuine disjunctives support disjunctive syllogisms, while categoricals with disjunctive predicates do not directly.

أما الشيخ فأثبت بين الواحد والكثير منع الجمع، فهو ليس بين مفهومي كثير فإن أردت المنافاة بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير، فالقضية منفصلة مركبة من قضيتين، منع الجمع باعتبار الصدق والتحقق بين القضيتين كما قرره؛ وإن أردت المنافاة بين مفهومي «الواحد» و«الكثير» في الصدق والحمل على هذا فالقضية حملية مركبة من موضوع واحد، إلا أنه قد ردّد في محمولها فصارت شبيهة بالمنفصلة. فالشارح لم يقل بأن لا منع جمع في الصدق على ذات واحدة، بل قال : منع الجمع المعتبر في المنفصلات إنما هو بحسب الوجود لا الحمل، وقد يكون بين مفهومين منافاة في الوجود في محل كالسواد والبياض، فإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون السواد موجوداً في هذا المحل أو يكون البياض موجوداً فيه كانت القضية منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «الموجود في هذا المحل إما سواد وإما بياض كانت القضية حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة؛ وبالجملة كما أن الحملية قد تشارك المتصلة فيما هو حاصل المعنى ومآله – كقولك : «طلوع الشمس ملزوم لوجود النهار» ولابد أن تكون مخالفة لها في صريح المفهوم منها كذلك الحملية قد تشارك المنفصلة في محصول المعنى ومآله، وإن كان المفهوم والصريح متخالفاً فيهما، والمنافاة قد تعتبر في القضايا بحسب الصدق والتحقق وهي المنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب صدقها على ذات واحدة وهي الحمليات الشبيهة بالمنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب الوجود في محل واحد، فإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «السواد والبياض متنافيان بحسب الوجود في محل واحد فهذه حمليّة صرفة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء أسود وإما أن يكون أبيض» فهذه منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «هذا الشيء إمّا أسود وإما أبيض»، فهذه حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة، والكل متشاركة في مآل المعنى ومحصوله وإن كانت متخالفه في المفهوم الصريح (شريف).
As for the Shaykh, he established between the one and the many a prevention of conjunction (man' al-jam'), so it is not between the two concepts of 'many'. For if you intend the incompatibility between 'this is one' and 'this is many', then the proposition is a disjunctive composed of two propositions, prevention of conjunction with respect to truth and verification between the two propositions as he determined. And if you intend the incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in truth and predication upon this, then the proposition is a categorical composed of a single subject, except that its predicate has been made disjunctive, so it becomes resembling a disjunctive. So the commentator did not say that there is no prevention of conjunction in truth upon one essence, but rather he said: the prevention of conjunction considered in disjunctives is only according to existence, not predication. And sometimes there is between two concepts incompatibility in existence in a locus, like blackness and whiteness. If you express them like your saying: 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it', the proposition is a disjunctive. And if you express them like your saying: 'That which exists in this locus is either blackness or whiteness', the proposition is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. And in summary, just as the categorical may share with the conditional in what is the core meaning and outcome – like your saying: 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of the existence of daytime' and it must be different from it in the explicit concept – likewise the categorical may share with the disjunctive in the core meaning and outcome, even if the concept and explicit wording differ in them. And incompatibility may be considered in propositions according to truth and verification – these are the disjunctives; and it may be considered in simple terms according to their truth upon a single essence – these are the categoricals resembling disjunctives; and it may be considered in simple terms according to existence in a single locus. For if you express them like your saying: 'Blackness and whiteness are incompatible according to existence in a single locus', this is a pure categorical. And if you express them like your saying: 'Either this thing is black or it is white', this is a disjunctive. And if you express them like your saying: 'This thing is either black or white', this is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. And all share in the ultimate meaning and core content even though they differ in explicit concept. (al-Sharif)
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies a logical distinction between genuine disjunctive propositions (munfasilah) and categorical propositions (hamliyyah) that have a disjunctive predicate, making them resemble disjunctives (shabihah bi al-munfasilah). The discussion begins with the Shaykh's point about the relation between 'one' and 'many'. The author explains that if we express the incompatibility as 'Either this is one or this is many', that is a genuine disjunctive (man'at al-jam') because it consists of two separate propositions that cannot both be true. On the other hand, if we express it as 'This is either one or many' with a single subject, it is a categorical with a disjunctive predicate; it only resembles a disjunctive. The commentator emphasizes that the key is whether the incompatibility is considered in terms of existence (al-wujud) or predication (al-haml). For an example, consider blackness and whiteness: the statement 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it' is a disjunctive (two propositions), while 'The existing thing in this locus is either blackness or whiteness' is a categorical with a compound predicate, similar to 'This thing is either black or white'. Both express the same meaning but differ in logical structure. The passage further notes that categorical propositions can share the core meaning (ma'al al-ma'na) with conditionals (muttasilah) as well, using the example 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of daytime' which is a categorical equivalent to a conditional. The overall lesson is that one must not confuse the logical form (explicit concept, al-mafhum al-sarih) with the ultimate meaning.

منع الجمع (man' al-jam')
مآل المعنى (ma'al al-ma'na)
