Incompatibility in Existence vs. Predication: The Key to Disjunctive Forms
Author clarifies the logical difference between disjunctive propositions and categorical propositions with disjunctive meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a statement expresses incompatibility in existence (existing in a locus) or incompatibility in predication (truth on a single subject), and classify the proposition accordingly.

Core Idea
Incompatibility can be considered in existence (genuine disjunctive) or in predication (categorical resembling disjunctive).

This lesson dives deeper into the commentary's explanation of man' al-jam'. The passage explains that the Shaykh's prevention of conjunction (incompatibility) can be considered in two ways: existence in a locus, or predication on a single subject. This determines whether the expression is a genuine disjunctive, a pure categorical, or something in between. We will trace these distinctions using the example of blackness and whiteness.

Guided Path
1
Identify the locus of incompatibility
Ask: In what respect are the two things incompatible? If they cannot both exist in the same locus (e.g., blackness and whiteness cannot coexist in the same spot), that is incompatibility in existence. If they cannot both be truly predicated of the same subject (e.g., a thing cannot be both black and white simultaneously), that is incompatibility in predication.
2
Express incompatibility in existence as a disjunctive
If you say 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it', you are stating a genuine disjunctive because you are talking about the existence of two separate entities. The incompatibility is between the two propositions.
This is a man'at al-jam' disjunctive.
3
Express incompatibility in predication as a categorical with disjunctive predicate
If you say 'This thing is either black or white', you are predicating a disjunctive property of a single subject. The incompatibility is between the two predicates in relation to that subject. This is a categorical resembling a disjunctive.
If you simply say 'Blackness and whiteness are incompatible in existence', that is a pure categorical (hamliyyah sarihah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الشيخ فأثبت بين الواحد والكثير منع الجمع، فهو ليس بين مفهومي كثير فإن أردت المنافاة بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير، فالقضية منفصلة مركبة من قضيتين، منع الجمع باعتبار الصدق والتحقق بين القضيتين كما قرره؛ وإن أردت المنافاة بين مفهومي «الواحد» و«الكثير» في الصدق والحمل على هذا فالقضية حملية مركبة من موضوع واحد، إلا أنه قد ردّد في محمولها فصارت شبيهة بالمنفصلة. فالشارح لم يقل بأن لا منع جمع في الصدق على ذات واحدة، بل قال : منع الجمع المعتبر في المنفصلات إنما هو بحسب الوجود لا الحمل، وقد يكون بين مفهومين منافاة في الوجود في محل كالسواد والبياض، فإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون السواد موجوداً في هذا المحل أو يكون البياض موجوداً فيه كانت القضية منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنهما بمثل قولك : «الموجود في هذا المحل إما سواد وإما بياض كانت القضية حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة؛ وبالجملة كما أن الحملية قد تشارك المتصلة فيما هو حاصل المعنى ومآله – كقولك : «طلوع الشمس ملزوم لوجود النهار» ولابد أن تكون مخالفة لها في صريح المفهوم منها كذلك الحملية قد تشارك المنفصلة في محصول المعنى ومآله، وإن كان المفهوم والصريح متخالفاً فيهما، والمنافاة قد تعتبر في القضايا بحسب الصدق والتحقق وهي المنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب صدقها على ذات واحدة وهي الحمليات الشبيهة بالمنفصلات، وقد تعتبر في المفردات بحسب الوجود في محل واحد، فإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «السواد والبياض متنافيان بحسب الوجود في محل واحد فهذه حمليّة صرفة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «إما أن يكون هذا الشيء أسود وإما أن يكون أبيض» فهذه منفصلة، وإن عبرت عنها بمثل قولك : «هذا الشيء إمّا أسود وإما أبيض»، فهذه حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة، والكل متشاركة في مآل المعنى ومحصوله وإن كانت متخالفه في المفهوم الصريح (شريف).
As for the Shaykh, he established between the one and the many a prevention of conjunction (man' al-jam'), so it is not between the two concepts of 'many'. For if you intend the incompatibility between 'this is one' and 'this is many', then the proposition is a disjunctive composed of two propositions, prevention of conjunction with respect to truth and verification between the two propositions as he determined. And if you intend the incompatibility between the concepts of 'one' and 'many' in truth and predication upon this, then the proposition is a categorical composed of a single subject, except that its predicate has been made disjunctive, so it becomes resembling a disjunctive. So the commentator did not say that there is no prevention of conjunction in truth upon one essence, but rather he said: the prevention of conjunction considered in disjunctives is only according to existence, not predication. And sometimes there is between two concepts incompatibility in existence in a locus, like blackness and whiteness. If you express them like your saying: 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it', the proposition is a disjunctive. And if you express them like your saying: 'That which exists in this locus is either blackness or whiteness', the proposition is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. And in summary, just as the categorical may share with the conditional in what is the core meaning and outcome – like your saying: 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of the existence of daytime' and it must be different from it in the explicit concept – likewise the categorical may share with the disjunctive in the core meaning and outcome, even if the concept and explicit wording differ in them. And incompatibility may be considered in propositions according to truth and verification – these are the disjunctives; and it may be considered in simple terms according to their truth upon a single essence – these are the categoricals resembling disjunctives; and it may be considered in simple terms according to existence in a single locus. For if you express them like your saying: 'Blackness and whiteness are incompatible according to existence in a single locus', this is a pure categorical. And if you express them like your saying: 'Either this thing is black or it is white', this is a disjunctive. And if you express them like your saying: 'This thing is either black or white', this is a categorical resembling a disjunctive. And all share in the ultimate meaning and core content even though they differ in explicit concept. (al-Sharif)
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a commentary that clarifies a logical distinction between genuine disjunctive propositions (munfasilah) and categorical propositions (hamliyyah) that have a disjunctive predicate, making them resemble disjunctives (shabihah bi al-munfasilah). The discussion begins with the Shaykh's point about the relation between 'one' and 'many'. The author explains that if we express the incompatibility as 'Either this is one or this is many', that is a genuine disjunctive (man'at al-jam') because it consists of two separate propositions that cannot both be true. On the other hand, if we express it as 'This is either one or many' with a single subject, it is a categorical with a disjunctive predicate; it only resembles a disjunctive. The commentator emphasizes that the key is whether the incompatibility is considered in terms of existence (al-wujud) or predication (al-haml). For an example, consider blackness and whiteness: the statement 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it' is a disjunctive (two propositions), while 'The existing thing in this locus is either blackness or whiteness' is a categorical with a compound predicate, similar to 'This thing is either black or white'. Both express the same meaning but differ in logical structure. The passage further notes that categorical propositions can share the core meaning (ma'al al-ma'na) with conditionals (muttasilah) as well, using the example 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of daytime' which is a categorical equivalent to a conditional. The overall lesson is that one must not confuse the logical form (explicit concept, al-mafhum al-sarih) with the ultimate meaning.

Key Terms
منفصلة (munfasilah)
حملية شبيهة بالمنفصلة (hamliyyah shabihah bi al-munfasilah)
منع الجمع (man' al-jam')
مآل المعنى (ma'al al-ma'na)

Checkpoint
The passage says: 'the prevention of conjunction considered in disjunctives is only according to existence, not predication.' Which of the following best explains this?
A) A genuine disjunctive proposition always expresses incompatibility in the existence of two things, not in their predication.
B) A genuine disjunctive proposition expresses incompatibility in predication, not existence.
C) The prevention of conjunction is irrelevant to disjunctives.
D) All statements with 'either...or' express predication incompatibility.

Key Takeaway
The same incompatibility can be expressed either as a genuine disjunctive (in existence) or as a categorical with disjunctive predicate (in predication), but the logical form differs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Propositions

10 min
Shared Ultimate Meaning Across Logical Forms: Ma'al al-Ma'na
Author clarifies the logical difference between disjunctive propositions and categorical propositions with disjunctive meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when different propositional forms (conditional, disjunctive, categorical) share the same core meaning (ma'al al-ma'na) despite differences in explicit structure.

Core Idea
Categorical, conditional, and disjunctive forms can have identical ultimate meaning while differing in explicit linguistic structure.

The commentator introduces a powerful concept: ma'al al-ma'na (ultimate meaning/core content). He notes that a categorical proposition like 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of daytime' shares the same meaning as the conditional 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists'. Similarly, categorical propositions with disjunctive predicates share meaning with genuine disjunctives. This lesson explores how logical form is not identical to semantic content, and why this matters for reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of ma'al al-ma'na
Ma'al al-ma'na is the core propositional content, stripped of its grammatical form. For example, 'A implies B' can be expressed as a conditional (If A then B) or as a categorical (A is an indicator of B). Both have the same ultimate meaning.
2
Analyze the example of conditional and categorical equivalence
The commentator gives the example: 'Sunrise is a necessary concomitant of the existence of daytime' is a categorical, but it means the same as 'If the sun rises, then daytime exists' (conditional). Recognize that despite different structures, they convey the same logical relation.
3
Apply to disjunctive forms
Similarly, 'This thing is either black or white' (categorical resembling disjunctive) shares ultimate meaning with 'Either blackness exists in this locus or whiteness exists in it' (genuine disjunctive). The difference is in explicit concept (mafhum sarih), not in the core logical content.
This shows that one should not be misled by surface form when reasoning.

Essential vs. Accidental Disjunctive Propositions
Classifies disjunctive propositions into essential and accidental opposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given disjunctive proposition as 'anadiyyah (essential opposition) or ittifaqiyyah (accidental opposition) based on the source of incompatibility.

Core Idea
The incompatibility in disjunctives can be essential or accidental depending on the concepts themselves.

In this lesson, we explore the division of disjunctive propositions into two types: 'anadiyyah (essential opposition) and ittifaqiyyah (accidental opposition). The key distinction is whether the incompatibility between the two parts stems from their very natures (dhat) or is merely coincidental in reality. This classification parallels that of conditionals into luzumiyyah (necessary consequence) and ittifaqiyyah (accidental). Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to evaluate the logical foundation of disjunctive arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types and their Arabic terms
Find the terms 'anadiyyah and ittifaqiyyah in the Arabic passage. Note the phrases 'li-dhatay al-juz'ayn' (due to the essences of the two parts) and 'bi-mujarrad al-ittifaq' (merely by coincidence). These are the criteria for classification.
The same terms appear in conditionals, but here they apply to disjunctives.
2
Analyze the examples for each type
For 'anadiyyah, the author gives 'even and odd', 'tree and stone', and 'Zaid being in the sea and not drowning'. These pairs are inherently incompatible: a number cannot be both even and odd, a tree cannot be a stone, and being in the sea normally precludes not drowning. For ittifaqiyyah, the example is 'black or writer' in the context of a black non-writer: the concepts are not inherently opposed, but the disjunction holds by accident of reality.
The ittifaqiyyah example is cut off in the source; the sense is that the opposition arises only from external circumstances.
3
Apply the criterion to a new Islamic example
Consider the proposition: 'Either this liquid is wine or it is vinegar.' Determine whether the opposition is essential or accidental. Wine and vinegar are not inherently incompatible—a liquid could be wine at one time and vinegar later—so the opposition is accidental. Justify using the definitions from the passage.
In usul al-fiqh, this classification helps distinguish between genuine contradictions and those that can be reconciled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦٣ - القضية المنفصلة العنادية والاتفاقية]
قال: وكلّ واحدة من هذه الثلاثة: إما عنادية – وهي التي يكون التنافي فيها لذاتي الجزأين كما في الأمثلة المذكورة - وإما اتفاقية – وهي التي يكون التنافي فيها بمجرد الاتفاق كقولنا للأسود اللاكاتب: «إما أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً حقيقية، أو «لا أسود أو كاتباً» مانعة الجمع، أو «أسود أو لا كاتباً» مانعة الخلق.
القول : كلّ واحدة من المنفصلات الثلاث إما عنادية أو اتفاقية، كما أن المتصلة إما لزوميّة أو اتفاقيّة، فنسبة العناد والاتفاق إلى المنفصلات كنسبة اللزوم والاتفاق إلى المتصلات.
أما العنادية فهي التي يكون الحكم فيها بالتنافي لذات الجزأين-أي حكم فيها بأن مفهوم أحدهما مناف للآخر مع قطع النظر عن الواقع، كما بين الزوج والفرد، والشجر والحجر، وكون زيد في البحر وأن لا يغرق.
وأما الاتفاقية : فهي التي حكم فيها بالتنافي لا لذات الجزأين، بل بمجرد الاتفاق - أي بمجرد أن يتفق في الواقع أن يكون بينهما منافاة، وإن لم يقتض مفهوم أحدهما أن يكون منافياً للآخر - كقولنا : للأسود
[63 - The disjunctive proposition: 'anadiyyah and ittifaqiyyah]
He said: And each one of these three is either 'anadiyyah – that in which the incompatibility is due to the essences of the two parts, as in the mentioned examples – or ittifaqiyyah – that in which the incompatibility is merely by coincidence, like our saying of the black non-writer: 'Either this is black or a writer' true, or 'Not black or a writer' exclusive (man'at al-jam'), or 'Black or not a writer' inclusive (man'at al-khuluw).
The commentary: Each one of the three disjunctives is either 'anadiyyah or ittifaqiyyah, just as the conditional is either luzumiyyah or ittifaqiyyah. So the relation of opposition ('anad) and coincidence (ittifaq) to disjunctives is like the relation of necessary consequence (luzum) and coincidence to conditionals.
As for the 'anadiyyah, it is that in which the judgment of incompatibility is due to the essences of the two parts – i.e., a judgment that the concept of one is incompatible with the other regardless of actual reality, as between even and odd, tree and stone, and Zaid being in the sea and not drowning.
As for the ittifaqiyyah, it is that in which the judgment of incompatibility is not due to the essences of the two parts, but merely by coincidence – i.e., merely because it happens in reality that there is incompatibility between them, even though the concept of one does not entail incompatibility with the other – like our saying: of the black...
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive propositions (al-munfasilat) into two types: 'anadiyyah (essential opposition) and ittifaqiyyah (accidental opposition). The classification parallels that of conditionals (al-muttasilat), which are divided into luzumiyyah (necessary consequence) and ittifaqiyyah (accidental). The author begins with the statement (probably from a matn) that each of the three disjunctive types – exclusive (مانعة الجمع), inclusive (مانعة الخلو), and exhaustive (مانعة الجمع والخلو) – can be either 'anadiyyah or ittifaqiyyah. Examples are given for each: e.g., 'Either this is black or a writer' (where 'writer' is accidentally incompatible with 'black' if the person is black but not a writer). The commentary then defines: 'anadiyyah means the incompatibility stems from the very natures (dhat) of the two concepts, regardless of external circumstances, like 'even' and 'odd' (they are inherently opposite) or 'tree' and 'stone' (different categories). Another example is 'Zaid is in the sea and Zaid is not drowning' – these are incompatible by definition: being in water normally precludes not drowning, but the example illustrates necessary opposition? Actually, the example is 'Zaid being in the sea and not drowning' – these are incompatible essentially because being in the sea without drowning is impossible? The text says 'كون زيد في البحر وأن لا يغرق' – this could be read as 'Zaid being in the sea and not drowning' – these are incompatible in reality but perhaps conceptually related? The author considers it 'anadiyyah because the concept of being in the sea normally entails the possibility of drowning? This example might require further explanation but we follow the text. Ittifaqiyyah is where the incompatibility is not due to the concepts themselves but happens to occur in reality, like 'Zaid is black and Zaid is a writer' – these concepts are not inherently incompatible, but if Zaid happens to be black and not a writer, the disjunction 'either black or writer' becomes exclusive by accident. The example cuts off but the sense is clear. The key takeaway: the classification depends on the source of incompatibility – essential (by definition) or accidental (by circumstance).

Key Takeaway

Essential vs. Accidental Disjunctive Propositions
Classifies disjunctive propositions into essential and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential from accidental disjunctive propositions by examining whether the opposition is due to the essences of the parts or merely coincidental.

Core Idea
Essential opposition is inherent; accidental opposition is situational.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive propositions (المنفصلات) are a key tool in defining and debating positions. The matn teaches that every disjunctive is either essential (عنادية) or accidental (اتفاقية). This lesson unpacks that distinction, showing how it applies to real examples from fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of the disjunction
Look at the two sides separated by 'أو' (or) in the proposition. These are the disjuncts.
2
Test if the opposition is due to the concepts themselves
Ask: Are the two concepts inherently contradictory, like even and odd? If yes, it's essential. The judgment of opposition holds regardless of external reality.
3
Check if the opposition is merely coincidental
If the concepts are not contradictory but happen to conflict in a specific instance, it's accidental. The opposition depends on the state of affairs, not on the meanings alone.
4
Classify the examples from the passage
Apply the test: 'tree and stone' is essential because the concepts of tree and stone are inherently different (one cannot be both). 'Black and writer' in the black non-writer example is accidental, because blackness and writerhood are not conceptually opposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية
الواحد والكثير، بل بين هذا واحد وهذا كثير، فإن القضية القائلة: «إمّا أن يكون هذا واحداً وإما أن يكون هذا كثيراً» مانعة الجمع لامتناع اجتماع جزأيها على الصدق.
فقد بان أن الإشكال إنما نشأ من سوء الفهم وقلة التدبّر.
[٦٣ - القضية المنفصلة العنادية والاتفاقية]
قال: وكلّ واحدة من هذه الثلاثة: إما عنادية – وهي التي يكون التنافي فيها لذاتي الجزأين كما في الأمثلة المذكورة - وإما اتفاقية – وهي التي يكون التنافي فيها بمجرد الاتفاق كقولنا للأسود اللاكاتب: «إما أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً حقيقية، أو «لا أسود أو كاتباً» مانعة الجمع، أو «أسود أو لا كاتباً» مانعة الخلو.
القول : كلّ واحدة من المنفصلات الثلاث إما عنادية أو اتفاقية، كما أن المتصلة إما لزوميّة أو اتفاقيّة، فنسبة العناد والاتفاق إلى المنفصلات كنسبة اللزوم والاتفاق إلى المتصلات.
أما العنادية فهي التي يكون الحكم فيها بالتنافي لذات الجزأين-أي حكم فيها بأن مفهوم أحدهما مناف للآخر مع قطع النظر عن الواقع، كما بين الزوج والفرد، والشجر والحجر، وكون زيد في البحر وأن لا يغرق.
وأما الاتفاقية : فهي التي حكم فيها بالتنافي لا لذات الجزأين، بل بمجرد الاتفاق - أي بمجرد أن يتفق في الواقع أن يكون بينهما منافاة، وإن لم يقتض مفهوم أحدهما أن يكون منافياً للآخر - كقولنا : للأسود اللاكاتب : «إما أن يكون هذا أسود أو كاتباً كانت حقيقية، فإنه لا منافاة بين مفهوم الأسود والكاتب، ولكن اتفق تحقق السواد وانتفاء الكتابة، فلا يصدقان لانتفاء الكتابة ولا يكذبان لوجود السواد.
ولو قلنا : «إما أن يكون هذا لا أسود أو كاتباً» كانت مانعة الجمع لأنهما لا يصدقان، ولكن يكذبان لانتفاء اللا أسود والكتابة معا في الواقع. ولو قلنا : «إما أن يكون هذا أسود أو لا كاتباً» كانت مانعة الخلو، لأنهما لا يكذبان ولكن يصدقان لتحقق السواد واللاكتابة بحسب الواقع.
Sections of conditional propositions. The one and the many, but rather between this being one and this being many, for the proposition: 'Either this is one or this is many' is a prohibitive of conjunction because its two parts cannot both be true. So it has become clear that the problem arose only from poor understanding and lack of reflection. [63 - The essential and accidental disjunctive proposition] He said: Each of these three is either essential – which is that in which the opposition is due to the essences of the two parts as in the mentioned examples – or accidental – which is that in which the opposition is merely by coincidence, like our saying about the black non-writer: 'Either this is black or a writer' as a true disjunction, or 'Not black or a writer' as a prohibitive of conjunction, or 'Black or not a writer' as a prohibitive of vacuity. The statement: Each of the three disjunctives is either essential or accidental, just as the conditional is either implicative or accidental. So the relation of essentiality and accidentally to disjunctives is like the relation of implication and accidentally to conditionals. As for the essential, it is that in which the judgment of opposition is due to the essences of the two parts – i.e., it judges that the concept of one is opposed to the other without regard to reality, as between even and odd, tree and stone, and Zayd being in the sea and his not drowning. As for the accidental, it is that in which the judgment of opposition is not due to the essences of the two parts, but merely by coincidence – i.e., merely because it happens in reality that there is opposition between them, even if the concept of one does not necessitate being opposed to the other – like our saying about the black non-writer: 'Either this is black or a writer' as a true disjunction, for there is no opposition between the concept of black and writer, but blackness happened to be realized and writing to be absent, so they are not both true because of the absence of writing, nor both false because of the existence of blackness. And if we say: 'Either this is not black or a writer' it would be a prohibitive of conjunction because they are not both true, but they are both false due to the absence of non-blackness and writing together in reality. And if we say: 'Either this is black or not a writer' it would be a prohibitive of vacuity, because they are not both false but they are both true due to the realization of blackness and non-writing according to reality.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies disjunctive propositions (المنفصلات) into essential (عنادية) and accidental (اتفاقية). It begins by noting that a previous problem arose from misunderstanding the distinction between one and many, but that is clarified. Then it quotes the matn saying each of the three disjunctive types (true, prohibitive of conjunction, prohibitive of vacuity) is either essential or accidental. The commentary explains that this classification parallels that of conditionals (متصلات) into implicative and accidental. The essential disjunctive is defined as one where the incompatibility between the two parts is due to their very concepts, regardless of external reality, e.g., even and odd, or tree and stone. The accidental disjunctive is where the incompatibility is merely coincidental in reality, not due to the concepts themselves. The example given is the black non-writer: 'Either this is black or a writer' as a true disjunction. Since blackness and writerhood are not conceptually opposed, but in this particular instance the object is black and not a writer, the two parts happen to be such that they cannot both be true (because the object is not a writer) and cannot both be false (because it is black). Then it shows how changing the parts to negations turns it into a prohibitive of conjunction or prohibitive of vacuity. This careful distinction is crucial in logical analysis of propositions in Islamic jurisprudence and theology, where one must distinguish between essential and accidental relations.

عنادية
اتفاقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
حقيقية

Key Takeaway
Essential disjunctives have inherent opposition; accidental disjunctives have coincidental opposition.

Negatives of Conditional Propositions
Defines the negative of each of the eight affirmative conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the negative of each type of conditional proposition (implicative, essential opposition, accidental opposition) and distinguish it from the affirmative.

Core Idea
The negative of a conditional negates the relation, not the truth of the parts.

In the previous lesson, you learned the eight affirmative conditional propositions. Now we look at their negatives. Understanding the negative is crucial because in logical argument, you often need to negate a conditional statement to test its validity or to refute an opponent's claim. This passage defines the negatives for each type of conditional, establishing a clear pattern.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight affirmatives
You know there are two implicative (لزومية and اتفاقية) conditionals and six disjunctives (three essential, three accidental). All are affirmative. This lesson defines their negatives.
2
Grasp the general principle of negation
The passage states: 'The negative of each is that in which what is judged in its affirmative is negated.' So each negative directly opposes the specific judgment made in the affirmative.
3
Identify the three negative types
The negatives are named after the relation they negate: سالبة لزومية (negation of implication), سالبة عنادية (negation of essential opposition), سالبة اتفاقية (negation of accidental opposition).
The passage begins to explain the implicative negative but is cut off.
4
Apply to an example
For an implicative affirmative 'If A then B' (judging implication), its negative judges 'It is not the case that if A then B' (i.e., negation of the implication). Similarly, for a disjunctive affirmative 'Either A or B' with essential opposition, the negative negates the opposition.

The statement: You have known eight propositions: two implicative and accidental conditionals, and six disjunctives: three essential and three accidental – all of these are affirmatives, because their mentioned definitions apply only to affirmatives, so we must define their negatives. The negative of each is that in which what is judged in its affirmative is negated. So since the implicative affirmative judges the implication of the consequent from the antecedent, the implicative negative is the negation of implication – i.e., that in which the negation of implication is judged, not that in which...

This passage defines the negatives (سوالب) of the eight conditional propositions previously discussed: two conditionals (متصلات: لزومية and اتفاقية) and six disjunctives (منفصلات: three essential and three accidental). All eight are affirmatives (موجبات). The author explains that each negative negates the specific relation affirmed in the affirmative. For example, the negative of the implicative conditional (لزومية) negates the implication (اللزوم) between antecedent and consequent, so it is called 'salba lizumiyya' (negative implicative). Similarly for essential and accidental. The passage begins distinguishing negatives from affirmatives and sets the pattern for defining them. The text ends mid-sentence, showing that the definition continues (likely completing the idea that the negative of a disjunctive negates the essential or accidental opposition).

Negatives of Conditional Propositions: Relation vs Terms
Defines negatives of eight conditional propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the negative of a conditional proposition from an affirmative conditional with negated terms, using the rule that negation concerns the relation (luzūm, ʻinād, ittifāq) and not the terms themselves.

Core Idea
The negative of a conditional negates the relation, not the terms.

In this passage, al-Qutb al-Tahtani defines the negatives of the eight conditional propositions. He emphasizes that the quality of a conditional depends on the relation between its parts, not on the polarity of the terms. This is analogous to negation in categoricals, which turns on the copula. Correctly identifying negatives is essential for constructing valid syllogisms using conditional premises, a common tool in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recall the eight affirmatives
The eight conditional propositions are: 2 conjunctive (implicative and coincidental) and 6 disjunctive (3 incompatible and 3 coincidental). All are affirmatives because their definitions only apply to affirmatives.
Negative definitions are needed because only affirmatives were defined earlier.
2
Learn the general rule for negatives
The negative of each conditional is the proposition that negates what was judged in its affirmative. For implicatives, it negates the implication; for incompatibles, the incompatibility; for coincidentals, the agreement.
The relation is negated, not the terms.
3
Apply to implicative conditionals
A negative implicative (sāliba luzūmiyya) judges the negation of implication, not the implication of negation. Example: 'It is not the case that if the sun is shining then night exists' negates the implication.
Compare with 'If the sun is shining then night does not exist' which affirms the implication of negation - that is affirmative.
4
Apply to coincidental conditionals
A negative coincidental (sāliba ittifāqiyya) judges the negation of agreement, not the agreement of negation. Example: 'It is not the case that if man is rational then the donkey is braying' negates the agreement.
The positive version would be 'If man is rational then the donkey is not braying' - that is affirmative coincidental.
5
Extend the analogy to categoricals
Just as negation in categoricals turns on the copula (salb al-ḥaml) not on the terms, so negation in conditionals turns on the relation (conjunction/disjunction) and its types. Any combination of term polarities can appear in both affirmatives and negatives.
This prevents the mistake of thinking a conditional with a negative term is automatically negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وسالبة كلّ واحدة من هذه القضايا الثمان هي التي يرفع فيها ماحكم به في موجباتها؛ فسالبة اللزوم تسمّى سالبة لزومية، وسالبة العناد تسمى سالبة عنادية، وسالبة الاتفاق تسمّى سالبة اتفاقية. القول: قد عرفت ثماني قضايا - متصلتان لزوميّة واتفاقية، ومنفصلات ست: ثلاث منها عناديات، وثلاث منها اتفاقيات - وهي كلها موجبات، لأن تعاريفها المذكورة لا تنطبق إلا على الموجبات، فلابد من تعريف سوالبها: فسالبة كل منها هي التي يرفع فيها ماحكم به في موجبتها، فلما كانت الموجبة اللزوميّة ماحكم فيها بلزوم التالي للمقدم كانت السالبة اللزومية سالبة اللزوم - أي ماحكم فيها بسلب اللزوم - لا ماحكم فيها بلزوم السلب، فإن التي حكم فيها بلزوم السلب موجبة لزومية لا سالبة. مثلاً إذا قلنا: «ليس البتة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود» كانت سالبة، لأنّ الحكم فيها بسلب لزوم وجود الليل لطلوع الشمس، وإذا قلنا: «إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فليس الليل موجوداً» كانت موجبة، لأن الحكم فيها بلزوم سلب وجود الليل لطلوع الشمس. ولما كانت الموجبة المتصلة الاتفاقية ماحكم فيها بموافقة التالي للمقدّم في الصدق كانت السالبة الاتفاقية سالبة الاتفاق، أي ماحكم فيها بسلب موافقة التالي للمقدّم - لا ما حكم فيها بموافقة السلب - فإنّها اتفاقية موجبة. فإذا قلنا: «ليس إذا كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق» كانت سالبة اتفاقية، لأن الحكم فيها بسلب موافقة ناهقية الحمار لناطقية الإنسان. وإذا قلنا: «إذا كان الإنسان ناطقاً فليس الحمار ناهقاً» كانت موجبة لأن الحكم فيها بموافقة سلب ناهقية الحمار لناطقية الإنسان. كما أن السلب في الحمليات بحسب سلب الحمل – لا باعتبار طرفيها عدولا وتحصيلا، فربما كان طرفا الحملية مشتملين على حرف السلب وتكون القضية موجبة كقولنا: «اللا آدمي لاعالم» كذلك السلب في المتصلات والمنفصلات بحسب سلب الاتصال ونوعيه - أعني اللزوم والاتفاق - وبحسب سلب الانفصال ونوعيه - أعني العناد والاتفاق - ولا اعتبار بأطراف الشرطيات في سلبها وإيجابها، بل الأقسام الأربعة - أعني كون الطرفين موجبتين وسالبتين، وكون المقدم موجبة والتالي سالبة وبالعكس - توجد في الموجبات والسوالب في المتصلات والمنفصلات (شريف).
He said: The negative of each one of these eight propositions is that in which what is judged in their affirmatives is negated. So the negative of implication is called 'negative implicative', the negative of incompatibility is called 'negative incompatible', and the negative of coincidence is called 'negative coincidental'. The commentary: You have known eight propositions: two conjunctive (implicative and coincidental) and six disjunctive: three of them incompatible and three coincidental – and all of them are affirmatives, because their mentioned definitions only apply to affirmatives, so it is necessary to define their negatives. The negative of each of them is that in which what is judged in its affirmative is negated. Since the affirmative implicative is that in which the implication of the consequent from the antecedent is judged, the negative implicative is the negation of implication – i.e., that in which the negation of implication is judged – not that in which the implication of negation is judged, for that in which the implication of negation is judged is an affirmative implicative, not a negative. For example, if we say: 'It is not the case that if the sun is shining then night exists', it is a negative, because the judgment in it is the negation of the implication of the existence of night from sunshine. And if we say: 'If the sun is shining then night does not exist', it is affirmative, because the judgment in it is the implication of the negation of the existence of night from sunshine. And since the affirmative conjunctive coincidental is that in which the agreement of the consequent with the antecedent in truth is judged, the negative coincidental is the negation of agreement – i.e., that in which the negation of agreement is judged – not that in which the agreement of negation is judged, for that is a coincidental affirmative. So if we say: 'It is not the case that if man is rational then the donkey is braying', it is a negative coincidental, because the judgment in it is the negation of the agreement of the donkey's braying with man's rationality. And if we say: 'If man is rational then the donkey is not braying', it is affirmative, because the judgment in it is the agreement of the negation of the donkey's braying with man's rationality. Just as negation in categoricals is with respect to the negation of the copula – not considering their terms as modificative or modificatory, for sometimes the two terms of a categorical contain a negation particle and the proposition is affirmative, as in our saying: 'The non-human is not-knowing' – similarly, negation in conjunctives and disjunctives is with respect to the negation of conjunction and its two types – namely implication and coincidence – and with respect to the negation of disjunction and its two types – namely incompatibility and coincidence – and no consideration is given to the terms of conditionals in their negation and affirmation; rather, the four divisions – i.e., the two terms being affirmative and negative, and the antecedent being affirmative and the consequent negative, and vice versa – occur in both affirmatives and negatives in conjunctives and disjunctives (Sharif).
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage defines the negatives of conditional propositions. The author first states a general rule: the negative of each of the eight conditional propositions (two conjunctive and six disjunctive) is the proposition that negates what was judged in its affirmative. He then names the three types: negative implicative (saliba luzumiyya), negative incompatible (saliba inadiyya), and negative coincidental (saliba ittifaqiyya). The commentary explains why definitions are needed: the earlier definitions only covered affirmatives. For the implicative conditional, the affirmative judges the implication of the consequent from the antecedent; therefore the negative judges the negation of that implication, not the implication of the negation. An example clarifies: 'It is not the case that if the sun is shining then night exists' is a negative, because it negates the implication of night from sunshine. In contrast, 'If the sun is shining then night does not exist' is affirmative, because it affirms the implication of the negation of night. Similarly for coincidental conditionals: the affirmative judges the agreement of the consequent with the antecedent in truth; the negative judges the negation of that agreement. The example 'It is not the case that if man is rational then the donkey is braying' is a negative coincidental, whereas 'If man is rational then the donkey is not braying' is affirmative. The final paragraph draws an analogy with categoricals: just as negation in categoricals turns on the copula (not on whether terms are positive or negative), so in conditionals negation turns on the relation (conjunction or disjunction) and its types. The terms themselves – whether affirmative or negative – do not determine the quality of the conditional; both affirmatives and negatives can have any combination of term polarities. This guards against a common misunderstanding: that a conditional with a negated antecedent or consequent is automatically a negative. Instead, the quality is determined by the relation.

Key Terms
سالبة لزومية
سالبة اتفاقية
سالبة عنادية
السلب في الحمليات

Key Takeaway
The quality of a conditional proposition is determined by the relation (implication, incompatibility, agreement), not by the positivity or negativity of its terms.

Negation in Conditional Propositions: Relation over Parts
Explains negation in conditional propositions, distinguishing relation from parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish positive from negative conditional propositions by identifying whether the judgment negates the connection/disjunction or affirms the negation of a part.

Core Idea
A conditional's polarity depends on the judgment of the relation, not the truth of its parts.

This passage from al-Qutbi's commentary on logic clarifies a common pitfall: thinking that a negative conditional requires negative parts. Instead, the key is whether the relation (connection or disjunction) is negated. We will work through examples involving sunlight and night, and rationality and braying, to see how the same parts can yield both positive and negative conditionals depending on where the negation is placed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts and relation
Find the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) in the conditional. Determine whether it is a connected (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila) proposition.
In the passage, 'If the sun is rising then night exists' has antecedent 'sun rising' and consequent 'night exists', and it is connected.
2
Locate the main judgment
Determine whether the judgment is about the relation (luzum/ittifaq) or about the parts. A negative conditional judges the negation of the relation.
An example: 'Not if the sun is rising then night exists' judges the negation of the necessity of night's existence for sunrise.
3
Apply the rule: negation of relation makes it negative; affirmation of a negated consequent makes it positive
Compare 'Not if A then B' (negative) with 'If A then not B' (positive). In the first, the relation is negated; in the second, the relation is affirmed but with a negated consequent.
The passage calls the former 'saliba' and the latter 'mujiba' even though both contain a 'not'.
4
Test with disjunctive case
For disjunctives, negation of opposition (inad) or accident (ittifaq) yields negative conditionals. The types of opposition (mani'at al-jam', etc.) determine the specific judgment.
A negative oppositional removes the opposition in truth, falsehood, or both, as explained in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية بلزوم السلب، فإن التي حكم فيها بلزوم السلب موجبة لزومية لاسالبة) مثلاً إذا قلنا : «ليس البنّة إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود» كانت سالبة، لأنّ الحكم فيها بسلب لزوم وجود الليل لطلوع الشمس، وإذا قلنا : «إذا كانت الشمس طالعة فليس الليل موجوداً كانت موجبة، لأن الحكم فيها بلزوم سلب وجود الليل لطلوع الشمس.
ولما كانت الموجبة المتصلة الاتفاقية ماحكم فيها بموافقة التالي للمقدّم في الصدق كانت السالبة الاتفاقية سالبة الاتفاق، أي ماحكم فيها بسلب موافقة التالي للمقدّم - لا ما حكم فيها بموافقة السلب - فإنّها اتفاقية موجبة.
فإذا قلنا : «ليس إذا كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق كانت سالبة اتفاقية، لأن الحكم فيها بسلب موافقة ناهقية الحمار لناطقية الإنسان. وإذا قلنا : «إذا كان الإنسان ناطقاً فليس الحمار ناهقاً كانت موجبة لأن الحكم فيها بموافقة سلب ناهقية الحمار لناطقية الإنسان.
(١) كما أن السلب في الحمليات بحسب سلب الحمل – لا باعتبار طرفيها عدولا وتحصيلا، فربما كان طرفا الحملية مشتملين على حرف السلب وتكون القضية موجبة كقولنا : «اللا آدمي لاعالم كذلك السلب في المتصلات والمنفصلات بحسب سلب الاتصال ونوعيه - أعني اللزوم والاتفاق - وبحسب سلب الانفصال ونوعيه - أعني العناد والاتفاق - ولا اعتبار بأطراف الشرطيات في سلبها وإيجابها ، بل الأقسام الأربعة - أعني كون الطرفين موجبتين وسالبتين، وكون المقدم موجبة والتالي سالبة وبالعكس - توجد في الموجبات والسوالب في المتصلات والمنفصلات (شريف).
وعلى هذا تكون السالبة العنادية سالبة العناد، وهي ماحكم فيها برفع العناد : إما رفع العناد الذي هو في الصدق والكذب العناديّة الحقيقية، وإما رفع العناد الذي هو في الصدق وهي مانعة الجمع وإما رفع العناد الذي هو في الكذب وهي مانعة الخلو - لا ماحكم فيها بعناد السلب.
والسالبة الاتفاقية ما يحكم فيها بسلب اتفاق المنافاة فيها على أحد الأنحاء - لا ما يحكم فيها باتفاق السلب.
The divisions of conditional propositions according to the negation of the connection. Indeed, that in which the judgment is about the negation of the connection is a positive necessary (conditional), not negative. For example, when we say: 'Not absolutely if the sun is rising then night exists,' it is negative, because the judgment in it is the negation of the necessity of the existence of night for the rising of the sun. And when we say: 'If the sun is rising then night does not exist,' it is positive, because the judgment in it is the necessity of the negation of the existence of night for the rising of the sun. Since the positive connected accidental (conditional) is that which is judged to have the consequent agreeing with the antecedent in truth, the negative accidental (conditional) is the negation of agreement, i.e., that which is judged to have the negation of the agreement of the consequent to the antecedent – not that which is judged to have the agreement of the negation – for that is a positive accidental (conditional). So if we say: 'Not if man is rational then the donkey is braying,' it is a negative accidental (conditional), because the judgment in it is the negation of the agreement of the braying of the donkey for the rationality of man. And if we say: 'If man is rational then the donkey is not braying,' it is positive, because the judgment in it is the agreement of the negation of the braying of the donkey for the rationality of man. Just as negation in categorical (propositions) is according to the negation of predication – not considering their two terms as to alteration and verification, so sometimes the two terms of the categorical include the letter of negation and the proposition is positive, like our saying: 'The non-human is non-knowing.' Similarly, negation in connected and disjunctive (conditionals) is according to the negation of connection and its two types – namely necessity and accident – and according to the negation of disjunction and its two types – namely opposition and accident – and no consideration is given to the parts of conditionals in their negation and affirmation. Rather, the four divisions – namely the two parts being positive and negative, and the antecedent being positive and the consequent being negative, and vice versa – are found in the positives and negatives among connected and disjunctive (conditionals) (al-Sharif). And upon this, the negative oppositional (conditional) is the negation of opposition, and it is that which is judged to have the removal of opposition: either the removal of opposition which is in truth and falsehood – the true oppositional – or the removal of opposition which is in truth – and it is the preventing of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') – or the removal of opposition which is in falsehood – and it is the preventing of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluww) – not that which is judged to have the opposition of negation. And the negative accidental (conditional) is that which is judged to have the negation of accidental incompatibility in one of the modes – not that which is judged to have the accident of negation.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage defines how negation operates in conditional propositions (shartiyya), whether connected (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). The author distinguishes between negating the relation (salb al-irtibat) and negating the parts. For connected conditionals, a negative judgment means denying that the antecedent necessitates the consequent (or that the consequent agrees accidentally). Examples are given using solar rising and night, and human rationality and donkey braying. The author then draws an analogy with categorical propositions, where negation is about the copula, not the terms. Then the text classifies disjunctive conditionals: the negative oppositional (saliba 'inadiyya) is defined as removing opposition, which can be genuine (both truth and falsehood), or only in truth (mani'at al-jam'), or only in falsehood (mani'at al-khuluww). The negative accidental (saliba ittifaqiyya) is defined as negating accidental incompatibility. Throughout, the key point is that the positivity or negativity of a conditional is determined by the judgment on the connection/disjunction, not by the positivity or negativity of its parts. This clarifies common confusion.

سالبة

A) 'If the sun is rising, then night exists.'
B) 'Not if the sun is rising, then night exists.'
C) 'If the sun is rising, then night does not exist.'
D) 'If the sun is not rising, then night exists.'

Key Takeaway
The positivity or negativity of a conditional proposition is determined by the judgment on the connection or disjunction, not by the affirmative or negative form of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Truth Conditions of Positive Connected Conditional
Specifies when a positive connected conditional is true or false based on part truth values.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a positive connected conditional by applying the distinction between necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals and the principle of correspondence to reality (nafs al-amr).

Core Idea
A conditional's truth is about the connection, not the truth of its parts.

The author of this passage clarifies a common misunderstanding: the truth or falsehood of a conditional proposition (muttasila) is not determined by whether the antecedent and consequent are individually true or false, but by whether the judgment of connection (e.g., necessity or accidental agreement) corresponds to reality (nafs al-amr). In this lesson, we will parse the specific cases given for when a positive connected conditional is true or false, paying careful attention to the distinction between necessary and accidental conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the evaluation principle
The author states that truth of a conditional is by correspondence of the 'judgment of connection' (al-hukm bi al-ittisal) to the actual state of affairs. Note that this is independent of the truth values of the parts themselves.
This principle reframes the entire discussion.
2
Distinguish necessary vs. accidental
The text first discusses the 'luzumiyya' (necessary) conditional, then adds an exception for 'ittifaqiyya' (accidental). In a necessary conditional, the connection is one of entailment; in an accidental one, it is merely a coincidence of truth values.
This distinction changes the truth conditions significantly.
3
List truth cases for necessary conditional
The author says a necessary conditional is true with: two true parts, two false parts, two unknown parts, and with a false antecedent and true consequent. It is false with: two false parts (again? note the ambiguity), false antecedent with true consequent, true antecedent with false consequent, and two true parts.
The double listing for 'two false parts' may indicate that the truth/falsity depends on whether the necessity relation actually holds. The general rule is that the conditional is false only when the antecedent is true and consequent is false, but the text includes extra cases that may reflect different readings.
4
Contrast with accidental conditional
For an accidental conditional, the author states that it cannot be false when both parts are true. This is because an accidental conditional is true whenever the parts have the same truth value – both true or both false – and false when they differ.
So for accidental conditionals, the truth conditions align more directly with the truth table for material equivalence.
5
Apply the general principle
Regardless of the specific case, always refer back to the core principle: the conditional is true if the judgment of connection matches reality, false otherwise. The enumerated cases are examples, not exhaustive definitions.
Use this principle to resolve ambiguous cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦٥- لزوم صدق أجزاء القضايا الشرطة أو عدمها]
قال: والمتصلة الموجبة تصدق عن صادقين وعن كاذبين وعن مجهولي الصدق والكذب وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق، دون عكسه – لامتناع استلزام الصادق الكاذب.
وتكذب عن جزاين كاذبين وعن مقدّم كاذب وتال صادق – وبالعكس - وعن صادقين.
هذا إذا كانت لزومية، وإمّا إذا كانت اتفاقية فكذبها عن صادقين محال.
القول: صدق الشرطية وكذبها إنما هو بمطابقة الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال لنفس الأمر وعدمها - لا بصدق جزأيها وكذبهما - فإن طابق الحكم فيها لنفس الأمر فهي صادقة وإلا فهي كاذبة – كيف كان جزآها. ثم إذا نسبنا جزأيها إلى نفس الأمر حصلت أربعة أقسام : لأنهما إما أن يكونا صادقين أو كاذبين، أو يكون المقدم صادقاً والتالي كاذباً،
[65 - Necessity of the truth of the parts of conditional propositions or their falsehood] He said: The positive connected (conditional) is true with two true (parts), with two false (parts), with two unknown truth and falsehood, and with a false antecedent and true consequent – except the opposite of that – because of the impossibility of the truth entailing the false. And it is false with two false parts, with a false antecedent and true consequent – and vice versa – and with two true parts. This if it is necessary; but if it is accidental, its falsity with two true parts is impossible. The statement: The truth and falsehood of the conditional is only by the correspondence of the judgment of connection and disjunction to the actual state of affairs or its lack – not by the truth and falsehood of its two parts. For if the judgment in it corresponds to the actual state of affairs, it is true; otherwise it is false, whatever its two parts are. Then if we relate its two parts to the actual state of affairs, four divisions result: because either they are both true, or both false, or the antecedent is true and the consequent is false,
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage outlines the truth conditions for a positive connected conditional (muttasila mujiba). The author distinguishes between necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals. For a necessary conditional, it is true when both antecedent and consequent are true, both false, both unknown, or when the antecedent is false and consequent true. It is false when both are false, when antecedent is false and consequent true, when both are true (but careful: this seems contradictory; actually text says 'and with two true parts' as a case of falsity means that if a necessary conditional has two true parts but the connection is not necessary, it is false? The text might be ambiguous). Actually, re-reading: 'وتكذب عن جزاين كاذبين وعن مقدّم كاذب وتال صادق – وبالعكس - وعن صادقين.' So it lists three cases of falsity: (1) both false, (2) antecedent false and consequent true, (3) both true? 'وبالعكس' likely means the opposite of the previous combination (false antecedent, true consequent) which is true antecedent, false consequent, and then 'وعن صادقين' means also false when both true? That would be for necessary conditional: if connection is necessary but both parts happen to be true, it might still be true? Actually, the canonical truth condition for necessary conditional is that it is false only when antecedent true and consequent false. So the text might be including cases where the conditional is false because the connection doesn't hold even if parts are true? But the next sentence clarifies: 'هذا إذا كانت لزومية' meaning these truth conditions apply to necessary conditionals. And then 'وإمّا إذا كانت اتفاقية فكذبها عن صادقين محال' meaning for accidental, it cannot be false with two true parts (because accidental conditional is true when both parts have same truth value). So the falsity cases listed before include 'two true parts' but that is problematic for necessary? Possibly the author means that for necessary conditional, if both parts are true but the connection is not necessary, then it is false. But the wording is ambiguous. The passage ends with a general principle: truth of conditional is about correspondence of the judgment of connection to reality, not about parts. Then it starts enumerating four cases of parts' truth values relative to reality. The commentary should note the ambiguity and the general principle.

Key Takeaway
A conditional's truth is determined by the correspondence of the connection judgment to reality, not by the truth values of its parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Then complete the enumeration by adding the missing fourth combination (antecedent false, consequent true) and explain for each combination whether the necessary conditional is true or false according to the text's first set of cases.

Propositions

5 min
Truth Conditions of Positive Conditional Propositions
Author explains truth conditions and composition of true/false conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a positive conditional proposition based on whether the connection between antecedent and consequent matches reality, not merely the truth of its parts.

Core Idea
A conditional is true only if the connection between antecedent and consequent matches reality.

In this passage, Qutbi clarifies a foundational distinction: the truth of a conditional statement (If A then B) is not determined by the truth or falsehood of A and B individually, but by whether the asserted connection (luzum) actually holds in reality (nafs al-amr). This insight corrects a common misunderstanding and is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic, especially those used in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish truth of parts vs. truth of connection
Note that the author explicitly states: 'The truth and falsehood of a conditional is only by the judgment of connection matching reality — not by the truth or falsehood of its parts.' This is the key principle.
2
Identify the four possible truth-value combinations
Consider the four combinations of truth values (sidq/kidhb) for the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali): both true, both false, true antecedent + false consequent, false antecedent + true consequent.
3
Determine which combinations yield a true conditional for luzumiyya
For implicative (luzumiyya) conditionals, a true conditional can be formed from all combinations except a true antecedent with a false consequent. The author explains this is because 'it is impossible for the true to entail the false.'
This is a necessary condition: if the antecedent is true and the consequent false, the conditional must be false.
4
Apply the rule to the author's examples
Examine each example: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal' (both true) is true; 'If Zayd is a stone, he is inanimate' (both false) is true; 'If Zayd writes, he moves his hand' (both unknown) is true; 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal' (false antecedent, true consequent) is true. Confirm that none have a true antecedent and false consequent.
5
Recognize the exception for coincidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals
The author notes that for coincidental conditionals (where the connection is not necessary), a true conditional cannot be composed from two true parts. This highlights that truth conditions vary by type of conditional.
Ittifaqiyya conditionals are false when both parts are true because the connection is merely accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والمتصلة الموجبة تصدق عن صادقين وعن كاذبين وعن مجهولي الصدق والكذب وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق، دون عكسه – لامتناع استلزام الصادق الكاذب. وتكذب عن جزاين كاذبين وعن مقدّم كاذب وتال صادق – وبالعكس - وعن صادقين. هذا إذا كانت لزومية، وإمّا إذا كانت اتفاقية فكذبها عن صادقين محال.
القول: صدق الشرطية وكذبها إنما هو بمطابقة الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال لنفس الأمر وعدمها - لا بصدق جزأيها وكذبهما - فإن طابق الحكم فيها لنفس الأمر فهي صادقة وإلا فهي كاذبة – كيف كان جزآها. ثم إذا نسبنا جزأيها إلى نفس الأمر حصلت أربعة أقسام : لأنهما إما أن يكونا صادقين أو كاذبين، أو يكون المقدم صادقاً والتالي كاذباً، وبالعكس، فلنبين أن كلا من الشرطيات من أي هذه الأقسام تتركب : فالمتصلة الموجبة الصادقة تتركب عن صادقين كقولنا : «إن كان زيد إنساناً فهو حيوان وعن كاذبين كقولنا : إن كان زيد حجراً فهو جماد» وعن مجهولي الصدق والكذب كقولنا : إن كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرك يده وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق كقولنا : «إن كان زيد حماراً كان حيواناً دون عكسه - أي لا تتركب من مقدّم صادق وتال كاذب لامتناع أن يستلزم الصادق الكاذب، وإلا لزم كذب الصادق وصدق الكاذب، أما كذب الصادق فلأن اللازم كاذب، وكذب اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم، وأما صدق الكاذب فلأن الملزوم فيها صادق وصدق الملزوم مستلزم لصدق اللازم. لا يقال : إذا صح تركيب المتصلة من مقدم كاذب وتال صادق أن كل متصلة موجبة تنعكس موجبة جزئية، فقد صح تركيبها من مقدم صادق وتال كاذب. لانا نقول : ذلك في الكلية لا في الجزئية. فإن قلت : لما اعتبر في جزأي المتصلة الجهل بالصدق والكذب زاد الأقسام على الأربعة. فنقول : تلك الأقسام عند نسبتها إلى نفس الأمر داخلة فيها. والموجبة الكاذبة تتركب عن الأقسام الأربعة، لأن الحكم باللزوم بين
He said: The positive conditional is true from two true [parts], from two false, from two unknown in truth/falsehood, and from a false antecedent and true consequent, but not its opposite – because it is impossible for the true to entail the false. It is false from two false parts, from a false antecedent and true consequent – and the reverse – and from two true [parts]. This is if it is implicative; but if it is coincidental, its falsehood from two true [parts] is impossible.
The statement: The truth and falsehood of a conditional is only by the judgment of connection or disconnection matching reality or not – not by the truth or falsehood of its parts. For if the judgment in it matches reality, it is true; otherwise it is false – however its parts are. Then if we relate its parts to reality, four divisions arise: because they are either both true, both false, or the antecedent true and consequent false, or the reverse. Let us explain from which of these divisions each conditional is composed: The true positive conditional is composed from two true, like our saying: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal'; from two false, like our saying: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is inanimate'; from two unknown in truth/falsehood, like our saying: 'If Zayd writes, he moves his hand'; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, like our saying: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal' – but not its opposite, i.e., it is not composed from a true antecedent and false consequent, because it is impossible for the true to entail the false; otherwise it would necessitate the falsehood of the true and the truth of the false. As for the falsehood of the true, because the consequent is false, and the falsehood of the consequent necessitates the falsehood of the antecedent. As for the truth of the false, because the antecedent in it is true, and the truth of the antecedent necessitates the truth of the consequent. It cannot be said: If the composition of the conditional from a false antecedent and true consequent is valid, then every positive universal conditional converts to a positive particular conditional, so its composition from a true antecedent and false consequent would be valid. For we say: That is in the universal, not the particular. If you say: Since ignorance of truth/falsehood was considered in the parts of the conditional, the divisions increase beyond four. We say: Those divisions, when related to reality, are included in them. The false positive conditional is composed from the four divisions, because the judgment of implication between
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage from the Sharh al-Risala al-Shamsiyya (Qutbi) explains the truth conditions of conditional propositions (al-qadaya al-shartiyya), specifically the positive conditional (al-muttasila al-mujiba). The author clarifies that truth or falsehood of a conditional is determined by whether the judgment of connection (al-ittisal) or disconnection (al-infisal) matches reality (nafs al-amr), not by the truth values of its parts. He then examines the four possible combinations of truth values of the antecedent and consequent (both true, both false, first true second false, first false second true) and specifies from which combinations the conditional can be true or false. For implicative conditionals (luzumiyya), the true positive conditional can be composed from three combinations except for a true antecedent and false consequent, because that would violate implication (the true cannot entail the false). He provides examples from common logical exercises: 'If Zayd is human, he is an animal' (both true), 'If Zayd is a stone, he is inanimate' (both false), 'If Zayd writes, he moves his hand' (both unknown), 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal' (false antecedent, true consequent). He then defends this against a potential objection regarding conversion and addresses a point about the number of divisions when ignorance is considered. Finally, he states that the false positive conditional is composed from all four divisions because the judgment of implication does not hold.

Key Terms
المتصلة الموجبة
اللزومية
الاتفاقية
نفس الأمر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which combination of antecedent and consequent is IMPOSSIBLE for a true positive implicative conditional?
A) Both true
B) Both false
C) False antecedent, true consequent
D) True antecedent, false consequent

Key Takeaway
A positive conditional is true if and only if the connection between antecedent and consequent matches reality, regardless of the truth values of the parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the conditional: 'If a person performs wudu with nabidh (date drink), it is valid.' Both parts are true in reality (wudu with nabidh is valid in Hanafi fiqh). According to the passage, is this conditional true? Explain using the principle of nafs al-amr.
?
Construct a true positive conditional from two false parts using an example from fiqh (e.g., 'If something is prayed at sunrise, it is invalid due to sunrise' – both false because prayer at sunrise is not necessarily invalid). Explain why it is true.

Truth-Value Combinations for a True Conditional
Author lists truth-value combinations for true conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four truth-value combinations that constitute a true affirmative muttasila.

Core Idea
A true conditional allows three combinations and uniquely prohibits true antecedent with false consequent.

The author begins by listing all possible truth-value combinations for the antecedent and consequent in a true affirmative conditional. This is a fundamental step in understanding how conditional propositions function in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure
A muttasila is a conditional statement with an antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī) linked by implication (luzūm).
2
Enumerate the combinations
The author lists four combinations: both true, both false, both unknown, false antecedent with true consequent.
Each combination is accompanied by an Islamic example from the text.
3
Recognize the excluded combination
The combination true antecedent with false consequent is explicitly ruled out. The author will later explain why.
This exclusion is unique to the true conditional.
4
Compare with false conditional
Note that the true conditional has only four valid combinations, while the false conditional (covered later) allows all four possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية وبالعكس، فلنبين أن كلا من الشرطيات من أي هذه الأقسام تتركب : فالمتصلة الموجبة الصادقة تتركب عن صادقين كقولنا : «إن كان زيد إنساناً فهو حيوان وعن كاذبين كقولنا : إن كان زيد حجراً فهو جماد» وعن مجهولي الصدق والكذب كقولنا : إن كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرك يده وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق كقولنا : «إن كان زيد حماراً كان حيواناً دون عكسه - أي لا تتركب من مقدّم صادق وتال كاذب لامتناع أن يستلزم الصادق الكاذب، وإلا لزم كذب الصادق وصدق الكاذب، أما كذب الصادق فلأن اللازم كاذب، وكذب اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم، وأما صدق الكاذب فلأن الملزوم فيها صادق وصدق الملزوم مستلزم لصدق اللازم. لا يقال : إذا صح تركيب المتصلة من مقدم كاذب وتال صادق أن كل متصلة موجبة تنعكس موجبة جزئية، فقد صح تركيبها وعندهم من مقدم صادق وتال كاذب. لانا نقول : ذلك في الكلية لا في الجزئية. فإن قلت : لما اعتبر في جزأي المتصلة الجهل بالصدق والكذب زاد الأقسام على الأربعة. فنقول : تلك الأقسام عند نسبتها إلى نفس الأمر داخلة فيها. هي والموجبة الكاذبة تتركب عن الأقسام الأربعة، لأن الحكم باللزوم بين المقدم والتالي إذا لم يكن مطابقاً للواقع جاز أن يكونا كاذبين – كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً كان العالم قديماً وأن يكون المقدم كاذباً والتالي صادقاً - كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً فالإنسان ناطق» - و بالعكس - كقولنا : «إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالخلاً موجود» وأن يكونا صادقين كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فزيد إنسان».
Divisions of conditional propositions. And conversely, let us show that each of the conditionals is composed from which of these divisions: The affirmative true muttasila is composed from two true [propositions], like our statement: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; and from two false, like our statement: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is inanimate'; and from two unknown in truth and falsity, like our statement: 'If Zayd writes, he moves his hand'; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, like our statement: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal' — but not its converse, i.e. it is not composed from a true antecedent and false consequent, because it is impossible for the true to entail the false. Otherwise, it would necessitate the falsehood of the true and the truth of the false. As for the falsehood of the true, because the consequent is false, and the falsehood of the consequent entails the falsehood of the antecedent. As for the truth of the false, because the antecedent in it is true, and the truth of the antecedent entails the truth of the consequent. It should not be said: If the composition of the muttasila from a false antecedent and true consequent is valid, then every affirmative muttasila converts to a particular affirmative, and it would thus be composed according to them from a true antecedent and false consequent. For we say: That is in the universal, not in the particular. If you say: When ignorance of truth and falsity is considered in the two parts of the muttasila, the divisions increase beyond four. We say: Those divisions, when related to actual reality, are included within them. They and the false affirmative compose from the four divisions, because the judgment of implication between the antecedent and consequent, if it does not correspond to reality, it is possible that both are false — like our statement: 'If the vacuum exists, the world is eternal'; and that the antecedent is false and the consequent true — like our statement: 'If the vacuum exists, then man is rational'; and the converse — like our statement: 'If man is rational, then the vacuum exists'; and that both are true — like our statement: 'If the sun is rising, then Zayd is a human.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage expands on the possible truth-value combinations that form a true or false affirmative conditional (muttasila). The author first lists four combinations for the true muttasila: both parts true, both false, both unknown, and false antecedent with true consequent. He then argues that a true antecedent with a false consequent is impossible because the truth of the antecedent would necessitate the truth of the consequent due to implication; otherwise, the true antecedent would become false and the false consequent would become true. An objection is raised concerning the validity of conversion ('aks) of the conditional: if the conditional is true from a false antecedent and true consequent, then according to the conversion rule, a universal affirmative conditional would convert to a particular affirmative, implying that a true antecedent with false consequent is possible. The author counters that this conversion applies only to the universal, not the particular. A further query about considering ignorance of truth/falsity potentially increasing the number of divisions is answered by noting that these divisions reduce to the four when referred to actual reality. Finally, the false affirmative muttasila is shown to be composed from any of the four combinations, as the judgment of implication does not match reality.

Key Terms
المتصلة
مقدم
تال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which combination is impossible for a true affirmative muttasila according to the passage?
A) Both antecedent and consequent true
B) Both false
C) False antecedent and true consequent
D) True antecedent and false consequent

Key Takeaway
A true conditional can have various truth-value combinations, but it can never have a true antecedent implying a false consequent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For each of the four combinations listed by the author, provide a new Islamic example not from the text. Ensure each example is a true affirmative muttasila.

The Impossibility Argument: Truth Cannot Imply Falsehood
Author provides a reductio argument against true antecedent with false consequent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that a true antecedent cannot entail a false consequent.

Core Idea
Truth implying falsehood would make truth false and falsehood true — a contradiction.

After listing the valid combinations, the author proves why the combination of true antecedent and false consequent is impossible. This argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum that reinforces the nature of implication (luzūm).

Guided Path
1
Assume the problematic combination
Suppose we have a true antecedent (T) and a false consequent (F), and the conditional 'If T then F' is true (implying T entails F).
2
Derive the first contradiction
Since the consequent is false, and the antecedent is true and entails it, the true antecedent must be false (because a true antecedent cannot lead to a false consequent). Thus the true antecedent becomes false.
This is the falsehood of the true.
3
Derive the second contradiction
Since the antecedent is true and entails the consequent, the false consequent must be true (because a true antecedent implies a true consequent). Thus the false consequent becomes true.
This is the truth of the false.
4
Conclude impossibility
Both contradictions show that assuming a true antecedent implying a false consequent leads to absurdity; therefore such a combination is impossible for a true conditional.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Objection on Conversion of Conditionals
Author addresses an objection about conversion of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the objection regarding conversion of conditionals and the author's response distinguishing universal from particular.

Core Idea
Conversion of a universal conditional yields a particular, not a universal, so the objection fails.

An objector argues that if a true conditional can have a false antecedent and true consequent, then by conversion a true antecedent with false consequent would be possible. The author clarifies that conversion applies differently to universal versus particular conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Recall the conversion rule for conditionals
An affirmative universal muttasila (e.g., 'Every time A then B') converts to a particular affirmative muttasila ('Sometimes B then A').
This rule is mentioned in the passage: 'كل متصلة موجبة تنعكس موجبة جزئية'.
2
Apply to the allowed combination
Take a true universal conditional with false antecedent and true consequent, e.g., 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal'. Its conversion would be 'Some case where Zayd is an animal, he is a donkey' – a particular, not universal.
3
Understand the objection
The objector thinks the conversion yields a universal affirmative, which would imply that a true antecedent with false consequent is possible. But the author says conversion only gives a particular.
4
See the author's response
The author says: 'That is in the universal, not in the particular.' So the objection only works if we mistakenly treat the particular conversion as universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Truth-Value Combinations for a False Conditional
Author lists truth-value combinations for false conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four possible truth-value combinations for a false affirmative muttasila.

Core Idea
A false conditional allows all four combinations, including the one prohibited for true conditionals.

The author concludes by discussing the false affirmative muttasila. Unlike the true conditional, the false one can have any combination of truth values because the implication does not correspond to reality.

Guided Path
1
Understand what makes a conditional false
A false muttasila is one where the claimed implication (luzūm) does not hold in reality. The judgment of implication is not true.
2
List the four combinations
The author says the false conditional can be composed from: both true, both false, false antecedent true consequent, true antecedent false consequent. The last one was impossible for true conditionals but is possible here.
Examples are given: both false: 'If vacuum exists, world is eternal'; false antecedent true consequent: 'If vacuum exists, man is rational'; etc.
3
Note the difference from true conditional
The false conditional allows the combination that the true conditional prohibits: true antecedent with false consequent.
4
Consider the 'ignorance' objection
Someone might think that if we consider unknown truth values, there are more divisions. But the author says these reduce to the four when related to actual reality.
This clarifies that the classification is based on actual truth, not knowledge.
