أقسام القضايا الشرطية وبالعكس، فلنبين أن كلا من الشرطيات من أي هذه الأقسام تتركب : فالمتصلة الموجبة الصادقة تتركب عن صادقين كقولنا : «إن كان زيد إنساناً فهو حيوان وعن كاذبين كقولنا : إن كان زيد حجراً فهو جماد» وعن مجهولي الصدق والكذب كقولنا : إن كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرك يده وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق كقولنا : «إن كان زيد حماراً كان حيواناً دون عكسه - أي لا تتركب من مقدّم صادق وتال كاذب لامتناع أن يستلزم الصادق الكاذب، وإلا لزم كذب الصادق وصدق الكاذب، أما كذب الصادق فلأن اللازم كاذب، وكذب اللازم يستلزم كذب الملزوم، وأما صدق الكاذب فلأن الملزوم فيها صادق وصدق الملزوم مستلزم لصدق اللازم. لا يقال : إذا صح تركيب المتصلة من مقدم كاذب وتال صادق أن كل متصلة موجبة تنعكس موجبة جزئية، فقد صح تركيبها وعندهم من مقدم صادق وتال كاذب. لانا نقول : ذلك في الكلية لا في الجزئية. فإن قلت : لما اعتبر في جزأي المتصلة الجهل بالصدق والكذب زاد الأقسام على الأربعة. فنقول : تلك الأقسام عند نسبتها إلى نفس الأمر داخلة فيها. هي والموجبة الكاذبة تتركب عن الأقسام الأربعة، لأن الحكم باللزوم بين المقدم والتالي إذا لم يكن مطابقاً للواقع جاز أن يكونا كاذبين – كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً كان العالم قديماً وأن يكون المقدم كاذباً والتالي صادقاً - كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً فالإنسان ناطق» - و بالعكس - كقولنا : «إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالخلاً موجود» وأن يكونا صادقين كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فزيد إنسان».
Divisions of conditional propositions. And conversely, let us show that each of the conditionals is composed from which of these divisions: The affirmative true muttasila is composed from two true [propositions], like our statement: 'If Zayd is a human, he is an animal'; and from two false, like our statement: 'If Zayd is a stone, he is inanimate'; and from two unknown in truth and falsity, like our statement: 'If Zayd writes, he moves his hand'; and from a false antecedent and true consequent, like our statement: 'If Zayd is a donkey, he is an animal' — but not its converse, i.e. it is not composed from a true antecedent and false consequent, because it is impossible for the true to entail the false. Otherwise, it would necessitate the falsehood of the true and the truth of the false. As for the falsehood of the true, because the consequent is false, and the falsehood of the consequent entails the falsehood of the antecedent. As for the truth of the false, because the antecedent in it is true, and the truth of the antecedent entails the truth of the consequent. It should not be said: If the composition of the muttasila from a false antecedent and true consequent is valid, then every affirmative muttasila converts to a particular affirmative, and it would thus be composed according to them from a true antecedent and false consequent. For we say: That is in the universal, not in the particular. If you say: When ignorance of truth and falsity is considered in the two parts of the muttasila, the divisions increase beyond four. We say: Those divisions, when related to actual reality, are included within them. They and the false affirmative compose from the four divisions, because the judgment of implication between the antecedent and consequent, if it does not correspond to reality, it is possible that both are false — like our statement: 'If the vacuum exists, the world is eternal'; and that the antecedent is false and the consequent true — like our statement: 'If the vacuum exists, then man is rational'; and the converse — like our statement: 'If man is rational, then the vacuum exists'; and that both are true — like our statement: 'If the sun is rising, then Zayd is a human.'
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage expands on the possible truth-value combinations that form a true or false affirmative conditional (muttasila). The author first lists four combinations for the true muttasila: both parts true, both false, both unknown, and false antecedent with true consequent. He then argues that a true antecedent with a false consequent is impossible because the truth of the antecedent would necessitate the truth of the consequent due to implication; otherwise, the true antecedent would become false and the false consequent would become true. An objection is raised concerning the validity of conversion ('aks) of the conditional: if the conditional is true from a false antecedent and true consequent, then according to the conversion rule, a universal affirmative conditional would convert to a particular affirmative, implying that a true antecedent with false consequent is possible. The author counters that this conversion applies only to the universal, not the particular. A further query about considering ignorance of truth/falsity potentially increasing the number of divisions is answered by noting that these divisions reduce to the four when referred to actual reality. Finally, the false affirmative muttasila is shown to be composed from any of the four combinations, as the judgment of implication does not match reality.

Coincidental Conditional Truth Conditions
Author contrasts the coincidental conditional with the implicational and discusses its truth conditions and a subtle investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the truth value of a coincidental conditional and explain why it can be false despite both parts being true.

Core Idea
A coincidental conditional can be false despite both parts being true if a hidden relation implies necessity.

In Islamic logic, propositions are divided into implicational (luzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya). While an implicational conditional is false only when the antecedent is true and the consequent false, the coincidental type has more nuanced truth conditions. This lesson unpacks that nuance, including a critical investigation that reveals an unexpected subtlety.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish luzumiyya and ittifaqiyya
Note that the passage first sets aside implicational conditionals. For the coincidental, the connection is arbitrary. Example: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' There is no real implication.
2
Examine the basic truth table
The author states that an ittifaqiyya cannot be false when both parts are true because they 'necessarily agree' in truth. Thus, it is true when both are true, and false in all other three combinations (both false, true-false, false-true).
This holds when both parts must be true for the conditional to be true.
3
Consider the weaker condition
If we only require the consequent to be true (ignoring the antecedent), the conditional is also true when the antecedent is false and consequent true, reducing false cases to just two.
4
Understand the investigation
The key subtlety: mere truth of both parts or of the consequent is insufficient. The conditional must also lack any relation that would imply necessity. If such a relation exists, the conditional can be false even if both parts are true.
Example: 'If man is rational, then 2+2=4' – if there is a logical relation (though not explicit), the conditional might be false because the relation would require implication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا إذا كانت المتصلة لزومية، وأما إذا كانت اتفاقية فكذبها عن صادقين محال، لأنه إذا صدق الطرفان وافق أحدهما الآخر بالضرورة في الصدق، كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق» فهي تصدق عن صادقين وتكذب عن الأقسام الثلاثة الباقية، لأن طرفيها إن كانا كاذبين أو كان التالي كاذباً والمقدّم صادقاً فكذبها ظاهر – لأن الكاذب لا يوافق شيئاً - و إن كان المقدّم كاذباً والتالي صادقاً فكذلك، لاعتبار صـدق الطرفين فيها. وأما إذا اكتفينا بمجرد صدق التالي : يكون صدقها عن صادقين وعن مقدّم كاذب وتال صادق، وكذبها عن القسمين الباقيين. وهاهنا بحث : وهو أن الاتفاقية لا يكفي فيها صدق الطرفين أو صدق التالي ، بل لابد مع ذلك من عدم العلاقة، فيجوز كذبها عن صادقين إذا كان بينهما علاقة تقتضي الملازمة بينهما. (۱) هذا حق، نعم المتصلة المطلقة - أعني التي اكتفي فيها بمجرد الحكم بالاتصال من غير أن يتعرض لعلاقة نفياً أو إثباتاً - يمتنع كذبها عن صادقين وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق (شریف).
This is if the muttasila is implicational. But if it is coincidental, then its falsehood from two true [parts] is impossible, because if both parts are true, one necessarily agrees with the other in truth, like our statement: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' So it is true from two true and false from the remaining three divisions, because if its two parts are both false, or if the consequent is false and the antecedent true, its falsehood is apparent — because the false does not agree with anything — and if the antecedent is false and the consequent true, likewise, due to considering the truth of both parts in it. But if we suffice with mere truth of the consequent, then it is true from two true and from a false antecedent and true consequent, and false from the remaining two divisions. And here there is an investigation: that for the coincidental [conditional], mere truth of both parts or truth of the consequent is not sufficient; rather, along with that, there must be absence of relation, so it may be false from two true if there is between them a relation that necessitates implication. (1) This is correct. Yes, the absolute muttasila — meaning that in which one suffices with merely judging connection without addressing relation positively or negatively — it is impossible for it to be false from two true [parts] and from a false antecedent and true consequent (al-Sharif).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between the implicational (luzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifaqiyya) types of conditional propositions. For the coincidental conditional, the author states that it cannot be false when both parts are true, because if both are true they necessarily agree in truth, making the conditional true. Thus, it is true from two true parts and false from the other three combinations (both false, true antecedent false consequent, false antecedent true consequent). However, if one only requires the truth of the consequent (rather than both parts), then the conditional is also true from a false antecedent and true consequent, and only false from the remaining two. An important investigation follows: for a coincidental conditional, mere truth of both parts or of the consequent is not enough; there must also be an absence of any relation that would imply proper implication. If such a relation exists, the conditional could be false even if both parts are true, because the relation would necessitate a connection that is not actually present. Finally, the commentator al-Sharif clarifies that the 'absolute muttasila' — which judges only connection without any condition about relation — cannot be false from two true parts or from a false antecedent and true consequent.

Key Terms
اتفاقية
لزومية
المتصلة المطلقة

D) When the antecedent is false

Key Takeaway
A coincidental conditional is true when both parts are true, but only if there is no underlying relation that would imply a necessary connection.

Truth Conditions of Conditional Propositions (Muttasil)
Author details truth conditions for lūzumiyya and ittifāqiyya conditionals, including the need for absence of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between lūzumiyya and ittifāqiyya conditionals and determine their truth values for each combination of antecedent/consequent truth values.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional depends not only on its parts but on the connection type.

Conditional propositions (al-muttasila) are fundamental in Islamic logic, used in kalam and fiqh to express implications. This passage from the Shamsiyya clarifies when a conditional is true or false, distinguishing between necessary (lūzumiyya) and coincidental (ittifāqiyya) connections. You will learn to evaluate conditionals systematically, just as the logicians did.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional type
Read the conditional proposition. Determine whether it is lūzumiyya (necessary connection) or ittifāqiyya (coincidental). The author gives examples with 'إن كان' (if).
Lūzumiyya conditionals often have a causal or logical relationship; ittifāqiyya are accidental.
2
List truth-value combinations
There are four combinations for the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tālī): both true, both false, antecedent false/consequent true, antecedent true/consequent false.
3
Apply lūzumiyya truth conditions
For a lūzumiyya conditional, the only case where it is false is when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false. In all other combinations, it is true.
The author gives examples like 'If non-existence existed, then the world is eternal' (both false, true conditional).
4
Apply ittifāqiyya truth conditions (standard)
For an ittifāqiyya conditional, it is true only when both parts are true; it is false in the other three cases. The author mentions that if we only require truth of the consequent, then it is also true when antecedent false and consequent true.
This is the standard view; the passage later notes a nuance.
5
Check the additional condition
Note the research point: for ittifāqiyya, mere truth of both parts is insufficient; there must also be absence of any relation that would imply a logical connection. If such a relation exists, the conditional becomes false even if both parts are true.
Sharīf clarifies that the absolute conditional (without relation) cannot be false when both are true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرم الرسالة الشمسية المقدم والتالي إذا لم يكن مطابقاً للواقع جاز أن يكونا كاذبين – كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً كان العالم قديماً وأن يكون المقدم كاذباً والتالي صادقاً - كقولنا : إن كان الخلاً موجوداً فالإنسان ناطق» - و بالعكس - كقولنا : «إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالخلاً موجود» وأن يكونا صادقين كقولنا : إن كانت الشمس طالعة فزيد إنسان». هذا إذا كانت المتصلة لزومية، وأما إذا كانت اتفاقية فكذبها عن صادقين محال، لأنه إذا صدق الطرفان وافق أحدهما الآخر بالضرورة في الصدق، كقولنا : إن كان الإنسان ناطقاً فالحمار ناهق» فهي تصدق عن صادقين وتكذب عن الأقسام الثلاثة الباقية، لأن طرفيها إن كانا كاذبين أو كان التالي كاذباً والمقدّم صادقاً فكذبها ظاهر – لأن الكاذب لا يوافق شيئاً - و إن كان المقدّم كاذباً والتالي صادقاً فكذلك، لاعتبار صـدق الطرفين فيها. وأما إذا اكتفينا بمجرد صدق التالي : يكون صدقها عن صادقين وعن مقدّم كاذب وتال ،صادق، وكذبها عن القسمين الباقيين. وهاهنا بحث ) : وهو أن الاتفاقية لا يكفي فيها صدق الطرفين أو صدق التالي ، بل لابد مع ذلك من عدم العلاقة، فيجوز كذبها عن صادقين إذا كان بينهما علاقة تقتضي الملازمة بينهما. (۱) هذا حق، نعم المتصلة المطلقة - أعني التي اكتفي فيها بمجرد الحكم بالاتصال من غير أن يتعرض لعلاقة نفياً أو إثباتاً - يمتنع كذبها عن صادقين وعن مقدم كاذب وتال صادق (شریف).
The antecedent and consequent, if not corresponding to reality, may both be false – as in our saying: 'If non-existence existed, then the world is eternal' – and the antecedent may be false and the consequent true – as in our saying: 'If non-existence existed, then man is rational' – and the opposite – as in our saying: 'If man is rational, then non-existence exists' – and both may be true, as in our saying: 'If the sun is rising, then Zayd is human.' This is when the conditional is lūzumiyya (implicative). But if it is ittifāqiyya (coincidental), then its falsehood with both true is impossible, because if both parts are true, one necessarily agrees with the other in truth, as in our saying: 'If man is rational, then the donkey is braying.' So it is true with both true and false in the remaining three cases, because if its two parts are both false, or the consequent is false and the antecedent true, its falsehood is apparent – because the false does not agree with anything – and if the antecedent is false and the consequent true, likewise, due to the consideration of truth of both parts in it. And if we are satisfied with just the truth of the consequent, then its truth occurs with both true and with false antecedent and true consequent, and its falsehood occurs with the remaining two cases. And here there is a research: that in the ittifāqiyya, the truth of both parts or the truth of the consequent is not sufficient; rather, along with that, absence of relation is necessary, so it is possible for it to be false with both true if there is a relation between them that necessitates implication. (1) This is correct; indeed, the absolute conditional – I mean that which is satisfied with merely the judgment of connection without addressing relation negatively or affirmatively – its falsehood with both true and with false antecedent and true consequent is impossible (by Sharīf).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for conditional (mutassil) propositions in logic, distinguishing between lūzumiyya (implicative) and ittifāqiyya (coincidental) conditionals. The author first lists four possible truth combinations for a lūzumiyya conditional: both false, antecedent false/consequent true, antecedent true/consequent false, both true. They give examples: 'If non-existence existed, then the world is eternal' is a classic example of both false. Then they note that for ittifāqiyya conditionals, which assert mere coincidence without causal connection, the conditional can be true when both parts are true (since they happen to both be true), but it is false if both are false or if antecedent true and consequent false. However, if the antecedent is false and consequent true, it is also considered false under the standard interpretation because the truth of both parts is required. The author then presents a nuance: if one only requires the truth of the consequent, then the conditional is true also when antecedent false and consequent true. Finally, there is a critical research note: for ittifāqiyya, mere truth of the parts is not enough; there must be absence of any relation that would imply a logical connection; otherwise it would be false even if both are true. The editor Sharīf clarifies that the absolute conditional – which judges connection without considering relation – cannot be false when both are true or when antecedent false and consequent true, thus defending the standard view.

Key Terms
المتصلة (al-muttasila): The conditional (if-then) proposition in logic. [Note: Stable term.]
اللزومية (al-lūzumiyya): An implicative conditional where the connection is necessary due to a relation. [Note: No significant shift.]
الاتفاقية (al-ittifāqiyya): A coincidental conditional where the connection is merely accidental, no necessary relation. [Note: Some later texts distinguish between ittifāqiyya and mursala (loose); this text uses ittifāqiyya as a technical term.]
المقدّم (al-muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional. [Note: Stable.]
التالي (al-tālī): The consequent of a conditional. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, for an ittifāqiyya conditional to be true when both parts are true, what additional condition is required?
A) The antecedent must be true.
B) The consequent must be necessary.
C) There must be no relation between the parts that would imply implication.
D) The parts must be analytically related.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions have precise truth conditions that vary based on whether the connection is necessary (lūzumiyya) or coincidental (ittifāqiyya); for the latter, absence of any implicational relation is also required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the conditional 'If the sun rises, then the prayer is valid.' Determine its truth value for each of the four truth combinations assuming it is lūzumiyya. Then, assuming it is ittifāqiyya, determine its truth values. Does the truth of the conditional change depending on the type?
?
Find an example of a conditional in the Quran (e.g., Surah Al-Baqarah, 2:177: 'If you are in doubt...') and classify it as lūzumiyya or ittifāqiyya. Explain your reasoning based on the truth conditions from this passage.

Truth Conditions of the Three Disjunctive Types
Author defines truth conditions for three types of exclusive disjunction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of munfasila (ḥaqīqiyya, māni‘a al-jam‘, māni‘a al-khuluw) by their truth conditions.

Core Idea
Three truth tables govern disjunction: exclusive or, not-both, and not-neither.

In this lesson, we break down the core passage where the author defines the truth conditions for the affirmative real disjunction, exclusive-of-union, and exclusive-of-void. Understanding these will let you analyze any disjunctive statement in Islamic logic, such as rulings or theological claims.

Guided Path
1
List the three possible truth-value pairs
For two propositions, there are four combinations: both true, both false, first true/second false, first false/second true. The disjunction concerns the three that exclude the other? Actually, the author enumerates three: both true, both false, one true and one false. So note these three cases.
The fourth combination is just the mirror of the third; the order doesn't matter here.
2
Map each disjunction type to its true pairs
For the real (ḥaqīqiyya) affirmative: true only when exactly one is true (third case). For māni‘a al-jam‘: true when not both true, so true for both false and one true/one false. For māni‘a al-khuluw: true when not both false, so true for both true and one true/one false.
Memorize which types allow which pairs.
3
Identify the negative cases
The negative of each type is true where the affirmative is false, and false where it is true. So the negative real disjunction is true when both true or both false, etc.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider: 'The world is either temporally originated or eternal.' Since both cannot be true (tawḥīd) and both cannot be false (must be one), it is a real disjunction. If we say 'The act is either obligatory or permissible', both could be false (it could be prohibited), so it is not ḥaqīqiyya but could be māni‘a al-jam‘.
Test with other examples to solidify.

أقسام القضايا الشرطية [−1"] قال: والمنفصلة الموجبة الحقيقيّة تصدق عن صادق وكاذب وتكذب عن صادقين وكاذبين. ومانعة الجمع تصدق عن كاذبين و عن صادق و كاذب وتكذب عن صادقين. ومانعة الخلو تصدق عن صادقين وعن صادق وكاذب وتكذب عن كاذبين والسالبة تصدق عما تكذب عنه الموجبة وتكذب عمّا تصدق عنه الموجبة. القول: الأقسام في المنفصلات ثلاثة لما ستعرف أن المقدم فيها لا يمتاز عن التالي بحسب الطبع، فطرفاها إما أن يكونا صادقين أو كاذبين أو يكون أحدهما صادقاً والآخر كاذباً ؛ فالموجبة الحقيقية تصدق عن وكاذب، لأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم اجتماع جزأيها وعدم ارتفاعهما، صادق (۱) الموجبة الحقيقية العنادية لما وجب تركيبها من جزأين يمتنع صدقهما وكذبهما معاً : وجب أن يكون تركيبها من قضية ومن نقيضها أومساوى نقيضها، كقولنا : «هذا العدد إما زوج وإما لازوج وقولنا : «هذا العدد إما زوج وإما فرد». والمانعة الجمع العنادية لما وجب تركيبها من جزأين يمتنع صدقهما : فقد وجب أن يكون تركيبها من قضيّة ومما هو أخص من نقيضها، كقولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر، فإن كل واحد من الشجر والحجر أخص من نقيض الآخر، والمانعة الخلو العنادية لما وجب تركيبها من جزأين يمتنع كذبهما فقط : وجب أن يكون تركيبها من قضية ومما هو أعم من نقيضها، كقولنا : هذا الشيء إما لا شجر و إما لاحجر» فإنّ كلا منهما أعم من نقيض الآخر. هذا إذا أخذناهما بالمعنى الأخص، وإما إذا اعتبرناهما بالمعنى الأعم
The affirmative real disjunctive is true with one true and one false and false with both true and both false. The exclusive-of-union (māni‘a al-jam‘) is true with both false and with one true and one false, and false with both true. The exclusive-of-void (māni‘a al-khuluw) is true with both true and with one true and one false, and false with both false. And the negative is true where the affirmative is false and false where the affirmative is true. The statement: The divisions of disjunctives are three, because you will know that the antecedent in them is not distinguished from the consequent by nature; so its two parts are either both true, both false, or one true and the other false. So the affirmative real is true with one true and one false, because it is that which judges the non-union of its two parts and the non-removal [of both]. (1) The contrarian (ʿinādi) real affirmative must be composed of two parts impossible to be both true and both false together: so it must be composed of a proposition and its contradictory or equivalent of its contradictory, as in our saying: 'This number is either even or not even' and our saying: 'This number is either even or odd.' And the contrarian exclusive-of-union must be composed of two parts that cannot both be true: so it must be composed of a proposition and something more specific than its contradictory, as in our saying: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone,' for each of tree and stone is more specific than the contradictory of the other. And the contrarian exclusive-of-void must be composed of two parts that cannot both be false only: so it must be composed of a proposition and something more general than its contradictory, as in our saying: 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone,' for each of them is more general than the contradictory of the other. This is when we take them in the most specific sense; but if we consider them in the more general sense...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for disjunctive (munfasil) propositions. The author first enumerates the three types: (1) al-munfasila al-ḥaqīqiyya (real disjunction), which is true only when exactly one part is true; (2) māni‘a al-jam‘ (exclusive of union — equivalent to 'not both'), which is true when not both are true, i.e., false only when both true; (3) māni‘a al-khuluw (exclusive of void — equivalent to 'at least one'), which is true when not both false, i.e., false only when both false. The negative of each is simply the opposite. The author then explains that the parts are not naturally distinguished, so the three possible truth-value pairs are both true, both false, one true/one false. The affirmative real disjunction is true in the third case only. Then a footnote (1) introduces a deeper classification: the 'contrarian' (ʿinādi) sense of these disjunctions, which specifies the logical relationship required for each type. For the real disjunction, the parts must be contradictories or equivalent to contradictories. For māni‘a al-jam‘, one part must be more specific than the contradictory of the other. For māni‘a al-khuluw, one part must be more general than the contradictory of the other. Examples illustrate each: 'This number is either even or odd' (real, mutually exclusive and exhaustive), 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' (exclusive of union but not exhaustive, both can be false), 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' (exclusive of void, at least one false). The passage ends by noting that these are the 'more specific' senses.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) māni‘a al-jam‘
C) māni‘a al-khuluw
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The truth conditions of disjunctive propositions are not uniform; each of the three types determines a distinct pattern of when the disjunction holds, and knowing these is essential for logical analysis in Islamic sciences.

The Contrarian (ʿInādī) Classification of Disjunctives
Author explains contrarian sense with logical relations and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical relationship required between parts of a disjunction for each contrarian type: contradictory, more specific, or more general than the contradictory of the other.

Core Idea
In their contrarian sense, disjunctives require specific logical relationships: contradictories for real disjunction, specificity for exclusive-of-union, generality for exclusive-of-void.

The author deepens the analysis by introducing the 'contrarian' (ʿinādī) sense of disjunctives. This classification specifies the logical relationship that must hold between the two parts. A real disjunction must involve contradictories or their equivalents, while māni‘a al-jam‘ requires one part to be more specific than the contradictory of the other, and māni‘a al-khuluw requires one part to be more general than the contradictory of the other. This lesson unpacks these relationships with examples from the source.

Guided Path
1
Understand the logical relations: contradictory, more specific, more general
Recall that two propositions are contradictories if they always have opposite truth values. A proposition P is more specific than Q if P implies Q but Q does not imply P. P is more general than Q if Q implies P but P does not imply Q.
These relations are crucial for the ʿinādī classification.
2
Analyze the contrarian real disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya ʿinādiyya)
The author states that the parts must be impossible to be both true and both false together. This forces them to be contradictories or equivalent to contradictories. Example: 'This number is either even or odd' – these are contradictories (every integer is either even or odd, and not both).
If the parts are not contradictories, the disjunction cannot be a real disjunction in the contrarian sense.
3
Analyze the contrarian māni‘a al-jam‘
The parts cannot both be true, but they can both be false. For this, one part must be more specific than the contradictory of the other. Example: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone' – 'tree' is more specific than 'not stone' (since 'not stone' includes trees and many other things), and similarly 'stone' is more specific than 'not tree'. Thus both cannot be true (a thing cannot be both tree and stone), but both can be false (it could be a mineral).
The relationship is: each part is more specific than the contradictory of the other.
4
Analyze the contrarian māni‘a al-khuluw
The parts cannot both be false, but they can both be true. For this, one part must be more general than the contradictory of the other. Example: 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone' – 'not a tree' is more general than 'stone' (since 'not a tree' includes stones and everything else), and 'not a stone' is more general than 'tree'. Thus both cannot be false (if both were false, it would be both a tree and a stone, impossible), but both can be true (e.g., a mineral is neither tree nor stone, so both 'not a tree' and 'not a stone' are true).
The relationship here is: each part is more general than the contradictory of the other.

Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions: Real, Mani'at al-Jam', Mani'at al-Khuluw
Author classifies disjunctive propositions into three types with truth conditions and examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three types of disjunctive propositions and determine their truth conditions from a given example.

Core Idea
The three disjunctives cover all combinations of truth values: exactly one true, not both true, not both false.

In this lesson, we examine the classification of disjunctive propositions into three types: the real (haqiqiyya), the lacking-collection (mani'at al-jam'), and the lacking-exclusion (mani'at al-khuluw). Each has distinct truth conditions that are essential for understanding how disjunctive reasoning works in Islamic logic, especially in juristic and theological arguments. The passage gives concrete examples to illustrate each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types in the text
Find the Arabic terms for the real, lacking-collection, and lacking-exclusion disjunctives in the passage. Note that the author introduces them systematically.
The terms are: حقيقية, مانعة الجمع, مانعة الخلو.
2
Extract truth conditions for each type
For each type, write down when it is true (تصدق) and when it is false (تكذب) based on the truth values of its two parts. Use the author's explicit statements.
Real: true when one true one false; false when both true or both false. Lacking-collection: true when both false or one true one false; false when both true. Lacking-exclusion: true when both true or one true one false; false when both false.
3
Analyze the examples given
Match the examples like 'even or odd' (real), 'tree or stone' (lacking-collection), and 'not a tree or not a stone' (lacking-exclusion) to their types. Verify that the truth conditions hold for each example.
Consider whether both parts can be true or false together for each example.
4
Understand the 'oppositional' subtypes
The passage discusses 'anadiyya (oppositional) versions where the parts are related as opposites. Note how the composition involves a proposition and its negation or something more specific/general.
This deepens the understanding of why the truth conditions apply.
5
Apply truth tables mentally
Create a truth table for each type with two propositions p and q. Verify that the truth conditions match the author's description.
Use p and q as placeholders for the two parts.

قال: والمنفصلة الموجبة الحقيقيّة تصدق عن صادق وكاذب وتكذب عن صادقين وكاذبين. ومانعة الجمع تصدق عن كاذبين و عن صادق و كاذب وتكذب عن صادقين. ومانعة الخلو تصدق عن صادقين وعن صادق وكاذب وتكذب عن كاذبين والسالبة تصدق عما تكذب عنه الموجبة وتكذب عمّا تصدق عنه الموجبة. القول: الأقسام في المنفصلات ثلاثة لما ستعرف أن المقدم فيها لا يمتاز عن التالي بحسب الطبع، فطرفاها إما أن يكونا صادقين أو كاذبين أو يكون صدقهما : فقد وجب أن يكون تركيبها من قضيّة ومما هو أخص من نقيضها، كقولنا هذا الشيء إما شجر وإما حجر، فإن كل واحد من الشجر والحجر أخص من نقيض الآخر، والمانعة الخلو العنادية لما وجب تركيبها من جزأين يمتنع كذبهما فقط : وجب أن يكون تركيبها من قضية ومما هو أعم من نقيضها، كقولنا : هذا الشيء إما لا شجر و إما لاحجر» فإنّ كلا منهما أعم من نقيض الآخر. هذا إذا أخذناهما بالمعنى الأخص، وإما إذا اعتبرناهما بالمعنى الأعم فلابد أن يكون أحدهما صادقاً والآخر كاذباً ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو لا زوجاً وتكذب عن صادقين لاجتماعهما حينئذ في الصدق، كقولنا : «إمّا أن يكون الأربعة زوجاً أو منقسمة بمتساويين» وتكذب عن كاذبين أيضاً لارتفاعهما ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون الثلاثة زوجاً أو منقسمة بمتساويين» ومانعة الجمع تصدق عن كاذبين وصادق وكاذب، لأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم اجتماع طرفيها في الصدق، فجاز أن يكون طرفاها مرتفعين فيكون تركيبها عن كاذبين كقولنا : إما أن يكون زيد شجراً أو حجراً» وجاز أن يكون أحد طرفيها واقعاً والآخر غير واقع، فيكون تركيبها عن صادق وكاذب، كقولنا : إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حجراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين لاجتماع جزأيها حينئذ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً». ومانعة الخلو تصدق عن صادقين وعن صادق وكاذب، لأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم ارتفاع جزأيها فجاز اجتماعهما في الوجود فيكون تركيبها عن صادقين، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا حجراً أو لا شجراً» وجاز أن يكون أحدهما واقعا دون الآخر، فيكون تركيبها عن صادق وكاذب، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا حجراً أو لا إنساناً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين لارتفاع جزأيها حينئذ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا إنساناً أو لا ناطقاً». فيصدق كل واحد منها تمامر وتما يتركب منه الحقيقية (شريف).
He said: The affirmative real disjunctive is true when one part is true and the other false, and false when both are true or both false. The lacking-collection disjunctive is true when both are false or one true and one false, and false when both are true. The lacking-exclusion disjunctive is true when both are true or one true and one false, and false when both are false. The negative is true where the affirmative is false, and false where the affirmative is true. The statement: The divisions of disjunctives are three because you will know that the antecedent does not differ from the consequent by nature; thus its two sides are either both true, both false, or one true and the other false. The affirmative real is true when one is true and the other false, because it is ruled to have neither both parts true nor both false. (1) The oppositional real affirmative, since it must be composed of two parts that cannot both be true and cannot both be false, must be composed of a proposition and its contradiction or equivalent of its contradiction, like our saying: 'This number is either even or not even' and our saying: 'This number is either even or odd'. The oppositional lacking-collection, since it must be composed of two parts that cannot both be true, must be composed of a proposition and something more specific than its contradiction, like our saying: 'This thing is either a tree or a stone', for each of tree and stone is more specific than the contradiction of the other. The oppositional lacking-exclusion, since it must be composed of two parts that cannot both be false only, must be composed of a proposition and something more general than its contradiction, like our saying: 'This thing is either not a tree or not a stone', for each of them is more general than the contradiction of the other. This is if we take them in the more specific sense; but if we consider them in the more general sense, then one must be true and the other false, like our saying: 'This number is either even or not even', and it is false when both are true because they then combine in truth, like our saying: 'Either four is even or divisible into two equals', and it is false when both are false because they are both non-existent, like our saying: 'Either three is even or divisible into two equals'. The lacking-collection is true when both are false or one true and one false, because it is ruled that its two sides cannot both be true. So it is possible that both sides are non-existent, so its composition is from two false statements, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is a tree or a stone', and it is possible that one side occurs and the other does not, so its composition is from a true and a false statement, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is a human or a stone', and it is false when both are true because its parts then combine, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is a human or a speaker'. The lacking-exclusion is true when both are true or one true and one false, because it is ruled that its two parts cannot both be non-existent. So it is possible that they coexist in existence, so its composition is from two true statements, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a stone or not a tree', and it is possible that one occurs without the other, so its composition is from a true and a false statement, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a stone or not a human', and it is false when both are false because its parts are then non-existent, like our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a human or not a speaker'. So each one is true completely and what composes the real (Sharif).
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of affirmative disjunctive propositions and their truth conditions. It begins by stating the truth conditions for each type: the real (haqiqiyya) is true when exactly one part is true; the lacking-collection (mani'at al-jam') is true when not both parts are true (so both false or one true one false); the lacking-exclusion (mani'at al-khuluw) is true when not both parts are false (so both true or one true one false). The negative of each is true where the affirmative is false. The author then explains that the three types arise because the two parts of a disjunctive can be both true, both false, or one true one false. The real covers only the case of exactly one true; the others cover the remaining possibilities. The text then goes into a more detailed discussion of 'oppositional' types ('anadiyya), which are special cases of these types where the parts are related as opposites. For example, the oppositional real is composed of a proposition and its negation (e.g., even/not even). The opposing lacking-collection has parts that cannot both be true, so they are more specific than each other's negations. The opposing lacking-exclusion has parts that cannot both be false, so they are more general. The author gives examples: for lacking-collection, 'tree or stone'—both cannot be true of one thing (something cannot be both a tree and a stone), but both can be false (it could be a human); for lacking-exclusion, 'not a tree or not a stone'—both cannot be false (if something is a tree, it is not a stone, so one is true), but both can be true (if it is a human, it is neither a tree nor a stone). The passage then explains that when taken in the 'more general sense', the real disjunctive simply requires one true and one false, and gives examples like 'even or not even'. Finally, it reiterates the truth conditions for each type with concrete examples: for lacking-collection, 'Zayd is a tree or a stone' (true when both false or one true one false); for lacking-exclusion, 'Zayd is not a stone or not a tree' (true when both true or one true one false). The examples use Zayd and natural kinds, which are consistent with Islamic thought.

Key Terms
منفصلة
حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

C) Mani'at al-khuluw (lacking-exclusion)
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
The truth conditions of the three disjunctive types determine when a disjunctive proposition is true: exactly one true (real), not both true (mani'at al-jam'), not both false (mani'at al-khuluw).

Three Types of Disjunctive Conditionals
Author classifies disjunctive conditionals into three types based on their truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive conditionals (man'at al-jam', man'at al-khuluw, al-haqiqiyya) and determine their truth conditions.

Core Idea
Disjunctive conditionals are defined by what they prohibit: coexistence, emptiness, or both.

In this lesson, we examine the key classification of disjunctive propositions based on their truth conditions. The author identifies three types: the preventing of coexistence (man'at al-jam'), the preventing of emptiness (man'at al-khuluw), and the true disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) which combines both. Understanding these distinctions is essential for evaluating arguments that use 'or' statements, common in Islamic legal reasoning and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three disjunction types
From the passage, list the three types: man'at al-jam', man'at al-khuluw, and al-haqiqiyya. Note that the first two are defined by a single restriction, while the third combines both.
2
Understand the truth condition for each type
For each type, determine when the disjunction is true: man'at al-jam' is true unless both sides are true; man'at al-khuluw is true unless both are false; al-haqiqiyya is true only when exactly one side is true.
3
Apply the conditions to given examples
Test the examples in the passage against these conditions. For instance, verify why 'Zayd is a human or a stone' is true for man'at al-jam' but would be false for al-haqiqiyya.
4
Connect to Islamic reasoning
Recognize that in Islamic jurisprudence, disjunctive statements often appear in rulings, e.g., 'The act is either wajib or mubah.' Distinguishing the type of disjunction affects the inference that can be drawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلابد أن يكون أحدهما صادقاً والآخر كاذباً ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون هذا العدد زوجاً أو لا زوجاً وتكذب عن صادقين لاجتماعهما حينئذ في الصدق، كقولنا : «إمّا أن يكون الأربعة زوجاً أو منقسمة بمتساويين» وتكذب عن كاذبين أيضاً لارتفاعهما ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون الثلاثة زوجاً أو منقسمة بمتساويين» ومانعة الجمع تصدق عن كاذبين وصادق وكاذب، لأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم اجتماع طرفيها في الصدق، فجاز أن يكون طرفاها مرتفعين فيكون تركيبها عن كاذبين كقولنا : إما أن يكون زيد شجراً أو حجراً» وجاز أن يكون أحد طرفيها واقعاً والآخر غير واقع، فيكون تركيبها عن صادق وكاذب، كقولنا : إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو حجراً»، وتكذب عن صادقين لاجتماع جزأيها حينئذ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد إنساناً أو ناطقاً». ومانعة الخلو تصدق عن صادقين وعن صادق وكاذب، لأنها التي حكم فيها بعدم ارتفاع جزأيها فجاز اجتماعهما في الوجود فيكون تركيبها عن صادقين، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا حجراً أو لا شجراً» وجاز أن يكون أحدهما واقعا دون الآخر، فيكون تركيبها عن صادق وكاذب، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا حجراً أو لا إنساناً»، وتكذب عن كاذبين لارتفاع جزأيها حينئذ، كقولنا : «إما أن يكون زيد لا إنساناً أو لا ناطقاً». فيصدق كل واحد منها تمامر وتما يتركب منه الحقيقية (شريف).
So one of them must be true and the other false, as in our saying: 'Either this number is even or it is not even'. And it is false from two truths because they would then both be true, as in our saying: 'Either four is even or divisible by two equals'. And it is false from two falsehoods as well due to their nonexistence, as in our saying: 'Either three is even or divisible by two equals'. And the preventing of coexistence (man'at al-jam') is true from two falsehoods and from one true and one false, because it is that in which it is judged that its two sides cannot both be true, so it is possible that both sides are absent, thus its composition is from two falsehoods, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is a tree or a stone'; and it is possible that one side occurs and the other does not, so its composition is from one true and one false, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is a human or a stone'; and it is false from two truths because then its two parts would both be true, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is a human or a speaker'. And the preventing of emptiness (man'at al-khuluw) is true from two truths and from one true and one false, because it is that in which it is judged that its two parts cannot both be absent, so it is possible that they both exist, thus its composition is from two truths, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a stone or not a tree'; and it is possible that one occurs without the other, so its composition is from one true and one false, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a stone or not a human'; and it is false from two falsehoods because then both parts would be absent, as in our saying: 'Either Zayd is not a human or not a speaker'. So each one of them is true completely? and from them the real disjunction is composed.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive conditional propositions into three types based on their truth conditions. The first type is the true disjunction (man'at al-jam' wa al-khuluw), which requires exactly one side to be true. The author gives an example: 'Either this number is even or it is not even.' This is a logical contradiction where one must be true and the other false. He then shows that if both sides were true (e.g., 'Four is even or divisible by two equals'), the disjunction would be false because both are true. Similarly, if both are false (e.g., 'Three is even or divisible by two equals'), it is false. So the true disjunction is true only when one is true and the other false. The second type is man'at al-jam' (preventing conjunction), which only forbids both being true together. Thus it is true when both are false (e.g., 'Zayd is a tree or a stone') and when one is true and the other false (e.g., 'Zayd is a human or a stone'), but false when both are true (e.g., 'Zayd is a human or a speaker' because Zayd is both a human and speaking). The third type is man'at al-khuluw (preventing emptiness), which only forbids both being false together. Thus it is true when both are true (e.g., 'Zayd is not a stone or not a tree' — both statements are true since Zayd is neither) and when one is true and the other false (e.g., 'Zayd is not a stone or not a human' — the first is true, second false), but false when both are false (e.g., 'Zayd is not a human or not a speaker' — both are false because Zayd is both human and speaking). The final sentence indicates that from these two types (man'at al-jam' and man'at al-khuluw) the real disjunction (al-haqiqiyya) is composed, meaning the true exclusive 'or' combines both restrictions. All examples use everyday objects like numbers and persons (Zayd) to illustrate logical principles.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو
الحقيقية

Key Takeaway
The three types of disjunctive conditionals are distinguished by what they prohibit: man'at al-jam' prohibits only both-true, man'at al-khuluw prohibits only both-false, and al-haqiqiyya prohibits both, requiring exactly one true.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Defining Universal, Particular, and Specified Conditionals
Defining the three quantities of conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between universal, particular, and specified conditional propositions based on the modes of the antecedent.

Core Idea
A universal conditional holds for all possible modes of the antecedent; a particular for some; a specified for a specific mode.

In categorical propositions, quantity is about the subject. But in conditionals, quantity is about the modes of the antecedent. This passage defines the three types and also explains the relation between affirmative and negative conditionals. Mastering this helps you correctly interpret conditional statements in Islamic texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the negation relation
Note the text says the negative conditional is true exactly where the affirmative is false, and vice versa. This follows the law of excluded middle for conditionals.
This is a foundational principle for conditional propositions.
2
Define universal conditional
A universal affirmative conditional is one where the consequent is always true (or always contrary) for every possible mode of the antecedent. The modes are the states the antecedent can take when combined with other compatible circumstances.
The universality is about the relation, not the antecedent term itself.
3
Define particular and specified
A particular conditional holds for some modes; a specified conditional holds for exactly one specific mode.
Particular uses 'some', specified uses 'a specific'.
4
Recognize universality of judgment
The text emphasizes that universality in conditionals is about the judgment (the relation) applying to all modes, not about the antecedent being universal in term logic.
This parallels the categorical case where universality is about the subject-predicate relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية هذا حكم الموجبات المتصلة والمنفصلة، وأما سوالبها فهي تصدق عن الأقسام التي تكذب عنها الموجبات، ضرورة أن كذب الإيجاب يقتضي صدق السلب، وتكذب عن الأقسام التي تصدق عنها الموجبات، لأن صدق الإيجاب يقتضي كذب السلب لامحالة. قال: وكلية الشرطية الموجبة أن يكون التالي لازماً أو معانداً للمقدم على جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن حصوله عليها، وهي الأوضاع التي تحصل له بسبب اقتران الأمور التي يمكن اجتماعها معه. والجزئية أن يكون كذلك على بعض هذه الأوضاع. والمخصوصة أن يكون كذلك على وضع معين. وسور الموجبة الكلية في المتصلة كلما» و «مهما» و «متى» وفي المنفصلة «دائماً». وسور السالبة الكلية فيهما «ليس البتة». وسور الموجبة الجزئية فيهما «قد يكون». والسالبة الجزئية فيهما «قد لايكون»، ويإدخال حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي، والمهملة بإطلاق لفظ «لو» و«إن» و«إذا» في المتصلة، و «اما» و «أو» في المنفصلة. القول: كما أن القضية الحمليّة تنقسم إلى محصورة ومهملة و مخصوصة، كذلك الشرطيّة منقسمة إليها ؛ وكما أن كلية الحملية ليست بحسب كلية الموضوع أو المحمول - بل باعتبار كلية الحكم - كذلك كلية
Sections of conditional propositions. This is the ruling for affirmative conjunctive and disjunctive [propositions]. As for their negatives, they are true about the cases where the affirmatives are false, necessarily because the falsehood of the affirmation necessitates the truth of the negation, and they are false about the cases where the affirmatives are true, because the truth of the affirmation necessarily necessitates the falsehood of the negation. He said: The universal affirmative conditional is that the consequent is a necessary consequent or contrary to the antecedent in all the modes that the antecedent can possibly have, which are the modes that occur to it due to the conjunction of things that can coexist with it. The particular is that it is so in some of these modes. The specified is that it is so in a specific mode. The quantifier of the universal affirmative in the conjunctive is 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata', and in the disjunctive 'da'iman'. The quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-battata'. The quantifier of the particular affirmative in both is 'qad yakun'. The particular negative in both is 'qad la yakun', by entering the negation particle on the universal affirmative quantifier. The indefinite is by uttering 'law', 'in', 'idha' in the conjunctive, and 'amma', 'aw' in the disjunctive. The statement: Just as the categorical proposition is divided into quantified, indefinite, and specified, likewise the conditional is divided into them; and just as the universality of the categorical is not according to the universality of the subject or predicate, but rather according to the universality of the judgment, likewise the universality...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage first concludes the discussion on the negatives of conditionals: the negative of a conditional is true exactly where the affirmative is false, and vice versa, following the law of non-contradiction. Then it moves to defining the universal, particular, and specified conditionals. A universal conditional (kulliya) is one in which the relationship between antecedent and consequent (either necessary consequence in conjunctive or mutual exclusion in disjunctive) holds for all possible modes (awda') of the antecedent. The modes are the states the antecedent can take when combined with other compatible circumstances. A particular conditional holds for some of those modes, and a specified conditional holds for a specific mode. The quantifiers (sur) for each type are listed: for universal affirmative conjunctive: كلما (kullama), مهما (mahma), متى (mata); for disjunctive: دائماً (da'iman). For universal negative: ليس البتة (laysa al-battata). For particular affirmative: قد يكون (qad yakun). For particular negative: قد لايكون (qad la yakun). The indefinite (muhmala) uses لو (law), إن (in), إذا (idha) for conjunctive, and إما (amma) or أو (aw) for disjunctive. Finally, the author notes that just like categoricals, conditionals are divided into quantified, indefinite, and specified, and the universality of a conditional is not because of the universality of the terms but because of the universality of the judgment, similar to categoricals. This guards against a common misunderstanding that the universal conditional requires the subject term to be universal; rather, it is the judgment (the relation) that applies to all instances.

Key Terms
الشرطية
الكلية
الجزئية
المخصوصة

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions have three quantities: universal (all modes), particular (some modes), and specified (one specific mode).

Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions
Listing the quantifiers for conditional propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to match the correct quantifier particle to each type of conditional proposition (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, indefinite).

Core Idea
Each quantity of conditional has a specific quantifier particle: all universal affirmatives use كلما for conjunctive and دائماً for disjunctive, etc.

Just as categorical propositions have quantifiers like 'all' and 'some', conditionals have their own particles. Memorizing these allows you to quickly assess the scope of a conditional statement. This passage lists all necessary quantifiers.

Guided Path
1
Learn universal affirmative quantifiers
For conjunctive conditionals, the universal affirmative uses the particles 'كلما', 'مهما', 'متى'. For disjunctive conditionals, it uses 'دائماً'.
2
Learn universal negative quantifier
Both types use 'ليس البتة' for the universal negative.
This means 'absolutely not'.
3
Learn particular affirmative and negative quantifiers
The particular affirmative is 'قد يكون' (it may be) and the particular negative is 'قد لايكون' (it may not be), which is formed by adding the negation particle to the affirmative quantifier.
4
Learn indefinite markers
Indefinite conditionals are signalled by the particles 'لو', 'إن', 'إذا' for conjunctive, and 'أما', 'أو' for disjunctive.
Indefinite means no quantification is specified; these particles are used without an explicit quantifier.

D) قد يكون

Key Takeaway
Each quantity of conditional has a specific quantifier particle; the indefinite uses particles like لو, إن, إذا for conjunctive and أما, أو for disjunctive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the following conditional statement in Arabic: 'إذا طلع الفجر فقد وجب الصوم.' Identify the quantifier and determine whether it is universal or particular. (Answer: The particle 'إذا' indicates an indefinite conditional, so it is not quantified; you cannot assume universality.)
?
Construct a universal affirmative disjunctive conditional using the correct quantifier on a topic from fiqh. Example: 'إما أن يصلي أو لا يصلي، دائمًا يجب عليه القضاء.' (But this might be forced; better to just match quantifiers.) Alternatively, list the quantifier for each type from memory.

8 min
Types and Quantifiers of Conditional Propositions
Defines quantification of conditionals and lists quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional proposition as universal, particular, or specified and match it to its correct quantifier.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are quantified by the scope of their implication or opposition.

Just as categorical propositions are quantified by 'all' and 'some', conditionals have their own quantifiers indicating how broadly the relation between antecedent and consequent holds. In this lesson, you will learn the three types—universal, particular, and specified—and the Arabic particles that express them.

Guided Path
1
Relate affirmation and negation
Note that the negative of a conditional is true exactly where the affirmative is false, and false where the affirmative is true. This is the principle of contradiction applied to conditionals.
This applies to both connected and disjunctive conditionals.
2
Define universal conditional
A universal affirmative conditional holds when the consequent is a necessary concomitant (luzum) of the antecedent in all possible states of the antecedent. For disjunctive conditionals, it requires that the disjuncts are completely opposed (inad).
The states are all the conditions that can be combined with the antecedent.
3
Define particular and specified conditionals
A particular conditional holds in some (at least one) of the antecedent's states. A specified conditional holds in exactly one particular state.
Some later logicians treat specified as a subtype of particular.
4
Memorize the quantifiers
Universal affirmative connected: 'kullama', 'mahma', 'mata'; universal affirmative disjunctive: 'da'iman'. Universal negative both: 'laysa al-battata'. Particular affirmative both: 'qad yakun'. Particular negative both: 'qad la yakun'. Unrestricted connected: 'law', 'in', 'idha'; unrestricted disjunctive: 'amma', 'aw'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقسام القضايا الشرطية
هذا حكم الموجبات المتصلة والمنفصلة، وأما سوالبها فهي تصدق عن الأقسام التي تكذب عنها الموجبات، ضرورة أن كذب الإيجاب يقتضي صدق السلب، وتكذب عن الأقسام التي تصدق عنها الموجبات، لأن صدق الإيجاب يقتضي كذب السلب لامحالة.
- الكلية والجزئية والمخصوصة في الشرطيات
قال: وكلية الشرطية الموجبة أن يكون التالي لازماً أو معانداً للمقدم على جميع الأوضاع التي يمكن حصوله عليها، وهي الأوضاع التي تحصل له بسبب اقتران الأمور التي يمكن اجتماعها معه.
والجزئية أن يكون كذلك على بعض هذه الأوضاع.
والمخصوصة أن يكون كذلك على وضع معين.
وسور الموجبة الكلية في المتصلة «كلما» و «مهما» و «متى» وفي المنفصلة «دائماً».
وسور السالبة الكلية فيهما «ليس البتة».
وسور الموجبة الجزئية فيهما «قد يكون».
والسالبة الجزئية فيهما «قد لايكون»، ويدخل حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي، والمهملة بإطلاق لفظ «لو» و«إن» و«إذا» في المتصلة، و «أما» و «أو» في المنفصلة.
القول: كما أن القضية الحمليّة تنقسم إلى محصورة ومهملة و مخصوصة، كذلك الشرطيّة منقسمة إليها؛ وكما أن كلية الحملية ليست بحسب كلية الموضوع أو المحمول - بل باعتبار كلية الحكم - كذلك كلية
الشرطية ليست لأجل أن مقدّمها أوتاليها كلّي - فإن قولنا : «كلما كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرّك يده» كلّية، مع أن مقدّمها وتاليها شخصيّان - بل بحسب كلية الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال، فالشرطية إنما تكون كلية إذا كان التالي لازماً للمقدم - أي في المتصلة اللزومية – أو معانداً له - أي في المنفصلة العنادية - في جميع الأزمان وعلى جميع الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم، وهي الأوضاع التي تحصل للمقدم بسبب اقترانه بالأمور الممكنة الاجتماع معه، فإذا قلنا : «كلما كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً»
Sections of conditional propositions. This is the ruling of affirmative connected and disjunctive propositions. As for their negatives, they are true for the divisions for which the affirmatives are false, necessarily because the falsity of affirmation necessitates the truth of negation, and they are false for the divisions for which the affirmatives are true, because the truth of affirmation necessitates the falsity of negation without exception. Universal, particular, and specified in conditionals. He said: The universal affirmative conditional is that the consequent is a necessary concomitant or an opponent of the antecedent in all the states that can occur for it, and these are the states that occur for it due to its conjunction with things that can be combined with it. The particular is that it is so in some of these states. The specified is that it is so in a particular state. The quantifiers of the universal affirmative in the connected are 'kullama' and 'mahma' and 'mata', and in the disjunctive 'da'iman'. The quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-battata'. The quantifiers of the particular affirmative in both are 'qad yakun'. The particular negative in both is 'qad la yakun', and the particle of negation enters upon the quantifier of universal affirmative. The unrestricted (muhmala) is by the absolute utterance of 'law' and 'in' and 'idha' in the connected, and 'amma' and 'aw' in the disjunctive. Statement: Just as the categorical proposition is divided into quantified, unrestricted, and specified, so too the conditional is divided into these; and just as the universality of the categorical is not according to the universality of the subject or the predicate—rather by consideration of the universality of the judgment—so too the universality of the conditional is not because its antecedent or consequent is universal—for our saying 'Every time Zayd is writing, he is moving his hand' is universal, while its antecedent and consequent are particular—rather according to the universality of the judgment of connection or disconnection. The conditional is only universal if the consequent is a necessary concomitant of the antecedent—i.e. in the implicative connected—or an opponent of it—i.e. in the oppositional disjunctive—in all times and in all states possible to combine with the antecedent, which are the states that occur for the antecedent due to its conjunction with things possible to combine with it. So if we say: 'Every time Zayd is a human, he is an animal.'
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on the quantification of conditional propositions in logic. It begins by stating the relationship between affirmation and negation: negatives of conditionals are true where the corresponding affirmatives are false, and false where affirmatives are true, due to the law of non-contradiction. Then it defines three types of conditionals based on the scope of the implication or opposition: universal (kulliyya), where the consequent is a necessary concomitant or opponent of the antecedent in all possible states; particular (juz'iyya), where it holds in some states; and specified (mukhususa), where it holds in exactly one specified state. The quantifiers (sawar) are then listed: for universal affirmative in connected conditionals, 'kullama', 'mahma', 'muta'; in disjunctive, 'da'iman'. Universal negative in both: 'laysa al-battata'. Particular affirmative: 'qad yakun'. Particular negative: 'qad la yakun'. The unrestricted (muhmala) are indicated by words like 'law', 'in', 'idha' for connected, and 'amma', 'aw' for disjunctive. The passage then compares conditionals to categoricals: just as categorical universality is based on the judgment (hukm) not the subject or predicate terms, so too conditionals are universal not because antecedent or consequent are universal terms, but because the judgment of connection or disconnection applies universally to all instantiations of the antecedent. The example 'Every time Zayd is writing, he is moving his hand' is universal even though 'Zayd' is a particular term, because the judgment covers all times Zayd writes. The condition for universality is that the consequent must be a necessary concomitant (lazim) in implicative conditionals, or an opponent (muanid) in disjunctive conditionals, in all times and all states possible to combine with the antecedent. The states (awda') are defined as the conditions that arise from conjoining the antecedent with other possible things. The passage ends with an example: 'Every time Zayd is a human, he is an animal.'

Conditional propositions are quantified by the scope of the relation: universal (all states), particular (some states), or specified (one state), and each has specific Arabic quantifiers.

7 min
Universality of Judgment in Conditionals
Clarifies that universality of conditionals is in the judgment, not the terms, using the Zayd example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a conditional with particular terms can be universal in judgment, using the example of Zayd.

Core Idea
Universality in conditionals is about the judgment of connection, not the terms.

A common pitfall is to think a conditional is universal only if its antecedent or consequent contain universal terms. However, as the author explains, universality is a property of the judgment itself. We will analyze the example 'Every time Zayd writes, he moves his hand' to see how it can be universal despite 'Zayd' being a particular term.

Guided Path
1
Recall how categorical universality works
Just as a categorical proposition like 'Every man is mortal' is universal not because 'man' is a universal term but because the judgment covers all individuals of that kind, so too in conditionals the universality lies in the judgment of connection.
The author explicitly makes this analogy in the passage.
2
Examine the conditional about Zayd
The conditional 'Every time Zayd is writing, he is moving his hand' has a particular antecedent ('Zayd writes') and a particular consequent ('he moves his hand'). Yet it is considered universal.
The quantifier 'kullama' (every time) makes it universal in scope.
3
Identify the basis of universality
The universality comes from the fact that the judgment of connection applies to all possible instances of the antecedent: every time Zayd writes, he moves his hand. The relation is necessary concomitance (luzum) in all states.
The states are the various conditions under which Zayd writes (e.g., at different times, places).
4
Generalize the condition for universality
For any conditional to be universal, the consequent must be a necessary concomitant or opponent of the antecedent in all possible states of the antecedent, regardless of whether the terms are universal or particular.

10 min
What Makes a Conditional Proposition Universal?
Definition of universal conditional proposition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a conditional proposition is universal by examining the judgment of connection or disconnection, rather than the quantity of its antecedent or consequent parts.

Core Idea
A conditional is universal not because its parts are universal, but because the implication holds in all times and all possible states.

In categorical logic, a universal proposition is one where the predicate applies to all members of the subject. But in conditional propositions, a different criterion applies. The author of this passage corrects a common mistake: thinking that a conditional is universal if its antecedent or consequent is universal. Instead, he clarifies that universality is about the judgment of connection (or disconnection) being constant across all times and accompanying states. This distinction is crucial for assessing the validity of conditional arguments in Islamic sciences like kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the misconception
Note the author's starting point: universality of a conditional is not due to the antecedent or consequent being universal. The example 'Whenever Zayd is writing, he moves his hand' has particular parts yet is universal.
This shows universality is not about the quantity of the parts.
2
Analyze the counterexample
Break down the example: 'Zayd is writing' and 'he moves his hand' are both particular (juz'iyya), but the conditional as a whole is universal (kulliyya) because it claims a constant implication.
The implication holds for every instance of Zayd writing.
3
Extract the correct criterion
The author states universality is 'by virtue of the universality of the judgment of connection or disconnection' (bi hasab kulliyyat al-hukm bi al-ittisal wa al-infisal). This means the relationship (implication or opposition) must be constant.
For implicative (muttasila): consequent must be necessary for antecedent. For exclusive disjunctive (munfasila 'inadiyya): consequent must be opposed to antecedent.
4
Understand the 'states' (al-awda')
The judgment must hold not only in all times but also in all possible states that can accompany the antecedent. These are conditions that can coexist with the antecedent, like Zayd standing or sitting when he is human.
Impossible states are excluded because they would make the conditional false.
5
Apply to implicative vs disjunctive
For the implicative, the test is 'luzum' (necessity of consequent) in all times and states. For the disjunctive, it is 'inad' (opposition) in all such situations.
This distinction is important when analyzing conditional syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الشرطية ليست لأجل أن مقدّمها أوتاليها كلّي - فإن قولنا : «كلما كان زيد يكتب فهو يحرّك يده كلّية، مع أن مقدّمها وتاليها شخصيّان - بل بحسب كلية الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال، فالشرطية إنما تكون كلية إذا كان التالي لازماً للمقدم - أي في المتصلة اللزومية – أو معانداً له - أي في المنفصلة العنادية - في جميع الأزمان وعلى جميع الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم، وهي الأوضاع التي تحصل للمقدم بسبب اقترانه بالأمور الممكنة الاجتماع معه ، فإذا قلنا : «كلما كان زيد إنساناً كان حيواناً أردنا به أن لزوم الحيوانية للإنسانية ثابت في جميع الأزمان، ولسنا نقتصر على ذلك القدر، بل نزيد مع ذلك أن اللزوم متحقق على جميع الأحوال التي أمكن اجتماعها مع وضع إنسانية زيد مثل كونه قائماً أو قاعداً أو كون الشمس طالعة أو كون الحمار ناهقاً - إلى غير ذلك ما لا يتناهى - وإنما اعتبر في الأوضاع أن تكون ممكنة الاجتماع لأنه لو اعتبر جميع الأوضاع مطلقاً - سواء كانت ممكنة الاجتماع أو لا تكون – لم تصدق شرطية كلية
The conditional [proposition] is not [universal] because its antecedent or consequent is universal - for our saying: 'Whenever Zayd is writing, he moves his hand' is universal even though its antecedent and consequent are particular - but rather by virtue of the universality of the judgment of connection or disconnection. So the conditional is universal only if the consequent is necessary for the antecedent - i.e., in the implicative conditional - or opposed to it - i.e., in the exclusive disjunctive - in all times and in all states possible to combine with the antecedent. And those are the states that occur for the antecedent due to its conjunction with the things possible to combine with it. So when we say: 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal' we mean by it that the necessity of animality for humanity is established in all times, and we do not restrict ourselves to that measure, but we add to that that the necessity is realized in all states that it is possible to combine with the state of Zayd's humanity, such as him standing or sitting, or the sun rising, or the donkey braying - and so on ad infinitum. And we only considered in the states that they be possible to combine because if we considered all states absolutely - whether they are possible to combine or not - a universal conditional would not be true.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage defines the universal conditional proposition (al-shartiyya al-kulliyya). The author begins by refuting a common misconception: that a conditional is universal because its antecedent or consequent is universal. He provides a counterexample: 'Whenever Zayd is writing, he moves his hand' is a universal conditional, yet both 'Zayd is writing' and 'he moves his hand' are particular propositions. Therefore, universality does not derive from the parts. Instead, the correct criterion is that the judgment of connection (al-ittisal) or disconnection (al-infisal) must be universal. For the implicative conditional (al-muttasila al-luzumiyya), this means the consequent is necessary for the antecedent in all times and in all possible states that can accompany the antecedent. For the exclusive disjunctive (al-munfasila al-'inadiyya), the consequent must be opposed to the antecedent in all such states. The author then explains what he means by 'states' (al-awda'): they are the conditions that occur for the antecedent due to its conjunction with things that are possible to combine with it. For example, when we say 'Whenever Zayd is human, he is an animal,' the necessity of animality for humanity must hold not only in all times but also in all states that can accompany Zayd's humanity, such as Zayd standing or sitting, the sun rising, a donkey braying, and so on. Finally, the author justifies why only states possible to combine are considered: if we considered all states, including impossible ones, a universal conditional would never be true. For instance, if the antecedent is false in some state, the implication may fail. This definition is essential for understanding how logicians evaluate the validity of conditional propositions in arguments.

Key Terms
شرطية
كلية

Key Takeaway
A conditional proposition is universal if and only if the judgment of implication (luzum) or opposition (inad) holds for all times and all possible states accompanying the antecedent.

?
