Why Universal Conditionals Exclude Accidental (Ittifaqiyya)
Author explains why universal conditional applies only to lizumiyya and inadadiyya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which types of conditional propositions can sustain a universal quantification and explain why accidental conditionals cannot.

Core Idea
Universal conditionals require necessary connection; accidental conditionals have none.

The concept of a universal conditional (kulliyya) is not applicable to every type of conditional. Some conditionals are based on accidental coincidence rather than logical necessity. In this lesson, we examine why the universal conditional is restricted only to implicative (lizumiyya) and contradictory disjunctive (inadadiyya) types, and why accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals fail to support a universal claim.

Guided Path
1
Understand the universal conditional
Recall that a universal conditional is true when the connection holds in all possible states (awda'). This is analogous to categorical universal propositions.
2
Examine the implicative connected
In muttasila lizumiyya, the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent, so in all states where antecedent is true, consequent is true.
This is the paradigmatic case for a universal conditional.
3
Examine the contradictory disjunctive
In munfasila inadadiyya, the two parts cannot both be true or both false, so in all states, the truth of one implies the falsehood of the other.
The universal holds because the contradiction is necessary.
4
Analyze the accidental
In ittifaqiyya, there is no necessary relation; the connection is mere coincidence. Therefore, it is possible for the antecedent to be true while the consequent is false in some state, making the universal false.
The author argues that without mulāzama (implication), the universal cannot be true.
5
Conclude the restriction
The universal conditional is only appropriately applied to conditionals with a necessary connection, not accidental ones.
This is why logicians restrict kulliyya to lizumiyya and inadadiyya.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى بعض الأوضاع لايكون التالي لازماً للمقدم، فلا يصدق أن التالي لازم للمقدّم على جميع الأوضاع، وهو مفهوم الكلية على ذلك التقدير. وأما في الانفصال : فلان من الأوضاع مالا يعاند التالي المقدم معه، كصدق الطرفين، فإنّ التالي على هذا الوضع لازم للمقدّم فيكون نقيض التالي معاندا للمقدّم، فلو كان المقدّم معانداً للتالي على هذا الوضع لزم معاندة الشيء للنقيضين وإنه محال، فعلى بعض الأوضاع لا يعاند التالي المقدّم، فلا يصدق أن التالي معاند للمقدم على سائر الأوضاع. وإنما خص هذا التفسير بالمتصلة اللزومية والمنفصلة العنادية، لأن الأوضاع المعتبرة في الاتفاقية ليست هي من الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مطلقاً، بل الأوضاع الكائنة بحسب نفس الأمر، لأنه لولا ذلك لم تصدق الاتفاقية الكلية، إذ ليس بين طرفيها علاقة توجب صدق التالي على تقدير صدق المقدم، فيمكن اجتماع عدم التالي مع المقدّم، وإلا لكان بينهما ملازمة؛ والتالي ليس متحققا على تقدير صدق المقدم على هذا الوضع، فعلى بعض الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع وضع المقدّم لا يكون التالي صادقاً . على تقدير صدق المقدّم فلايكون التالي صادقاً على تقدير صدق المقدّم على جميع الأوضاع الممكنة الاجتماع مع المقدم، فلا تصدق الكلية الاتفاقية.
In some states, the consequent is not necessary for the antecedent, so it is not true that the consequent is necessary for the antecedent in all states, which is the concept of universality according to that estimation. As for the disjunctive: because among the states there is that in which the consequent does not contradict the antecedent with it, like the truth of both sides. For the consequent in this state is necessary for the antecedent, so the contradictory of the consequent is contradictory to the antecedent. So if the antecedent were contradictory to the consequent in this state, that would entail a thing contradicting two contradictories, which is impossible. Thus in some states the consequent does not contradict the antecedent, so it is not true that the consequent contradicts the antecedent in all states. This interpretation is specifically restricted to the implicative connected and the contradictory disjunctive, because the states considered in the accidental are not absolutely possible states of coexistence, but rather states existing according to actual fact. Because otherwise the universal accidental would not be true, since there is no relation between its two sides that necessitates the truth of the consequent upon the truth of the antecedent. So it is possible for the non-existence of the consequent to coexist with the antecedent, otherwise there would be implication between them. And the consequent is not realized upon the truth of the antecedent in this state. So in some states possible of coexistence with the state of the antecedent, the consequent is not true upon the truth of the antecedent. Thus the consequent is not true upon the truth of the antecedent in all states possible of coexistence with the antecedent, so the universal accidental is not true.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage explains why the interpretation of universal conditional (kulliyya) applies only to the implicative connected (muttasila lizumiyya) and the contradictory disjunctive (munfasila inadadiyya), not to the accidental (ittifaqiyya). The author begins with the connected: if the consequent is not necessary for the antecedent in some states, then the universal 'the consequent is necessary for the antecedent in all states' is false. Similarly for the disjunctive: if there is a state where the consequent does not contradict the antecedent, then the universal 'the consequent contradicts the antecedent in all states' is false. The reason given is that the accidental cannot sustain a universal claim because its truth condition depends on actual states, not all possible states. Since there is no real relation (mulazama) between antecedent and consequent, it is possible for the consequent to be false while the antecedent is true in some coexistent state, making the universal accidental false. This clarifies that universal conditional quantifies over all possible states (awda') that are compatible with the antecedent, and only when the relation is necessary does the universal hold.

Key Terms
كلية
متصلة لزومية
منفصلة عنادية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because there is no necessary relation between its parts, so the consequent may be false while antecedent is true.
C) Because it is always false.
D) Because it is not a proper conditional.

Key Takeaway
The universal conditional requires necessary connection (luzūm or ʿinād) between parts; without it, universal quantification over all possible states is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Quantifying conditionals: states not individuals
Author defines particular conditional and explains quantifiers via analogy with categoricals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of conditionals (makhsusa, mahbura, muhmala) and explain how their quantifiers range over times/states rather than individuals.

Core Idea
In conditionals, quantifiers range over times and states, not individuals.

This lesson explains a crucial shift from categorical to conditional propositions. While categoricals quantify over individuals, conditionals quantify over times and states. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly interpreting conditional statements in Islamic logical analyses, such as those used in fiqh and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall categorical quantifiers
In categorical propositions, quantifiers (all, some) apply to individuals. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' means all individuals that are intoxicants are forbidden. Remember this as a baseline.
2
Shift to conditional quantifiers
In conditionals, the quantifier does not apply to the antecedent or consequent terms but to the times or states in which the connection holds. The passage says particularity is 'of times and states' (azman wa ahwal).
This is the key conceptual leap.
3
Identify the three types of conditionals
Using the analogy with categoricals, classify a conditional as: (a) makhsusa if it refers to a specific state/time (e.g., 'If you come today'), (b) mahbura if the quantity over states is explicitly all or some, (c) muhmala if no quantity is specified.
The passage explicitly compares these to 'makhsusa', 'mahbura', and 'muhmala' in categoricals.
4
Apply to examples from the text
Analyze the given examples: 'Sometimes if this is an animal, it is a human' — the connection holds only in the state of rationality, so it is particular (partial) but not makhsusa because no single state is named. The example 'If you come today' is makhsusa because 'today' specifies a time.
Notice the difference: 'some states' vs 'a specific state'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا عرفت مفهوم الكلية فكذلك جزئية المتصلة والمنفصلة ليست بجزئية المقدم والتالي، بل بجزئية الأزمان والأحوال، حتى يكون الحكم بالاتصال والانفصال في بعض الأزمان وعلى بعض الأوضاع المذكورة، كقولنا : «قد يكون إذا كان هذا الشيء حيواناً كان إنساناً»، فإن الحكم بلزوم الإنسانية للحيوان إنما هو على وضع كونه ناطقاً، وكقولنا : «قد يكون إما أن يكون هذا الشيء نامياً أو جماداً، فإن العناد بينهما إنما يكون على وضع كونه من العنصريات. وأما خصوصية الشرطية فبتعين بعض الأزمان والأحوال، كقولنا : إن جئتني اليوم أكرمتك»، وأما إهمالها فبإهمال الأزمان والأحوال. وبالجملة الأوضاع والأزمنة في الشرطية بمنزلة الأفراد في الحملية، فكما أن الحكم فيها إن كان على فرد معين فهي مخصوصة، وإن لم يكن فإن بين كمّية الحكم بأنه على كلّ الأفراد أو على بعضها فهي المحصورة، وإلا فهي المهملة؛ كذلك الشرطية إن كان الحكم بالاتصال أو الانفصال فيها على وضع معين فهي المخصوصة، وإلا فإن بين كمية الحكم بأنه على جميع الأوضاع أو بعضها فهي محصورة وإلا فمهملة.
And when you have understood the concept of universality, likewise the particularity of the connected and disjunctive is not particularity of the antecedent and consequent, but rather particularity of times and states, so that the judgment of connection and disjunction is in some times and upon some of the mentioned states, like our saying: 'Sometimes if this thing is an animal, it is a human', for the judgment of the implication of humanity for animal is only upon the state of it being rational. And like our saying: 'Sometimes it is either that this thing is growing or inanimate', for the contradiction between them is only upon the state of it being elemental. As for the particularity of the conditional (makhsusa), it is by specifying some times and states, like our saying: 'If you come to me today, I will honor you'. As for its being unspecified (muhmala), it is by leaving the times and states unspecified. In summary, the states and times in the conditional are in the position of individuals in the categorical. Just as in the categorical, if the judgment is upon a specific individual, it is particular; if not, then if the quantity of the judgment is specified as all or some individuals, it is quantified; otherwise it is unspecified. Similarly in the conditional: if the judgment of connection or disjunction is upon a specific state, it is particular; if not, then if the quantity of the judgment is specified as all states or some, it is quantified; otherwise it is unspecified.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage clarifies that particularity (juz'iyya) in conditional propositions is not about the antecedent or consequent themselves being particular, but about the time (zaman) or state (wad') in which the connection holds. The author gives examples: 'Sometimes if this thing is an animal, it is a human'—the implication of humanity holds only under the state of rationality. Similarly, 'Sometimes it is either growing or inanimate'—the contradiction holds only under the state of being elemental. Then the author distinguishes between a particular conditional (makhsusa), where a specific time/state is mentioned (e.g., 'If you come today'), and a quantified conditional (mahbura), where the quantity (all or some states) is specified, versus an unspecified conditional (muhmala) where no quantity is indicated. This is an analogy to categorical propositions where individuals are replaced by states/times. The key insight: in conditionals, the quantifier ranges over times and states (awda' wa azmina), not over the terms themselves.

Key Terms
جزئية (juz'iyya)
مخصوصة (makhsusa)
محصورة (mahbura)
مهملة (muhmala)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The antecedent is a particular term.
B) The consequent is a particular term.
C) The connection holds in some states or times.
D) The proposition is about a specific individual.

Key Takeaway
The quantifier in a conditional proposition always ranges over times and states, not over the antecedent or consequent terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

(c) 'If the sun rises, it is daytime.' Explain your reasoning based on the presence or absence of a specific time/state or quantifier.

Quantifiers for Universal Affirmative Connected Conditionals
Enumerating quantifiers for conditionals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correctly apply the three Arabic quantifiers (kullama, mahma, mata) for universal affirmative connected conditionals and distinguish them from categorical quantifiers.

Core Idea
Three words quantify universal conditionals just as 'kull' does for categoricals.

Conditional propositions, like categoricals, need quantifiers to indicate universality. For connected conditionals (mutasila) that are universal affirmative, Arabic uses three specific words: 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'. Mastering these is essential for expressing general conditional relationships in Islamic logic, such as those found in usul al-fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the quantifier pattern
Just as categorical universals use 'kull' (every), conditional universals use 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata'. These words appear at the beginning of the antecedent and bind the entire conditional statement.
2
Memorize the three words
Commit to memory: 'kullama' (whenever), 'mahma' (whatever/whenever), 'mata' (when). Each signals that the conditional holds for all times or states.
Some texts use 'mahma' for categoricals too; in conditionals it always indicates universality.
3
Connect to an example from Islamic sciences
Think of a fiqhi conditional: 'Kullama wujida al-sababu wujida al-musabbabu' (Whenever the cause exists, the effect exists). This uses 'kullama' as the universal quantifier.
4
Distinguish from categorical quantifiers
Do not confuse with 'kull' which quantifies subjects in categorical propositions. 'Kullama', 'mahma', 'mata' are exclusively for conditionals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السور في الشرطيات] وسور الموجبة الكلّية في المتصلة «كلّما» و «مهما» و «متی» كقولنا :
[The quantifiers in conditionals] And the quantifiers of the universal affirmative in the connected are 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata', as in our saying:
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This brief passage introduces the quantifiers (sur) specifically used for conditional propositions. The author states that for universal affirmative connected conditionals (muttasila), the Arabic quantifiers are 'kullama' (whenever), 'mahma' (whenever/whatever), and 'mata' (when). These words indicate that the connection holds in every time/state. The text is cut off but would have provided an example. This is analogous to 'kull' (every) for categoricals. The student should memorize these quantifiers as essential for expressing universal conditionals.

Key Terms
كلّما (kullama): Quantifier for universal affirmative connected conditionals, meaning 'whenever'.
مهما (mahma): Quantifier for universal affirmative connected conditionals, meaning 'whenever' or 'whatever'. [Note: Some texts use 'mahma' for both conditionals and categoricals.]
متى (mata): Quantifier for universal affirmative connected conditionals, meaning 'when'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The quantifiers 'kullama', 'mahma', and 'mata' are used to express universal affirmative connected conditionals in Arabic logic.

Quantifier Words for Conditional Propositions
Author lists quantifier words for each type of quantified conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct quantifier word for each type of quantified conditional proposition (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) for both connected and disjoined conditionals.

Core Idea
Conditional quantifiers are the key to determining the logical force of conditional statements.

In this passage, the author lists the specific quantifier words used for each type of quantified conditional proposition. These quantifiers function like the 'all', 'some', 'no' for categorical propositions, but tailored for conditionals. Mastering these quantifiers is essential for logical analysis in Islamic sciences where conditional arguments are used, such as in kalam proofs and fiqh reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Recognize that conditionals are either connected (muttasila) or disjoined (munfasila). The quantifier words differ slightly between them.
2
Find the universal affirmative quantifiers
For connected conditionals, the universal affirmative quantifiers are 'kullamā', 'mahmā', and 'matā' (all meaning 'whenever'). For disjoined, it is 'dā'iman' ('always'). Note the example given: 'Kullamā the sun is rising, then the day exists' and 'Dā'iman either the sun is rising or the day does not exist'.
3
Find the universal negative quantifier
The universal negative quantifier for both types is 'laysa al-batta' ('not at all'). The example shows its use in connected and disjoined conditionals.
4
Find the particular quantifiers
The particular affirmative quantifier is 'qad yakūnu' ('it may be') for both types. The particular negative is 'qad lā yakūnu' ('it may not be'). Examples illustrate each.
5
Understand negation of universal quantifiers
Adding 'laysa' before a universal affirmative quantifier (e.g., 'laysa kullamā') negates the universal, yielding a particular negative. This follows the rule that negating a universal gives a particular negative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسور الموجبة الكلية في المتصلة «كلّما» و «مهما» و «متی» كقولنا : كلّما - أو مهما أو منى - كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود». وفي المنفصلة دائماً» كقولنا : دائماً إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة أو لا يكون النهار موجوداً». وسور السالبة الكلية فيهما «ليس البنّة»، أما في المتصلة فكقولنا : «ليس ألبتة إذا كان الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود»، وأما في المنفصلة فكقولنا : «ليس البنّة إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً». وسور الموجبة الجزئية فيهما قد يكون » كقولنا : «قد يكون إذا كان الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجوداً» و «قد يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة أو يكون الليل موجوداً». وسور السالبة الجزئية فيهما «قد لا يكون » كقولنا : «قد لا يكون إذا كان الشمس طالعة كان الليل موجوداً» و «قد لا يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً». وبإدخال حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي كـ«ليس كلّما» و ليس مهما» و «ليس متى» في المتصلة، و «ليس دائماً» في المنفصلة، لأنا إذا قلنا : «كلما كان كذا كان كذا كان مفهومه الإيجاب الكلي، فإذا قلنا : «ليس كلّما يكون معناه رفع الإيجاب الكلي لامحالة، وإذا ارتفع الإيجاب الكلّي تحقق السلب الجزئي على ما حققته فيما سبق، وهكذا في البواقي
The quantifier of the universal affirmative in the connected is 'kullamā', 'mahmā', and 'matā', as in our saying: 'Kullamā' — or 'mahmā' or 'matā' — the sun is rising, then the day exists. And in the disjoined, it is 'dā'iman', as in our saying: 'Dā'iman either the sun is rising or the day does not exist.' The quantifier of the universal negative in both is 'laysa al-batta'. As for the connected, it is like our saying: 'Laysa al-batta if the sun is rising then the night exists.' And as for the disjoined, it is like our saying: 'Laysa al-batta either the sun is rising or the day exists.' The quantifier of the particular affirmative in both is 'qad yakūnu', as in our saying: 'Qad yakūnu if the sun is rising then the day exists' and 'Qad yakūnu either the sun is rising or the night exists.' The quantifier of the particular negative in both is 'qad lā yakūnu', as in our saying: 'Qad lā yakūnu if the sun is rising then the night exists' and 'Qad lā yakūnu either the sun is rising or the day exists.' And by inserting the negation particle on the universal affirmative quantifiers, like 'laysa kullamā' and 'laysa mahmā' and 'laysa matā' in the connected, and 'laysa dā'iman' in the disjoined. For when we say: 'Kullamā such and such, then such and such', its concept is universal affirmative; then when we say: 'Laysa kullamā', its meaning is the negation of the universal affirmative inevitably. And when the universal affirmative is negated, the particular negative is realized, as I have verified previously. And similarly for the rest.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage enumerates the quantifier words (sūr) used for each type of quantified conditional proposition. The author systematically lists them: for universal affirmative in the connected (muttasila), the quantifiers are 'kullamā', 'mahmā', 'matā' (all meaning 'whenever'), with the example 'Whenever the sun is rising, the day exists'. For the disjoined (munfasila), the universal affirmative quantifier is 'dā'iman' ('always'), as in 'Always either the sun is rising or the day does not exist' (i.e., they cannot both be false). The universal negative quantifier in both types is 'laysa al-batta' ('not at all'), with examples: for connected, 'Not at all is it the case that if the sun is rising, the night exists'; for disjoined, 'Not at all is it the case that either the sun is rising or the day exists' (meaning they are not together true). The particular affirmative quantifier is 'qad yakūnu' ('it may be'), and the particular negative is 'qad lā yakūnu' ('it may not be'), each with appropriate examples. Finally, the author explains that by prefixing the negation particle 'laysa' to the universal affirmative quantifiers (e.g., 'laysa kullamā'), we obtain the particular negative, because negating a universal yields a particular negative (a standard conversion rule). He notes this was already established. This section provides the precise vocabulary for quantifying conditionals, essential for logical analysis.

Key Terms
سور
كلّما
دائماً
ليس البتة
قد يكون
قد لا يكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) ليس كلّما
B) ليس دائماً
C) ليس البتة
D) قد لا يكون

Key Takeaway
The quantifier system for conditionals uses distinct words: كلّما for universal affirmative connected, دائماً for universal affirmative disjoined, ليس البتة for universal negative in both, قد يكون for particular affirmative, and قد لا يكون for particular negative.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Propositions

Quantifiers for Conditional Propositions
Author enumerates quantifiers for conditional propositions and mentions indefinite particles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the quantifier (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative, indefinite) for any conjunctive or disjunctive conditional proposition used in Islamic logic texts.

Core Idea
Conditional quantifiers mirror categorical ones but use different words.

In Islamic logic, every proposition must be quantified to specify its logical force. For conditionals, special quantifier words are used: for conjunctive conditionals (if-then), words like كلّما (whenever) indicate universal affirmative; for disjunctive (either-or), دائماً (always) does the same. This lesson teaches you the entire range of quantifiers for both types.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of conditional
Determine if it is conjunctive (متصل) or disjunctive (منفصل) from the connective words. Conjunctives use إذا, إن, لو; disjunctives use إما, أو.
2
Find the quantifier particle
Look for words like كلّما, دائماً, ليس البتة, قد يكون, قد لا يكون. If none is present and only a connective appears, the conditional is indefinite.
3
Determine the quantity and quality
Map the quantifier: كلّما/مهما/متى = universal affirmative; ليس البتة = universal negative; قد يكون = particular affirmative; قد لا يكون = particular negative.
4
Identify indefinite conditionals
If the quantifier is absent and replaced by لو, إن, إذا for conjunctive or أما, أو for disjunctive, the conditional is indefinite (مهمل).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلّما - أو مهما أو منى - كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود. وفي المنفصلة دائماً كقولنا: دائماً إما أن تكون الشمس طالعة أو لا يكون النهار موجوداً. وسور السالبة الكلية فيهما ليس البتة، أما في المتصلة فكقولنا: ليس ألبتة إذا كان الشمس طالعة فالليل موجود، وأما في المنفصلة فكقولنا: ليس البتة إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً. وسور الموجبة الجزئية فيهما قد يكون كقولنا: قد يكون إذا كان الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجوداً و قد يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة أو يكون الليل موجوداً. وسور السالبة الجزئية فيهما قد لا يكون كقولنا: قد لا يكون إذا كان الشمس طالعة كان الليل موجوداً و قد لا يكون إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجوداً. وبإدخال حرف السلب على سور الإيجاب الكلي كـ ليس كلّما و ليس مهما و ليس متى في المتصلة، و ليس دائماً في المنفصلة، لأنا إذا قلنا: كلما كان كذا كان كذا كان مفهومه الإيجاب الكلي، فإذا قلنا: ليس كلّما يكون معناه رفع الإيجاب الكلي لامحالة، وإذا ارتفع الإيجاب الكلي تحقق السلب الجزئي على ما حققته فيما سبق، وهكذا في البواقي. وإطلاق لفظة لو و إن و إذا في الاتصال و أما و أو في الانفصال للإهمال، كقولنا: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، و إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن لا يكون النهار موجودا.
Whenever - or whenever, whenever - the sun is rising, then day exists. And in the disjunctive: always, e.g., always either the sun is rising or day does not exist. And the quantifier of universal negative in both is 'not at all'. As for the conjunctive, e.g., 'not at all if the sun is rising then night exists'. As for the disjunctive, e.g., 'not at all either the sun is rising or day exists'. And the quantifier of particular affirmative in both is 'sometimes', e.g., 'sometimes if the sun is rising then day exists' and 'sometimes either the sun is rising or night exists'. And the quantifier of particular negative in both is 'sometimes not', e.g., 'sometimes not if the sun is rising then night exists' and 'sometimes not either the sun is rising or day exists'. And by inserting the particle of negation onto the quantifier of universal affirmative, like 'not whenever' and 'not whenever' and 'not whenever' in the conjunctive, and 'not always' in the disjunctive, because if we say 'whenever this is that' its concept is universal affirmative; when we say 'not whenever' its meaning is the lifting of the universal affirmative inevitably; and when the universal affirmative is lifted, the particular negative obtains, as I established previously, and likewise in the rest. And the use of the words 'if', 'if', 'when' in the conjunctive, and 'either', 'or' in the disjunctive, for indefiniteness, e.g., 'if the sun is rising then day exists', and 'either the sun is rising or day does not exist'.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the quantifiers (سور) applicable to conditional propositions (قضايا شرطية), both conjunctive (متصل) and disjunctive (منفصل). The author first lists the universal affirmative quantifier: for conjunctive, it is 'كلّما' or 'مهما' or 'متى' (all meaning 'whenever'), as in 'Whenever the sun is rising, day exists'. For disjunctive, it is 'دائماً' (always), as in 'Always either the sun is rising or day does not exist'. Then the universal negative quantifier: 'ليس البتة' (not at all) for both, with examples. Then particular affirmative: 'قد يكون' (sometimes) for both. Then particular negative: 'قد لا يكون' (sometimes not). Next, the author explains how to derive the particular negative by negating the universal affirmative quantifier: by adding the negation particle 'ليس' to the universal affirmative quantifier, e.g., 'ليس كلّما' (not whenever), which lifts the universal affirmative and thereby confirms the particular negative. This mirrors the categorical proposition quantifiers. Finally, the author mentions the indefinite particles (أدوات الإهمال): for conjunctive, 'لو' (if), 'إن' (if), 'إذا' (when); for disjunctive, 'أما' (either), 'أو' (or). These are used when the quantifier is omitted and the proposition is indefinite. For example, 'إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود' (if the sun is rising, then day exists). This lesson establishes the complete vocabulary for quantifying conditional statements, essential for logical analysis.

متصلة
منفصلة
إهمال

Key Takeaway
Each conditional proposition in Islamic logic is either quantified by a specific particle indicating its scope (universal/particular, affirmative/negative) or left indefinite, and this quantifier is essential for logical inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In a kalam argument, you read: 'ليس البتة إذا كانت الحوادث محدثة فإنها لا تحتاج إلى محدث.' Identify the quantifier type and rewrite the proposition as a universal negative conditional. Which conditional type is it?
?
2. In a fiqh text: 'قد لا يكون إذا كان المبيع معيباً فإن البيع فاسد.' Identify the quantifier and determine if it is a particular negative conjunctive conditional.

The Six Basic Compositions of Conditionals
Lists the six compositional types of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six compositional types of conditional propositions based on the kinds of their component propositions.

Core Idea
A conditional is made of two propositions; exactly six compositional types cover all possibilities.

This passage introduces the building blocks of conditional propositions. The author lists six ways a conditional can be composed: from two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical with a conjunctive, a categorical with a disjunctive, or a conjunctive with a disjunctive. Mastering these types is essential for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, especially in usul al-fiqh where conditionals appear in legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two component propositions
Every conditional has two parts: the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali). Each part is itself a proposition of one of three types: categorical (hamliyya), conjunctive (muttasila), or disjunctive (munfasila).
Categorical propositions are simple subject-predicate statements; conjunctives and disjunctives are themselves conditionals.
2
Enumerate all pairwise combinations
Consider all possible ordered pairs of the three proposition types: (H,H), (H,M), (H,F), (M,M), (M,F), (F,F) where H=categorical, M=conjunctive, F=disjunctive. These give exactly six combinations.
The order matters because antecedent and consequent are distinct parts.
3
Verify that no other combination exists
Since a proposition can only be of one of the three kinds, and the conditional is made of two, all possibilities are covered by the six ordered pairs.
The author confirms: 'no more than these divisions'.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Take the conditional: 'If the prayer is valid, then the ablution is valid.' This is a conjunctive conditional composed of two categoricals (each a hamliyya). It belongs to the first type.
Try to find examples for each of the six types from fiqh or kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: والشرطية قد تتركب عن حمليتين وعن متصلتين وعن منفصلتين وعن حملية ومتصلة وعن حملية ومنفصلة وعن متصلة ومنفصلة، وكل واحدة من هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة في المتصلة تنقسم إلى قسمين لامتياز مقدمها عن تاليها بالطبع بخلاف المنفصلة، فإنّ مقدّمها إنما يتميز عن تاليها بالوضع فقط، فأقسام المتصلات تسعة والمنفصلات ستة. وأما الأمثلة فعليك باستخراجها من نفسك. أقول: لما كانت الشرطيّة مركبة من قضيتين والقضية إما حملية أو متصلة أو منفصلة، كان تركيبها إما من حمليتين أو متصلتين أو منفصلتين أو من حملية ومتصلة أو حمليّة ومنفصلة أو منفصلة ومتصلة لاتزيد على هذه الأقسام، لكن كلّ واحد من الأقسام الثلاثة الأخيرة ينقسم في المتصلة إلى قسمين، لأن مقدّم المتصلة متميّز عن تاليها بحسب الطبع : (۱) قد عرفت أن الحملية إنما تتركب من المفردات أو ما هو في حكم المفردات، وأن الشرطية تتركب من قضيتين، فأدنى ما يتصوّر من تركيب الشرطية تركيبها من حمليتين، وإذا تركبت من غير الحمليّات، فلابد أن تنحل بالأخرة إلى الحمليات المنحلة إلى المفردات، إذ لو لم تنحل أجزاء الشرطية أو جزء جزئها إلى الحمليات لزم تركيبها من أجزاء غير متناهية، فالحمليّة إما جزء الشرطية أو جزء جزئها، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهي (شريف).
He said: The conditional may be composed of two categoricals, or of two conjunctives, or of two disjunctives, or of a categorical and a conjunctive, or of a categorical and a disjunctive, or of a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And each one of these last three, in the conjunctive, divides into two parts due to the antecedent's distinction from the consequent by nature, unlike the disjunctive, for its antecedent is distinguished from its consequent only by position. So the divisions of conjunctives are nine, and of disjunctives six. As for examples, you must extract them yourself. I say: Since the conditional is composed of two propositions, and a proposition is either categorical or conjunctive or disjunctive, its composition is either from two categoricals, or two conjunctives, or two disjunctives, or from a categorical and a conjunctive, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a disjunctive and a conjunctive; no more than these divisions. But each of the last three divisions, in the conjunctive, divides into two, because the antecedent of the conjunctive is distinguished from its consequent by nature. (1) You have known that the categorical is composed of simples or what is in the ruling of simples, and that the conditional is composed of two propositions. So the least conceivable composition of the conditional is its composition from two categoricals. And if it is composed from non-categoricals, then it must eventually be resolved into categoricals which resolve into simples, because if the parts of the conditional or the part of its part did not resolve into categoricals, then its composition from infinite parts would follow. So the categorical is either a part of the conditional or a part of its part, and so on until it ends.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage defines the possible compositions of a conditional proposition. The matn (قال) first lists six basic types of composition: from two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical and a conjunctive, a categorical and a disjunctive, and a conjunctive and a disjunctive. Then it explains that for conjunctive conditionals, the last three types (mixed with a categorical or a disjunctive) each split into two, because in a conjunctive, the antecedent and consequent are naturally distinguished (e.g., one may be the cause of the other), so the order matters. In contrast, in a disjunctive, the antecedent and consequent are only distinguished by position (no natural precedence), so no further split occurs. Thus total types: for conjunctive conditionals: the three pure types (two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives) count as one each, and the three mixed types each split into two, giving 3 + (3×2) = 9. For disjunctive conditionals: the three pure types count as one each, and the three mixed types do not split, giving 3 + 3 = 6. The sharh (أقول) expands by explaining that the minimal composition is from two categoricals, and any non-categorical composition must eventually reduce to categoricals, otherwise infinite regress would occur (since categoricals are composed of simple terms). This emphasizes that all conditionals ultimately rest on categorical foundations. The commentator (al-Sharif al-Jurjani) notes that the student should derive examples themselves.

Key Terms
حمليّة
متصلة
منفصلة
مقدّم
تالي

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are built from two propositions of three possible types, yielding exactly six compositional forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following conditionals into one of the six compositional types: (a) 'If the sky is clear, then it is not raining.' (b) 'Either the prayer is void or the ablution is broken.' (c) 'If it is true that if the sun rises then it is day, then the night has ended.' (d) 'If the water is pure, then either it is not used for purification or it is permissible.'
